HEADLINE: Republicans Resisting Census Plan to Correct Undercounting

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 26

 BODY:
While thousands of people in California and North Dakota grow anxious over the delay in further Federal aid to help them recover from devastating floods, the bill to provide it is hung up, in part, by the continuing political dispute over how the Census Bureau will count the population in 2000.
At issue is the bureau's proposal to make up for expected undercounting of some groups with a well-established scientific method called statistical sampling, which is routinely used for the monthly calculation of the unemployment rate and other Government statistics. Besides making sampling an integral part of the census for the first time, officials want to use the technique to conduct a "quality check" afterward, adjusting the count based on those results. 
 But many Republicans think sampling will benefit Democrats, and they say that using this method would allow the Clinton Administration to rig the count for partisan advantage.
Census officials say sampling is needed to avoid a repeat of the 1990 Census, in which, studies found, an estimated 10 million people went uncounted and another 6 million were counted twice. Bureau officials argue that getting an accurate count using traditional methods would be prohibitively expensive, if it was possible at all.
"Our experience in '90 says, as do all of the experts that have been invited by us to look at the issue, that if we continue to rely just on trying to physically count people we will at best -- at best -- only equal the 1990 results," said Martha Farnsworth Riche, the director of the Census Bureau.
The political stakes are high since the count determines the allocation of Congressional seats and the lines for Congressional and state legislative districts. Prominent among historically undercounted groups are recent immigrants, inner city blacks and rural residents. Many Republicans complain that these groups tend to vote Democratic.
Earlier this month, Senate Republicans backed away from an amendment to the $8.4 billion disaster relief bill that would have barred the Census Bureau from spending any money in the current fiscal year on preparations for using statistical sampling in the 2000 census. Recently, however, Republican leaders in the House and Senate have sought to reinsert the amendment into the disaster relief measure. President Clinton has threatened to veto the bill if the prohibition survives.
In 1991, Speaker Newt Gingrich supported the use of sampling to correct the error in the 1990 census. Recently, he and other Republican Congressional leaders wrote a letter to Ms. Riche contending that the Census Bureau must try to count all residents the traditional way and that the country "cannot rely on statistical schemes that compromise accuracy for the sake of economy."
What some strategists advising the Republican National Committee fear is that statistical sampling may swell the number of minorities in black urban districts that are now safe Democratic seats, making them, in essence, overpopulated. As a result, state legislatures might redraw lines for Congressional districts that shift the "excess" population -- loyal Democrats -- into suburban swing districts where they could tip the balance away from Republicans.
But some dispassionate observers say that much of the debate is shrouded in misperceptions. They see a repeat of the "motor voter" law that Congress enacted in 1993 to make it easier for people to register to vote. At the time many Republican critics feared that it would lead to more Democrats on the voting rolls. But since the law went into effect three years ago, both parties have benefited equally.
"A lot of stuff has taken on a life of its own," Tim Storey, who follows reapportionment and redistricting issues for the National Council of State Legislatures, said of the debate over sampling. "People take it as gospel that Republicans lose through sampling. But it's never been proven."
About 35 percent of those missed in the 1990 census were black, and a large number of the whites who went uncounted were Hispanic -- two groups that tend to be loyal Democrats. But, with the exception of New York and Maryland, the states where the most people were missed were all in the Sun Belt, a region of the country where Republicans are ascendant.
Indeed, a crude projection done recently by the Congressional Research Service indicated that if sampling was used to estimate the uncounted, Mississippi would retain a Congressional seat it would otherwise lose. Yet among the Congressional leaders fighting against the use of sampling for the 2000 Census is Senator Trent Lott, Republican of Mississippi.
"It's a two-edged sword that cuts both ways," said a Republican Senate staffer involved in the issue. "There is a parochial standpoint that says if I'm from Georgia or New Mexico it's great to have a more accurate count because my state will get more representation. But do we want more representation if it results in a Democratic seat?"
Given the potential political impact and the fact that statistical sampling is little understood by the average voter, some Republicans say Democrats, who control the executive branch, and thus, the Census Bureau, could rig the reapportionment.
"By and large, the notion of statistical sampling scares the living daylights out of me," said Benjamin Ginsberg, a former general counsel of the Republican National Committee who is an expert on political redistricting. "It is so subject to political manipulation."
Such sentiments have offended the civil servant statisticians designing the next census. They say they just want to make sure that the 2000 census is the most accurate it can be. And besides, they argue, their plans call for a panel of independent experts to spot any violation of professional practice.
Critics of the use of sampling also cite legal concerns. The Constitution states that an actual enumeration be conducted of the country's population every 10 years to determine representation in the House. Critics say that the instruction precludes the use of sampling, and that the Census Bureau could find its results invalidated by the Supreme Court.
In their recent letter to Ms. Riche, the Republican leaders, who have traditionally been more frugal about Government spending, said that the legal questions raised by sampling were so great that "the census must be funded at levels necessary to comply explicitly with the Constitution."
Census Bureau officials respond by saying that it has always been next to impossible to get an exact count anyway and that changed demographics makes the use of sampling ever more compelling.

HEADLINE: Imaging Device Makes Chip-Sized Cameras a Real-World Possibility

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, May 26

 BODY:
The digital still camera that Toshiba will start selling this summer does not look much different from dozens of cameras that have flooded the market lately. But on the inside, the camera holds what could be the seed of a revolution.
The camera is the first by a major Japanese electronics company to use a new type of image-capturing device that might one day make possible the exquisitely tiny camera-on-a-chip. 
 Such cheap micro-cameras could be plastered everywhere as security monitors or placed in the rear bumpers of cars to help drivers back up. They might even make possible the camera in the glasses frame used by Tom Cruise in "Mission: Impossible." And instead of highlighting a passage in a book with a colored marker, a student of the future might take a snapshot of the passage with a camera built into a pen.
Until now, camcorders and digital still cameras, which store images electronically rather than on film, have captured images using a chip called a charge-coupled device, or C.C.D.
But these devices have some drawbacks. They use a lot of power, requiring bulky batteries, and are made with a process different from that used to make other microelectronic circuits. That makes it difficult to combine a C.C.D. with other circuits on the same piece of silicon.
The LSI Logic Corporation, for instance, will introduce a chip that has almost all the circuitry needed for a digital still camera -- but not the C.C.D., which must still remain separate.
The Toshiba Corporation's imaging chip, however, is made using a process known as CMOS (pronounced SEA-moss), the same process used for making many other integrated circuits.
That process could result in economies of scale by allowing the imaging chips to be made on big production lines used for other chips. It should make it possible to combine on a single piece of silicon the image sensor, timing controls, image compression and memory needed for a camera -- producing the camera-on-a-chip. CMOS imaging chips also use far less power than C.C.D.'s.
One big application could be video cameras small and cheap enough to be built into every personal computer, allowing PC users to hold video conferences. Creative Laboratories Inc., the Singapore company known for its Sound Blaster audio boards, is introducing a PC video conferencing system using a CMOS imaging chip from VLSI Vision Inc. in Edinburgh.
Sensors using CMOS, which stands for complementary metal-oxide-semiconductor, could also tilt the balance of power in the industry, allowing Americans and Europeans a chance to catch up with the Japanese, who now dominate the market both for C.C.D.'s and the camcorders and digital cameras that use them.
"CMOS imaging allows other companies to get into the imaging business," said Bryan Ackland, a researcher for Lucent Technologies, the equipment manufacturer spun off from the AT&T Corporation. Lucent has been researching CMOS imaging chips but has not yet decided to make a product, he said.
The Sony Corporation and the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company are the leading suppliers of the charge-coupled devices, about 20 million of which were produced for use in cameras and camcorders last year. Including simpler devices used in fax machines and other office equipment, the total market was estimated at about $650 million.
But the CMOS sensors have been pioneered by two start-ups, VLSI in Scotland and Photobit L.L.C. in La Crescenta, Calif., a spinoff from the National Aeronautics and Space Administration's Jet Propulsion Laboratory. Other start-ups include Omnivision Technologies in San Jose, Calif., and Suni Imaging Microsystems in Mountain View, Calif.
Numerous big American companies, including Eastman Kodak, Polaroid, Hewlett-Packard, I.B.M., Intel and National Semiconductor, are working on CMOS imaging chips, said Eric R. Fossum, chief scientist at Photobit, who introduced the technology to many of those companies when he was at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory. By contrast, he said, when he approached Japanese companies a few years ago, "the reaction was fairly uniformly uninterested."
That might no longer be the case. Toshiba has become a leader in the new imaging technology. "We've suddenly got a shot of credibility from Toshiba," Peter B. Denyer, chief executive of VLSI, said.
Matsushita is also working on CMOS imagers that would be combined on a chip with image-compression circuitry, Akira Matsuzawa, a researcher on the project, said.
Both types of imaging chips have an array of cells representing the individual picture elements, or pixels. When light falls on the surface of the silicon cells, it is converted into an electric charge in proportion to the brightness of the light.
The problem delivering the signals to a device that reads them. Connecting a wire to each pixel would cover the chip, blocking out the light. And the charges are so weak that they would be obliterated if put on a common wire, called a bus.
The C.C.D., invented in the late 1960's at Bell Laboratories, solved this problem by transferring the charges from pixel to pixel in an elaborate bucket brigade. But this requires three different voltages and a lot of time and power.
But with semiconductor features constantly shrinking, it has become possible to put an amplifier inside each picture cell to increase the charge, allowing it to be transferred off the chip on a common wire and making the CMOS approach practical.
Still, some attempts to develop such sensors have floundered because the images have been poorer in quality and resolution than those captured by C.C.D.'s. A particularly big problem has been noise, which produces unwanted lines or dots on the image.
So far, CMOS imagers have been used in only a few applications where low cost has been more important than image quality. VLSI Vision's sensor is used in the Kidcam, a black-and-white camcorder for children that is sold by Tyco Toys.
But now the quality is improving and the chips are starting to advance into mainstream products.
The Toshiba camera uses a CMOS imaging chip with 330,000 pixels, standard for consumer-level cameras. The camera, which is expected to sell for about $500 in the United States, can be plugged directly into a card slot in a personal computer to transfer the images for display on a monitor.
The Vivitar Corporation, a camera equipment maker based in Newbury Park, Calif., is introducing a digital still camera using a CMOS imaging chip from VLSI Vision. It will capture 800,000 pixels, the first such camera with that resolution to have a price of less than $500, said Alexis Gerard, publisher of Future Image Report, a Burlingame, Calif., newsletter on digital cameras.
At a technical conference in February, Toshiba announced that it had developed a CMOS imaging chip containing 1.3 million pixels, the highest resolution now supported by monitors.
Not everyone is so gung-ho on CMOS imagers. With C.C.D.'s, "the picture quality is much better than with CMOS image sensors," said Nobukazu Teranishi, a senior manager in the sensor research laboratory of the NEC Corporation.
Sony, which has produced 50 million C.C.D.'s to date and now churns out nearly a million a month, is increasing its manufacturing capacity to meet increased demand.
A problem for producers of CMOS imagers is the head start that C.C.D. makers have. "The C.C.D. is very mature technology and they have a very strong marketing capability," said Junichi Nakamura, a researcher at the Olympus Optical Company, which has a small research project on CMOS imagers.
But Vern Klein, who is in charge of selling LSI Logic's new camera chip to digital camera makers, disagreed. "All our customers believe that CMOS sensors will be the way forward," he said.

HEADLINE: Cooling Down the Heated Talk;
Still Defiant, but Subtler, Industry Awaits E.P.A. Rules

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH 

 BODY:
Corporate America is not a quick study. Again and again, companies have responded to proposed environmental rules by threatening bankruptcies, huge layoffs, foreign inroads into American markets, even an end to the car-based American way of life -- and it has never worked.
Finally, though, companies are acknowledging that the sky did not fall every time they were forced to clean up their act and their air. Now, as the Environmental Protection Agency prepares to impose yet another set of strict new air pollution controls this July, they are moving their doom-and-gloom approach, if not off the stove, to the furthest back burner. 
 "If we've learned any lesson, it's that you have to engage the debate on a different basis than costs," said John M. McManus, manager of environmental strategies for American Electric Power, a huge utility.
But without the unifying plea of untenable burdens, industry approaches the E.P.A.'s latest rules, aimed at reducing smog and soot, in disarray. Many industries are trying to cut their own deals with the agency. Others are putting pressure on mayors and governors to lobby Washington for them. Still others are attacking the science -- and sometimes the scientists -- on which the new rules are based.
The camps have been forming since November, when the agency began holding hearings on its proposed rules. The E.P.A. said existing levels in the air of ozone and "fine particulates" -- dust specks 28 times as thin as a human hair -- were causing a high incidence of childhood asthma, and possibly tens of thousands of adult deaths a year.
Industry says the E.P.A. has not conclusively proved this, and thus should not set stricter rules. Environmentalists and regulators counter that the agency, which Congress set up for the purpose of keeping the environment healthy and safe, must issue regulations unless they are proved unnecessary, not the other way around.
"Companies are attacking the scientific legitimacy of standards only because their cost arguments have been disproven so many times," said Daniel Rosenberg, a lawyer for the U.S. Public Interest Research Group, an environmental lobbying group. Added David G. Hawkins, a lawyer for the Natural Resources Defense Council: "This is not a scientific debate. It is an attempt to change policy and weaken the law."
Historically, the courts have sided with the environmentalists. A decade ago, the steel industry lost a suit, which went all the way to the Supreme Court, seeking to prohibit the E.P.A. from regulating pollutants unless it was certain they were a health risk. "The Supreme Court basically said, the more uncertainty there is, the more discretion the E.P.A. should have," conceded Andrew G. Sharkey, president of the American Iron and Steel Institute.
Still, industry sees no choice but to take this tack again. If the E.P.A. issues tougher rules in July, localities will probably force companies to filter out more dust, and to control emissions of nitrous oxides, benzene and other chemicals that are the precursors of ozone. That could cost companies millions of dollars.
Still, after so many years of crying wolf, companies are loath to make the cost argument. Even American Electric Power, once the undisputed ringleader of the anti-Clean Air Act gang, has muted its cries. Some quick history can show why.
In the early 1970's, the utility mounted a $3.7 million advertising campaign to contend that technology did not exist to meet proposed rules on sulfur dioxide and particulates. In 1975 an equipment maker, Research Cottrell, began marketing a sulfur dioxide smokestack scrubber that proved American Electric wrong.
So then the utility complained that it would cost $260 million a power plant to install enough scrubbers, leading to skyrocketing electric costs. The E.P.A. did not budge, the cleanup tab turned out to be much lower, and electric rates went up only marginally.
Skip ahead to the late 1980's, when Congress and the E.P.A., worrying about acid rain -- caused by sulfur-based pollution that can travel wide distances -- insisted on even stricter sulfur controls. American Electric said the new standards would cost utilities more than $5 billion, raising residential electricity rates 12 percent and industrial rates as much as 39 percent.
Again, Washington gave no ground. But scrubber technology improved and railroad deregulation made it cheaper to bring in low-sulfur coal. The utility industry's costs were less than half its projections.
Small wonder that American Electric and its sister utilities are loath to cry economic foul this time. Instead, they are asking that the existing 10-year ozone and large-particulate- abatement program, which the E.P.A. mandated in 1990, be allowed to run its course.
"Let us finish this first and then discuss what else must be done," said Robert A. Beck, vice president for environmental affairs at the Edison Electric Institute, the trade group for utilities.
All the new approaches -- the begging for time, the emphasis on science, the absence of inflated cost claims -- reflect industry's new relationship with investors, customers and the courts.
In the 70's, while the E.P.A. did not believe industry's threats of financial disaster, investors did -- and sent Rust Belt stocks plummeting. Now utilities, heavy manufacturers and steelmakers fear that inflated cost forecasts could touch off another selling spree.
"I always ask companies, 'Are you budgeted to meet new rules?' and they always answer, 'Oh, certainly,' " said Kenneth W. P. Hoffman, a steel analyst with Prudential Securities Research.
Consumers, always edgy about placing a dollar value on health, have been made more uneasy by the debate over managed health care and disclosures about the tobacco industry's suppression of data.
"The public has a bad taste in its mouth about cost-benefit issues," conceded Augustine E. Moffitt Jr., vice president for safety, health and environment at Bethlehem Steel.
The courts have been little help. In 1992, for example, a Federal court ruled that the E.P.A. must stick to a strict timetable for reviewing whether rules must be tightened.
In the face of all this adversity, industry's once-united front has fragmented. The inter-industry Air Quality Standards Coalition, which insists that the E.P.A. stick with existing rules, only pretends to speak with one voice. Petroleum companies, which might have to reformulate gasoline under new rules, also want to maintain the status quo. But electric utilities simply want to postpone new rules while they deal with deregulation.
And chemical companies, which are far more worried about pending toxic-waste changes, are currying favor with the E.P.A. by offering to settle for a stricter interim air standard that could be strengthened or eased, depending on what additional research indicates.
When the Chemical Manufacturers Association belatedly joined the air-quality coalition in February, Joe J. Mayhew, its assistant vice president for environmental policy, visited Mary Nichols, the E.P.A.'s assistant administrator for air and radiation, to reassure her that his industry would remain conciliatory.
"We really believe that if we go to the E.P.A. with a supportive attitude and some data making our case, we'll do better than if we just keep arguing about costs," Mr. Mayhew said. Indeed, he concedes that Du Pont, Dow Chemical and several other companies have made money by recapturing "pollutants" like ammonia or other chemical ingredients from their waste-water streams, and by providing nonpolluting substitutes for some banned substances like chlorofluorocarbons.
Still, the conciliatory approach remains a rarity. Most companies still contend that the E.P.A. is relying on flawed studies -- and too few of them, at that. They are particularly incensed about a study by two professors at Harvard's School of Public Health that links fine dust particles to premature deaths. The professors have refused to release their raw data or talk to the press; earlier this month, Harvard agreed to let an independent research group reanalyze the data.
The E.P.A. hotly disputes that it overemphasized that study. Gary Guzy, its chief policy adviser, said the agency combed 185 ozone studies, and 86 particulate studies, before deciding to set new standards.
"Some say this is junk science, that we picked numbers out of a hat, but there is compelling evidence that people are getting sick," he said.
The American Petroleum Institute, for one, is trying to muster equally compelling evidence that they are not. In the process, it is using science as a back door through which to reintroduce cost issues.
The institute recently used scientific hypotheses about the link between soot and smog levels and respiratory illness to calculate the savings to society of a reduction in lost workdays or hospitalizations stemming from such illnesses. It concluded that in ozone-troubled Chicago, for one, industry expenditures of $2.5 billion a year to meet stricter standards would yield only $33 million worth of health benefits.
"Ten years ago, I can't think of a single industry that really quantified the benefits as well as costs," said Paul Bailey, the institute's director of health and environmental affairs.
Industry is not confining its attention to the E.P.A., of course. Last year's Congressional Review Act gives Congress the right to overturn rules it thinks are unreasonable, and some companies are trying to get their representatives to call for a vote on the proposed rules. But far more are taking their pleas to the citizenry; American Electric, for example, is sending its executives to speak to Rotary and Kiwanis Clubs.
Some are even letting histrionics -- albeit not cost-related ones -- creep back into the fray. Citizens for a Sound Economy, an inter-industry group, is taking out ads warning that the E.P.A.'s new rules will mean the end of backyard barbecues and fireworks -- a prediction that a top E.P.A. official termed "ridiculous."
Environmentalists, not surprisingly, are fighting back. The Sierra Club and Mr. Rosenberg's group recently collected thousands of postcards from families supporting tougher rules, and orchestrated a May 9 event in which mothers and their asthmatic children delivered them to the White House.
For now, industry has forgone similar media events. But environmentalists expect that if civilized whispers and scientific studies do not derail the new smog and soot rules, industry will again make cacophony its tactic of choice.
"The science argument is their lead argument inside the Beltway, but they're still running doom-and- gloom ads and Op-Eds elsewhere," Mr. Rosenberg noted. "And if the science argument doesn't work, you're going to see even more."

With its threats of dire consequences from tough anti-pollution measures repeatedly proven to be exaggerated, American industry lately has softened its rhetoric.
 
EARLY 70's
Industrial companies led the campaign against stricter pollution controls, contending that environmentalists were overstating the problem and that the technology did not exist to meet Government demands. The market responded with affordable solutions. Photo of newspaper headline dated May 4, 1970.
 
MID- TO LATE 70's
Business warned that complying with clean air and water regulations would cost jobs, drive up utility rates and make American industry less competitive. The tab turned out to be much lower. Photo of newspaper headline dated Nov. 24, 1974.
 
LATE 80's TO EARLY 90's
Industry and conservative lawmakers sought to impose cost-benefit tests on environmental regulations, arguing that the health return on capital investment often was low. The public was uncomfortable putting a price tag on health. Photo of newspaper headline dated Aug. 15, 1990.

Though still resistant to ever-tougher rules, companies increasingly seek partnerships with Government in ecology-friendly projects and promote their environmental performance. Photo of newspaper headline dated Oct. 19, 1994.

HEADLINE: Stocks and Franc Fall in France After Surprise Victory by the Left

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: PARIS, May 26

 BODY:
French stocks tumbled today, and the franc was lower, too, after the left won a surprise victory in the first round of a parliamentary election.
The benchmark CAC-40 stock index plunged in its biggest single-day loss in more than four years as the prospect of a Socialist-led government stunned investors who had driven the market higher on expectations that the center-right would win. 
 The index closed down 108.16 points, or 3.91 percent, at 2,654.74, its biggest fall in percentage terms since October 1992. The biggest losers were shares in companies involved in privatizations and revampings that risk being halted if a Socialist-led coalition gains power after the second round of elections on Sunday.
Many French voters have grown dissatisfied with privatizations and corporate overhauls, as well as the Government's austerity measures, which are intended to bring the country into line with requirements for the European common currency. The country's high unemployment has also been a source of discontent.
The franc initially reacted sharply to the election results, but prices steadied as traders anticipated that a radical shift in policy, particularly on European monetary union, was unlikely if the left won the runoff.
"The feeling, particularly in the bond market, is that the two major parties on the right and the left are committed to meeting" the criteria for monetary union, Joanne Perez, a market strategist at Merrill Lynch & Company, said. The Socialists might loosen fiscal policies slightly, but still try to ensure that France is accepted among the first round of countries in the currency union, she said.
"So even if the Socialists get in, we don't expect a real increase in the deficit in the next year or two," she said.
The franc was lower against the German mark, which generally gains when questions are raised about the currency union, because of it is seen as the safest European currency. Demand for marks also drove that currency higher against the dollar in European trading, though the mark's strength eased later in the day. Other European stock markets were higher.
Elsewhere, stocks were marginally higher in Japan, helped by some solid corporate earnings reports. The benchmark Nikkei average of 225 shares gained 34.50 points.
[On Tuesday in Tokyo, Japanese stocks closed lower. The Nikkei was down 153.61 points, or 0.77 percent, at 19,889.89.]
Financial markets were closed in the United States for Memorial Day. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Rowland's Welfare Rule Is Criticized as Too Tough

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, May 26

 BODY:
The three Republican Governors in the metropolitan region have all worked aggressively to cut the welfare rolls, but only one -- John G. Rowland of Connecticut -- has cut off food stamps to able-bodied adults.
The decision reflects Mr. Rowland's strong view that all welfare recipients must find jobs, or perform volunteer work, to retain benefits. Since February, 4,862 people in Connecticut have had their food stamps cut off. 
 Under the new Federal welfare law, able-bodied adults without children can receive food stamps for only three months unless they find a job or perform volunteer work. But 41 states have obtained waivers from that rule because of depressed economies.
Mr. Rowland, unlike his counterparts in New Jersey and New York, has refused to apply for the waiver, and some of his fellow Republicans in Connecticut view that decision as too harsh. Last week, the State Senate voted 29 to 7 to require the Governor to seek the waiver, with 10 Republicans voting for the measure.
"There is a thin line between being tough-minded and hardhearted," the State Senate President pro tem, Kevin B. Sullivan, a Democrat, said before the vote.
Connecticut lawmakers and governors in other states have asserted that it would be unfair to penalize food stamp recipients in economically depressed areas for not finding jobs. Some, like Gov. George E. Pataki of New York, have said that workfare programs that would provide volunteer opportunities in return for food stamps are not yet extensive enough.
State Representative Brian E. Mattiello, a Republican who was Mr. Rowland's point man two years ago in negotiations to scale back welfare benefits, has told Mr. Rowland that cutting off food stamps was going too far. "I acknowledge that with a benefit comes a responsibility, but we're talking about food," he said.
Most single adults receive about $120 a month in food stamps, and must have a net income below the poverty line to qualify. Under the new Federal welfare law, able-bodied adults between ages 18 and 50 without children are limited to three months of food stamps over a three-year period. Extensions can be granted if the person is working 20 hours a week, or is enrolled in a state-sponsored workfare program.
But the Federal Government agreed to waive the time limit for places where jobs are scarce. The Department of Agriculture, which administers the food stamps program, has approved waivers for areas where the unemployment rate exceeds 10 percent or in localities that have been deemed "labor surplus" areas.
Areas are designated as having a "labor surplus"if they have an unemployment rate exceeding the national average by 20 percent for two consecutive years.
Although the Connecticut Labor Department classified 15 cities and towns, including Hartford, as labor surplus areas, Mr. Rowland has refused to apply for a waiver.
Instead, he established a workfare program that would provide volunteer placements. In return for volunteering 25 hours a month at private nonprofit organizations, the individual could continue to receive food stamps for another year.
Mr. Rowland declined to be interviewed about the program, but his spokesman, Dean C. Pagani, said, "He believes the way to go is to stick with a volunteer program, and send people a message that they need to work to receive welfare."
The debate over food stamps could affect how Mr. Rowland enforces a 21-month limit, the nation's shortest, on family welfare benefits that will be phased in, starting in November. If Mr. Rowland continued food-stamp benefits for adults without children, it could set a precedent for continuing benefits for thousands of people on family welfare.
David Berry, a spokesman for the Social Service Department, disputed the notion that people could not find jobs. He pointed out that 43 percent of the people on family welfare have found work since it became a requirement in July 1995.
"We don't feel there are no jobs out there," Mr. Berry said.
Many nonprofit groups that are supposed to be offering volunteer slots have criticized the workfare program as ill-conceived, and the United Way of Connecticut last week lobbied for the bill to force Mr. Rowland to apply for the waiver.
"These are not people who are going to be ideal volunteers," said Sherby Worthen, a senior vice president of the Connecticut United Way, explaining that it was unrealistic to expect these people to find placements. Many volunteer jobs are with children or the elderly and are not well-suited for welfare recipients with criminal histories and problems with drug abuse, she said.
Outside a shelter on the outskirts of downtown Hartford, Charles Lee, 38, said that he had stopped receiving food stamps in March and that he had not known that volunteering was a way to continue getting them.
The loss of the benefits, he said, has imposed another burden in his struggle to survive. "Basically, I have to eat whatever people will give me now," said Mr. Lee, who tries to observe a Muslim diet that prohibits pork and other foods. "When someone is giving you something from their heart, you don't want to complain, but it's not healthy."
Donald Natalie, who had worked five years delivering meals to the elderly, said he was unaware that volunteering would return his monthly food stamps of $120. He said he would try to contact his social worker about it, but did not have a quarter to call her.
"Maybe in a week or so I could do that," he said, explaining that it had been only a few weeks since he had been discharged from a monthlong drug treatment program for his addiction to tranquilizers.
Still, as he sat on a bed in an emergency shelter Friday evening, he did not look ready to do any sort of work. His weight has dropped to 160 pounds from 190 since his food stamps were cut off. The last two weeks were his first in a shelter, and before that he slept in the back seat of his daughter's car. "I don't know whether I can handle the stress of any job," he said. "I feel like I've hit rock bottom and that the food stamps are just icing on the cake."

HEADLINE: A Rich Museum and Its Poor Cousin;
Detroit Institutions Are Entangled in the Politics of Race, Class and Labor

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, May 27

 BODY:
The Detroit Institute of Arts, the nation's fifth-largest art museum, is crumbling because of financial problems. Strong fences have been built around much of its base because of the imminent risk that sections of the outer walls will fall away from the building's corroded steel frame. Employees place sponges and mop heads just below gallery windows in the museum during snowstorms to catch the condensation dribbling down the single panes of thin glass.
A block away, the same city government that owns the art museum has just finished building the nation's largest museum of black history and culture. The double-paned glass dome over the rotunda at the new Museum of African-American History is specially designed to admit light while keeping the building at a steady temperature and humidity. The museum's outside doors are bronze, and many of the inside doors are mahogany. The towering decorative masks over the main entrances are partly plated with 14-carat gold. 
 The visible contrast between the museums partly reflects the difficulty of raising money to maintain old cultural institutions and the relative ease of fund-raising for new buildings. But behind the contrast also lies a remarkable example of how race, class, labor unions and big-city politics can affect cultural institutions. These issues have echoes in cultural battles in other cities, although seldom are the fights as nasty or as public as they have been in Detroit lately.
When daily management of the Milwaukee Public Museum was transferred to a private group five years ago, community activists unsuccessfully fought the transfer all the way to the Wisconsin Supreme Court. The Detroit art museum tried to follow the Milwaukee museum to private management but failed -- even though the black-history museum has always been privately managed, even as it receives greater city financing.
The tensions in Detroit were most visible during the failed attempt this spring by Mayor Dennis Archer to transfer daily management of the art museum to its mostly suburban benefactors. Those patrons, who already furnish the bulk of the museum's budget and were prepared to donate more, agreed to guarantee the jobs and salaries of all municipal workers at the museum. The museum itself would have remained city property.
But the art museum's 64 unionized janitors, guards and maintenance workers objected vehemently to losing their status as civil servants. The loss of that status would have made them more subject to disciplinary procedures and weakened their bargaining position for future labor contracts.

For the Proletariat, Not the Bourgeoisie
At a tumultuous special session in March, the Detroit City Council ended up siding with the unionized workers and blocking the management transfer. "I am for the proletariat, I am not for the bourgeoisie," declared Kay Everett, the City Council member who held the council's rotating chairmanship during the special session.
Union officials told City Council members that Detroit, the nation's largest city with a black majority, should not transfer control of one of its cultural jewels to a group of mostly white suburban residents. "The race issue was probably made the key role," said Bret A. Ceriotti Sr., the museum's chief union steward, who is white. He also told the council that it would be better to close the museum entirely than give more control to private donors.
But some of the same issues of race and class have helped the black history museum win broad public support. Voters here have approved two bond issues to cover most of the $38.4 million in construction costs.
The ample budget has produced a breathtaking museum, with a core exhibition designed by Ralph Appelbaum, the exhibition expert who also designed the Holocaust Museum in Washington. Like the Holocaust Museum, the black history museum derives its emotional power from photographs, videos and clearly worded explanatory panels.
Yet the black history museum is relatively weak in actual artifacts. Indeed, many of the traditional African masks are on loan from the art museum.
By contrast, the art museum has a remarkable collection of masterpieces, including one of the largest and most famous murals by Diego Rivera, the Mexican muralist. But with money in short supply, there are virtually no signs explaining the context or importance of the artworks.
Two years ago, the Metropolitan Museum of Art in New York borrowed Fra Angelico's "Annunciatory Angel" from Detroit and sold posters of the painting to commemorate the occasion. The 15th-century Italian Renaissance masterpiece now hangs in an obscure gallery here, ignored by visitors, with a simple label and no text to explain its significance.
The difficulty in raising money for the Detroit art museum results from the Detroit area's unusually deep social, racial and economic divisions. As reported in the 1990 census, the city's median household income, at $18,742, was barely half the national average. By comparison, Oakland County, which encompasses most of Detroit's sprawling northern suburbs, had a median household income of $43,407.
Racial issues also divide the city from the nearly all-white suburbs. The Justice Department files more housing discrimination lawsuits in Detroit's suburbs than anywhere else in the nation, while suburban police officers frequently follow people driving out of the city on residential streets instead of highways. And many city residents distrust white investors from the suburbs.
The art museum has particularly suffered in this climate. Uncertainty about its future has already begun to hurt donations of money and art. Representatives of rival museums across the country have been busily getting in touch with wealthy Michigan collectors, trying to persuade them to leave masterpieces to out-of-town institutions instead.
The latest blow came when Samuel Sachs 2d, the art museum's 62-year-old executive director, announced on May 12 that he would step down on Sept. 1 to become director of the Frick Collection in New York City. Mr. Sachs said that while he was not leaving to protest the City Council's decision, he was tired of the politics.
"There is some self-preservation left in me after 12 years" in Detroit, he said.
The new Museum of African-American History, which was founded in 1965 by a local physician, Dr. Charles Wright, has prospered in this contentious political climate. Coleman Young, the populist black Mayor here from 1974 through 1993, actively promoted the black history museum, while often criticizing the art museum for not exhibiting enough works by black artists.

Construction Costs Paid by the City
The city paid for construction of a large building in the mid-1980's to house Dr. Wright's collection, which is still owned by a nonprofit corporation he set up and which occupies city-owned buildings free of charge. Shortly before leaving office, Mr. Young decided that the city should build the even larger building that opened last month.
Excavation for the new building began immediately, even though architects had not yet come up with a design. A $20 million bond issue for construction was also approved by voters, still without any architect's plans. "We dug a hole and left it there for almost a year," until the design was ready, said Harold R. Varner, the chief architect.
Mr. Young left his more frugal successor, Mayor Archer, with little choice but to finish the project. Mr. Archer ended up spending another $18.4 million in city money to that purpose. Detroit officials have not yet decided what to do with the building that housed the black history museum for 10 years.
Yet even this popular project has not been immune to politics. Several dozen demonstrators picketed the museum when it opened, contending that it gave too much attention to slavery and did not present a sufficiently uplifting portrayal of black history. Callers to local radio stations expressed dismay that a white man, Mr. Appelbaum, played such a large role in designing the museum.
Such political maneuvering is not unique to Detroit, although it has been particularly visible here. Exhibitions on controversial issues, like the Smithsonian Institution's two years ago on the atomic bombing of Japan in 1945, have often attracted controversy themselves. Few experts see a way to avoid such squabbles.

With Public Funds Comes Politics
"Any institution heavily dependent on public funds will inevitably be involved in a political debate," said Sharon F. Patton, an associate professor of art at the University of Michigan and the director of the university's Center for Afro-American and African Studies.
Such disputes tend to be particularly open when they involve the small and shrinking number of museums directly managed by government agencies. While many big museums still receive various forms of aid from local governments, most are now operated by private, nonprofit groups or are owned by universities.
The Detroit Institute of Arts started as a private collection in 1885 and was donated to the city in 1919 by trustees. The city erected a Beaux-Arts granite building on donated land to house the collection, and automobile magnates contributed lavishly. The Ford family paid Rivera to paint one of his largest murals glorifying industrial workers in the museum's huge marble court in 1932.
But the decay of many inner cities in recent decades has put pressure on municipal budgets at the same time that corporate support for the arts has grown. The result has been a move toward private management of big art museums like Detroit's. Among major American art museums, only the San Francisco Museum of Fine Arts has a comparably high level of direct municipal control.
The four art museums in the nation larger than Detroit's -- the Metropolitan Museum of Art in New York, the National Gallery of Art in Washington, the Museum of Fine Arts in Boston and the Philadelphia Museum of Art -- give donors more influence over the day-to-day management.
The contrast in management structures of the Detroit Institute of Arts and the Museum of African-American History is one reason for their very different fates. While the art museum struggles with its union, the nonprofit group running the black-history museum has been free to pursue much cheaper alternatives. Kimberly Camp, the museum's president, said the nonprofit structure had permitted a streamlined management.
To the dismay of labor officials, the black history museum has contracts with nonunion companies to provide security guards and most maintenance workers. The private guards at the black history museum earn $7 an hour while unionized guards at the art museum earn $11.21 an hour.
The question now for many Detroit-area residents and for art followers around the country is how this tale will end. The black history museum attracted 100,000 visitors in the three weeks after it opened on April 12, even more than its leaders expected, and is receiving a flood of gifts from big corporations like the Ford Motor Company and AT&T.
The future of the art institute is much less clear. Mayor Archer has asked the City Council to find an extra $1.1 million in the fiscal year beginning July 1 to offset a steady reduction in state aid. About $14 million of a $25 million municipal bond issue has not yet been sold but could eventually go toward the $60 million cost of repairing the museum's walls and windows.
Nobody has come up with a solution yet for the museum's biggest problem: a special endowment that provides $8 million of the museum's $30 million operating budget will be exhausted by the middle of next year.
Several City Council members argue that the art museum should be able to raise as much money as a privately run institution. Museum officials counter that benefactors are reluctant to donate money to government agencies.
Maryann Mahaffey, the City Council's president, who voted with Ms. Everett against a transfer of control, said during the council's special session that she believed the museum's $4.9 million "rainy day" fund and other reserves would allow it to continue operations while city officials tried to find a long-term solution.
A few city officials still have hopes of drafting a new plan for greater private involvement in the museum. But with elections in November, the City Council is showing little interest in such an approach.
As Mr. Sachs prepares to move to Manhattan to manage the Frick Collection, he is delighted to be leaving this city's battles behind.
"Politics and art and culture really don't mix very well," he said. "They both may be necessary for a society, but apart from each other they are better, for all parties concerned."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Registers Broad Gains, Aided by U.S. Economic Data

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar rose broadly yesterday on a mix of healthy United States economic data, heightened prospects for a weak single European currency and remarks by Switzerland's top banker that caused a selloff in the Swiss franc.
The buying pushed the dollar to the highest level against the mark in more than a week and the highest against the yen in nearly two weeks. 
 "The dollar, after some weeks of consolidation, looks like it's coming back a bit," said Earl I. Johnson, foreign-exchange economist at the Bank of Montreal's Chicago branch.
In New York, the dollar settled at 116.68 yen, up from 115.66 yen on Friday and 116.43 yen on Monday in Europe. The dollar rose to 1.7055 marks, from 1.6935 marks on Friday, and 1.6901 marks on Monday in Europe. The pound fell to $1.6316, from $1.6345 on Friday, and $1.6405 Monday in Europe. Financial markets in the two largest centers for currency trading -- London and New York -- were closed on Monday for national holidays.
Much of the dollar's gain came in European dealings after Hans Meyer, president of the Swiss National Bank, confirmed it was pumping money into the system to counter an unwanted rise in the Swiss franc.
The uncertainties created by the French elections and plans for a single European currency in 1999 have raised demand for the Swiss franc in recent days as a haven. Switzerland is not a participant in the single currency, the euro.
"We will remain vigilant and if necessary counter" a development in the currency market that threatens economic recovery, Mr. Meyer said. A strong currency tends to impede economic growth by making exports less competitive.
"The Swiss economy isn't doing very well at all, so they don't want a strong currency at this juncture," said John Hazelton, chief currency trader at the Manufacturers and Traders Trust Company in New York. "The only thing they can do is ease monetary policy and provide as much money as they can."
The dollar rose to 1.4212 Swiss francs in New York trading, up from 1.4032 Friday, and 1.4008 Monday in Europe.
The dollar was further helped by a surprisingly large gain in the Conference Board's monthly survey of consumer sentiment in the United States, which rose to a 28-year high in May. The survey results were viewed as a strong inducement for the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates at its meeting on July 1 and 2.

HEADLINE: Confidence Index Soars; Home Resales Off

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Consumer confidence rose to a 28-year high in May, the Conference Board said yesterday. A separate economic report showed that home resales fell more than expected in April as borrowing costs continued to creep up.
The Conference Board said its index of consumer confidence increased to 127.1 in May, above analysts' expectations, from April's reading, which was revised upward to 118.5 from the previous estimate of 116.8. 
 An index gauging consumer expectations for the next six months rose to 110.3, from a revised 103.2 in April, while an index tracking consumers' assessment of present conditions jumped to 152.2 from a revised 141.6.
With the economy continuing to create jobs as stocks set records, consumers remain confident about the economy and about their economic prospects, analysts said. That means they should continue to spend. Consumer outlays account for two-thirds of economic activity over all.
"There's no doubt that unemployment at 4.9 percent and the stock market at record highs were the ingredients for this high consumer confidence," said Kevin Flanagan, money market economist at Dean Witter Reynolds Inc. in New York. "This indicates consumers will continue to spend, which will keep pressure on the Federal Reserve to raise rates again."
Earlier this month, policy makers at the Federal Reserve voted to leave interest rates unchanged, betting that the economy was slowing sufficiently to avert a resurgence of inflation. They had raised rates earlier in the year.
Analysts have said that the Federal Reserve may still feel compelled to raise rates again this year, perhaps at its next monetary policy meeting in July. They said the decision this month not to raise rates was driven by evidence that economic growth had moderated substantially from the 5.6 percent rate of the first quarter, as well as by reports showing that inflation was almost nonexistent at the producer level and subdued at the consumer level.
In yesterday's report, the Conference Board said fewer respondents than last month expected their incomes to rise over the next six months. The index showing expectations for higher incomes slipped to 22.5 in May from 23.6 in April. The indexes measuring plans to buy a home, automobile or major appliances over the next six months all fell.
In the report on home resales, the National Association of Realtors said resales fell 2.4 percent in April, exceeding forecasts, to an annual rate of 4.060 million. During March, resales decreased a revised 1.7 percent, to an annual rate of 4.16 million. The March total was originally estimated as a 2.8 percent drop.
The figures represent closings on resales, which lag behind contract signings by a few months. Resales make up about 85 percent of all single-family home sales.
"Buyers are just cooling their heels," said Alan Day, vice president of the Stratevest Group in Burlington, Vt. He said he expected sales to increase in months ahead as low unemployment, strong consumer confidence and continued stock market gains pushed more buyers into the market.
Resales rose 3.1 percent in the Northeast during April, to a 660,000 annual rate, but fell 3.9 percent in the South, to 1.47 million, and 6.1 percent in the West, to 920,000. They were unchanged in the Midwest, at 1.01 million.
Experts differ about whether home resales, and the housing market as a whole, can remain close to the record levels of last year and 1997's first quarter or whether demand for houses will slow as the year progresses.
Those who bet demand will slow cite a declining rate of household formation, a lack of pent-up demand and consumer debt burdens. They also note that mortgage rates increased by more than half a percentage point between February and April.
The average rate on a 30-year fixed mortgage rose to 8.14 percent in April from 7.90 percent in March. That put the average principal and interest payment on a $100,000 loan at $743.55 last month, up from $726.81 in March.

HEADLINE: Essay;
Send Beijing a Message

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Mike Mansfield, the great Senate majority leader after Lyndon Johnson, offered an amendment every year to draw down American forces in Europe. President Nixon and the foreign-policy establishment regularly denounced it as isolationist.
Once, after a meeting with legislative leaders in the Cabinet Room, Nixon drew Mansfield aside to say: "Stick with that amendment of yours. I use it all the time to keep the Europeans in line." 
 The threat of Congressional action redirecting U.S. foreign policy has been used by Presidents skilled in quiet diplomacy. Their message to foreign leaders: If you don't make some concessions to American opinion, I won't be able to restrain my irresponsible legislators. Now here's how we can stop them . . .
Bill Clinton doesn't seem to get the hang of that in dealing with China. On the advice of business executives, and after the personal urging of Indonesia's James Riady, Clinton threw away his economic leverage. Ever since he flip-flopped -- not only betraying human rights supporters but ignoring China's arming of Iran -- he has been all give and no take.
His pre-emptive concession in 1994 on low tariffs was based on the new China lobby's assurances that free trade would lead toward free enterprise and freedom of expression.
But the result of three years' appeasement was drearily expressed in the State Department's latest report: "All public dissent . . . was effectively silenced by intimidation, exile, the imposition of prison terms. . . . No dissidents were known to be active at year's end."
Clinton's open-doormat policy is a failure. China rings up sales in our market, strengthening its army's grip at home, while the White House wrings its hands.
Now the House of Representatives is considering the resumption of leverage. Last year it voted 2 to 1 to approve an extension of most-favored-nation status; this year the vote will be much closer.
That's because China's assault on U.S. interests has created a remarkable coalition here. On the left, unions resisting free trade join liberals defending human rights and the integrity of Tibet to demand pressure on China. On the right, anti-Communist hard-liners find themselves linked with the Christian Coalition and Catholic bishops who oppose religious persecution and forced abortion.
As a result, the heat is on Congress to act as "tough cop" to Clinton's "nice cop." The House's Democratic leader, Dick Gephardt, denounces "free-market Stalinism" while the Republican leader, Dick Armey, publicly agonizes at switching from his M.F.N. support last year. Lawmakers have a free shot at voting their consciences and their constituencies because they know the Senate will not override a Clinton veto.
Let the House send Beijing a message. A vote against extending M.F.N. would strengthen the President's wet noodle of a hand and would not, this year at least, impose higher tariffs or harm the economy of Hong Kong.
Congress can further strengthen Clinton's hand in this paradoxical way: by pursuing investigations into Chinese penetration of the White House and influence on our elections.
Asians set great store by "face." We are constantly reminded how any attempt to impose the value of freedom, or to restrain the arming of rogue states, would be taken as a terrible insult to the pride of China's new leaders.
But what about American face? If it is shown that Lippo, Huang, Trie & Co., in cahoots with the Beijing arms dealer and White House visitor Wang Jun, tried to manipulate U.S. policy and elections, should we not take umbrage at that insult?
While warning us loudly not to interfere in its internal affairs, China was secretly interfering in America's internal affairs. Chinese spokesmen deny everything. But Zhou Enlai's heirs are highly sophisticated diplomats and intelligence agents. They know we have tapes of taps on their Los Angeles consulate that show unlawful political fund-raising activity. They know these could be declassified and played at a Senate hearing or at the U.N.
Prepare to take diplomatic offense, Mr. President. Let Beijing know we will accept a "rogue operation" excuse, but only if accompanied by cooperation with the F.B.I., human rights gestures and restraint on arms sales. Otherwise, the nice-cop President won't be able to control that tough-cop Congress on low tariffs next time around.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Iran's Leader Blames U.S. For Poor Ties

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: TEHERAN, Iran, May 27

 BODY:
In his first extended remarks since winning a landslide victory last week, Iran's newly elected President, Mohammed Khatami, blamed the United States today for two decades of animosity and said that Washington held the key to improving ties.
"We are sorry to see that the United States' policy has always been hostile to our revolution," said President-elect Khatami, a moderate whose stunning upset victory reflected rising dissatisfaction with Iran's ruling clerics. 
 He said that any warming of relations with Washington would depend on observable changes in America's attitude toward Iran. "But unfortunately we have not seen anything," he said. "Therefore, the key to the problem is in their hands and not ours."
Political experts have described Mr. Khatami's election as a turning point in Iran's revolution, raising some expectation that he might moderate the Islamic republic's policies toward the West.
But the stance taken today by Mr. Khatami did not differ radically from the usual hard line taken by other Iranian officials. And it was close to that of the departing President, Ali Akbar Hashemi Rafsanjani, whom Mr. Khatami will replace in August.
It was not as uncompromising, however, as some of the positions taken by Iran's supreme leader, Ayatollah Ali Khamenei, who has the final say over changes in Iranian policy and has rejected improving ties with Washington.
Senior United States officials with President Clinton at a NATO summit meeting in Paris insisted today that Iran must first change its policies before it could expect any changes in the longstanding American campaign to isolate it.
"The things that concern us and trouble us about Iran's behavior in the world need to change for us to think more liberally about Iran's role in the international community," said the White House spokesman, Mike McCurry. "And there's no evidence that we see of any change in the fundamental policies of that regime."
The United States has imposed economic sanctions on Iran, accusing it of sponsoring terrorism, pursuing nuclear arms and supporting efforts to wreck the Middle East peace efforts.
Mr. Khatami dismissed the charges of Iranian involvement in international terrorism. But he said Iran opposed the Middle East peace effort, while making it clear that Teheran would not take any action to disrupt it.
He also took a hard line toward Israel, accusing it of state terrorism.
On domestic issues, Mr. Khatami said the time was ripe to insure more democracy in Iran but insisted this had to be done while defending the Islamic Government from enemies.
He said Iran was stable enough and had recovered sufficiently from its war with Iraq in the 1980's to begin guaranteeing its 60 million citizens full constitutional rights, including free thought, life, employment, assembly and association.
"We hope to gradually witness a more legal society," he said, "with more clearly defined rights and duties for citizens and the Government."
The remarks seemed likely to arouse opposition among conservatives, even though Mr. Khatami was careful to clarify that he was advocating changes only within Iran's existing system of Islamic Government.

HEADLINE: Congress Wages War on Free Trade

BYLINE: By Jeffrey E. Garten; Jeffrey E. Garten, dean of the Yale School of Management and former Under Secretary of Commerce in the first Clinton Administration, is author of "The Big Ten: The Big Emerging Markets and How They Will Change Our Lives.".

DATELINE: NEW HAVEN

 BODY:
The struggle between the Clinton Administration and Congress over renewing China's most-favored-nation trading status is a symptom of a much larger problem with American trade policy.
That is the growing tendency of Congress to use trade sanctions to pursue non-economic goals, including protecting human rights, improving conditions for foreign workers, safeguarding the environment, stopping illegal drug trafficking and curtailing nuclear weapons sales. Yes, these are worthy goals, but using trade sanctions to try to achieve them will only backfire. 
 Trade has never been more crucial to national well-being. Exports have accounted for nearly a third of our economic growth in the 1990's, and some 12 million people owe their jobs to sales of American products abroad.
Yet the Administration's legal authority to conclude major trade negotiations has expired, and Congress won't renew it. The reason: The House and Senate cannot resolve whether the President's trade negotiators should be obligated to tie environmental and labor standards to all commercial accords. Thus the Administration cannot, for example, pursue the creation of free trade areas in Latin America and Asia -- two of the President's highest priorities.
Congress, pursuing other foreign policy goals, has also broadened its use of unilateral sanctions, including embargoes on Cuba (to try to force the collapse of the Castro Government) and Iran (to express outrage against terrorism). In the case of the most-favored-nation fight over China, it's no longer just the usual human rights concerns that critics are bringing up, but also complaints (fueled largely by the religious right) over Beijing's policies on birth control and its crackdown on unregistered evangelical churches.
So why won't trade sanctions help? First, Europe and Japan steadfastly refuse to join us in using trade as a lever; instead, they merrily conclude their own commercial deals, such as ongoing French investments in Iran's oil production. By imposing sanctions unilaterally, we only incur resentment among the countries we want to influence, and we undercut our own companies, which are increasingly standard-bearers of American values and influence.
If Congress continues to use trade sanctions as a weapon, the United States will forfeit its leadership in the cause for open global markets. Just look at South America, where Brazil is now trying to build a gigantic trade bloc along with Argentina, Chile and others, without American participation. Or watch how Japan is spinning a commercial web from Seoul to Jakarta while Washington ties itself in knots over whether to let the President negotiate more trade agreements.
As billions of new consumers enter the world economy for the first time, trade will be ever more important to American living standards. The big emerging markets -- China, India, Indonesia, Turkey, Mexico and Brazil -- are growing two to three times faster than Europe and Japan. Already the United States exports more to the top 10 emerging markets than to Europe and Japan combined.
Our fastest-growing trading partners are the very countries that present the biggest human rights and security concerns: Why would we want to cut off our influence with them now?
The Administration and Congress should face the reality that not every national goal -- whether it be trade or military security or human rights -- can receive the same attention; nor is every objective equally important to our broad national interests. In most cases, commercial diplomacy should be the leading edge of our foreign policy, because free trade creates private wealth and opens ties between American and foreign businessmen. These ties can create pressure for more democracy and encourage foreign governments to loosen controls on their societies. This has happened in South Korea, for example.
To relieve the pressure to link trade to other objectives, we need credible global policies on human rights, labor standards, weapons sales and environmental protection. One approach is for Washington to help put teeth into United Nations agencies like as the International Labor Organization, which monitors treatment of workers worldwide but lacks the power to condemn countries with egregious labor practices.
Moreover, if the United States would substitute diplomatic pressure for economic sanctions to punish countries whose policies we most strongly oppose, we might find that Europe and Japan will be more willing to do the same.
Washington should also enlist American businesses, and powerful industry associations like the Business Roundtable and the National Association of Manufacturers. American companies have so far have been too hesitant to adopt higher standards for treatment of employees overseas and to invest in schools, hospitals and other community projects where they do business. After all, they have the most to lose if Congress continues its heavy-handed ways.
Trade has the potential to expose emerging nations to those values Americans hold highest. Linking trade to so many other foreign policy goals will insure that we lose on all accounts.

HEADLINE: Money Doesn't Buy Test Scores, Studies Say

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL 

 BODY:
In ordering New Jersey to equalize school spending by giving more to poor districts earlier this month, the New Jersey Supreme Court made the common-sense assumption that money is a key to improving inner-city education.
But current education research suggests that unless new money is put into specific programs like math enrichment or budgeted toward specific goals like reducing the size of kindergarten classes, it is likely to be absorbed in salaries or bricks and mortar, which generate little direct gain in student achievement. 
 "Money, of course, buys things," said Chris Pipho, a spokesman for the Education Commission of the States, a public policy and research organization based in Denver. "What complicates matters is that it may be bamboozled away."
The New Jersey ruling declared that Gov. Christine Todd Whitman's plan to improve urban schools by emphasizing curriculum changes instead of spending is unconstitutional. It will result in an extra $1,000 or so per pupil for the poorest districts, for a total of roughly $250 million in extra state spending next year.
The court, in its 5-to-1 majority opinion, asserted that the money would make a difference. "Real achievement still depends on the sufficiency of educational resources," Associate Justice Alan B. Handler wrote. But what seems obvious to jurists is a matter of bitter contention among academic researchers. Economists, sociologists and statisticians have been wrestling with the question for a quarter-century, decades in which declining urban school systems have been suing their states (including New Jersey) to correct spending imbalances.
The most comprehensive survey to date of the links between spending and student performance -- an analysis of several hundred independent studies by Eric Hanushek, an economist at the University of Rochester -- found no consistent evidence that more money beyond a certain level of spending led to higher achievement on standardized tests.
But other specialists point to a handful of experiments that they say prove extra dollars do matter. One such experiment is Project STAR in Tennessee. Researchers have long been intrigued by findings from Tennessee's ambitious controlled experiment in which about 11,000 children in kindergarten through third grade were randomly assigned either to large classes (22 to 25 students) or small ones (13 to 17 students) during the mid-1980's.
Alan Krueger, a Princeton University researcher, found that the first year a child was assigned to a small class, his or her score on standardized tests rose sharply. Thereafter, though, the gains from the smaller class were marginal. "It's worth the money to get kids ready for learning," Mr. Krueger concluded.
When pressed, most researchers agree that the states could buy better schools, provided the resources are carefully budgeted for what works rather than what administrators, teachers and parents want.
"Money is necessary," said Frank Levy, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, "but it is never sufficient."
He and others point with optimism to the national school standards movement and support setting tangible goals, backing the effort with cash and rewarding teachers and administrators. "Money tied to standards can really have some leverage," Mr. Levy said.
The intellectual debate over school finances really began with the sociologist James Coleman's 1966 report, "Equality of Educational Opportunity." Mr. Coleman found that wide variations in school spending did not have any systematic impact on reading and math scores.
"Coleman was really surprised, as were others," recalled Gary Burtless, an economist at the Brookings Institution.
In 1986, Mr. Hanushek picked up the work by surveying hundreds of research papers that studied whether money affected student achievement. His conclusion: not really.
He updated the work three years ago, and found similar results. A few studies suggested a link between student achievement and smaller class size, but there was almost no link with teacher salaries, teacher experience and outlays per pupil.
Mr. Hanushek's analysis has been challenged, notably by Larry Hedges of the University of Chicago's Department of Education.
Crude counts of success or failure to find returns to investments in education are misleading, Mr. Hedges argued, because the studies amount to apples and oranges. Reanalyzing the results, but weighting each study according to sample size and the statistical certainty of the conclusions, he estimated that a $500 increase in spending per student would increase scores by several percentage points. That translates into a $25 billion spending increase for the nation's 51.2 million schoolchildren -- the equivalent of a 10 percent raise in the nation's current public education spending of $244 billion.
Investing scarce education dollars where they would do the most good is difficult, Mr. Levy said. For even when educators quietly agree on the best use of extra money, there may be political pressures to spend it in other ways.

LENGTH: 857 words

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
Interpublic invests in an Internet provider aimed at 'the transition generation' of young adults.

BYLINE: By Stuart Elliott 

 BODY:
THE Interpublic Group of Companies is expanding its presence in the realm of on-line advertising by investing in Tripod Inc., an innovative interactive marketing and media company that focuses primarily on consumers in their 20's.
Interpublic, the world's third-largest agency company, is spending about $2.5 million for an estimated 10 percent stake in Tripod, a five-year-old enterprise based in Williamstown, Mass. Tripod's operations include a Web site, at www://tripod.com; a magazine, Tools for Life, distributed with the Prentice Hall textbooks sold on campuses by Viacom Inc., and Student Advantage, a card offering discounts on goods and services in travel, entertainment and other fields. The operations all specialize in what Tripod describes on its home page as "the transition generation, young adults who are moving from college to the workplace." 
 The investment by Interpublic, which owns giant agency networks like McCann-Erickson Worldwide and Lowe Group, is part of a $10 million infusion of additional financing for Tripod from investors that also include the Rho Management Company, a venture capital firm, and Cowen & Company, an investment bank that helped bring Interpublic and Tripod to each other's attention.
The deal is emblematic of how the huge agency companies that dominate Madison Avenue are staking claims in cyberspace through investments in independent companies as well as by supporting internal start-ups.
That strategy offers as many risks as it does rewards. For instance, although Interpublic has profited handsomely from an investment in CKS Group, a joint venture with the Infusion Com Corporation that was formed in November went sour as Infusion Com filed for Federal bankruptcy protection four months later.
"You can't bat 1.000," said Barry R. Linsky, senior vice president for planning and business development at Interpublic in New York.
"It's learning on the fly, like shooting at a moving target," he added. "The trick is to manage your exposure and therefore your risk."
Mr. Linsky discussed the Tripod agreement -- the first giving Interpublic a stake in what is known as an Internet content provider -- in a telephone interview yesterday. Details of the deal are scheduled to be announced today.
"We're looking for opportunities where we can be a strategic investor, not a passive investor," he said, "and for ventures that will enable us to pass insights to our agencies and our clients."
Interpublic executives began meeting with Tripod managers around Labor Day last year, Mr. Linsky recalled.
"The more we understood what they were doing," he said, "the greater appreciation we had for what they were about: building an interactive destination with an orientation for a young audience, the so-called Generation X that's important to a lot of advertisers but not easy to reach." Those consumers, at the heart of the 18-to-34-year-old demographic segment, are among the most desirable because they are acquisitive and in the embryonic stages of forming brand preferences.
The Tripod Web site is divided into content sections that bear headings like "Work and Money," "Living and Travel," "TriTeca" and "Daily Scoop." Among Tripod's service offerings are resume writing, job hunting and helping younger computer users create home pages.
"People in this generation are living in a fundamentally different reality than people 10 or 15 years ago," said Bo Peabody, 26, chief executive of Tripod. "It's a much scarier, less secure environment in which mutual funds and condoms are as important as music and sports.
"When they now come out of college, it's an entrepreneurial, do-it-yourself culture," he added. "You have to be savvy, and a little bit cagey, about your life. And they see the Web, with its 'infotainment' and 'edutainment,' becoming a medium with which to solve their problems."
Tripod's advertisers include the AT& T Worldnet Service of the AT& T Corporation, Amtrak, the Chase Manhattan Corporation, the Fidelity mutual funds sold by the FMR Corporation, the Ford Motor Company, Mastercard, Northwest Airlines, the Sony Corporation and Visa.
"We obviously think Interpublic can be a big help to us in the advertising community," Mr. Peabody said.
"One of our mantras is 'Know what you don't know,' " he added. "While we may know the audience, and the technology to build communities on line, we may not know some of the relationship-building that goes on in an industry like that."
Mr. Peabody founded Tripod along with Brett Hershey, a classmate of his at Williams College, and Richard Sabot, an economics professor there. Tripod now has 40 employees: 36 in Williamstown and 4 in New York.
The $10 million from the investors including Interpublic compares with the "almost exactly $4 million we've spent up until now," Mr. Peabody said, from investors like New Enterprises Associates, a leading venture capital firm. Mr. Peabody predicted that Tripod would become profitable in the first quarter of 1998.
Gil Fuchsberg, director for new media and technologies at Interpublic, will become one of five members of the Tripod board. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH
