HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Slip as Traders Await Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices fell yesterday, pushing the yield on the benchmark 30-year bond above the psychologically important level of 7 percent for the first time in nearly a month.
Prices edged lower as nervous investors adjusted their holdings in anticipation of economic data scheduled for release this week, including reports on durable goods and gross domestic product, that are expected to be negative. 
 Another factor weighing on prices were the actions of securities dealers who were preparing for Treasury note auctions, beginning today with a two-year issue and ending tomorrow with a five-year maturity. Dealers will typically cause yields to rise ahead of an auction to make securities that much more attractive to investors, making for quicker distribution.
The 30-year bond fell 15/32, to a price of 95 2/32 to yield 7.02 percent, up from 6.98 percent on Friday. The yield last ended at more than 7 percent on April 28, when it was 7.11 percent. Bond prices and their yields move in opposite directions.
Economic data released yesterday included the consumer confidence figures for May. The report, by the Conference Board, showed that the level of confidence has risen to a 28-year high; that is a negative for bonds because it suggests continued consumer spending and possible inflation, which could erode the value of long-term investments like bonds.
Also released were data on existing housing sales, which fell, as expected, to an annualized rate of 4.06 million units in April from 4.16 million in March. Even though the number is lower, the trend in housing remains strong.
Greg Jones, an economist with Briefing.com, a market analysis firm in Menlo Park, Calif., noted that yields were now higher than they were before last week's meeting of Federal Reserve policy makers, at which interest rates were left unchanged.
"The market's confidence in the Fed has clearly suffered a blow, as bond yields have risen sharply in the wake of Fed inaction," Mr. Jones said. A strong reading in the consumer-confidence report, he added, "highlights the risk of inaction, and argues for a tightening" when Fed policy makers next meet in July.
Trading in the Treasury market thinned in the afternoon, with little impetus for buyers.
"The market found it difficult to simply shrug off the firmer confidence measures especially because they confirmed the trend reflected in the preliminary University of Michigan consumer sentiment figures released a week ago," said Anthony Chan, chief economist for the Banc One Investment Advisors Corporation in Columbus, Ohio.
"With two confidence numbers telling us that the consumer may be back again in the second and third quarters, it is hard to keep faith in the consensus view that expects a major slowing in economic growth in the second quarter," Mr. Chan said. "Besides, with strength in both the current and future components of this index, investors were not able to look the other way and assume that much of this strength might simply dissipate within a month or two."
At the Treasury's regular weekly bill auction yesterday, rates were down but demand was not as good as many had expected. The rate for a three-month bill dropped to 5.03 percent, from 5.17 percent a week earlier; the rate for a six-month issue fell to 5.26 percent, from 5.35 percent.
In when-issued trading ahead of today's auction, the two-year maturity was being offered at a price to yield 6.30 percent, while the five-year was offered at a price to yield 6.62 percent for tomorrow's auction.
Following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three-month and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: William Cunningham, a Guardian of the Poor, Dies at 67

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, May 27

 BODY:
The Rev. William T. Cunningham, a Roman Catholic priest who became a leading Detroit civil rights advocate and founder of one of the nation's largest programs to feed and train the urban poor, died here on Monday. He was 67.
The cause was pneumonia and a liver infection that followed his treatment for gallbladder cancer, which had spread to the liver and lymph nodes, said Robert P. Schweers, a spokesman for the Barbara Ann Karmanos Cancer Institute. 
 It was Detroit's race riots, the nation's bloodiest of the 1960's, that prompted Father Cunningham to found his nonprofit civic organization, called Focus: Hope. Starting with just a few high school students as volunteers, he built the group into a sprawling enterprise that distributes food to 47,500 poor people a month and trains more than a thousand mostly black youths each year to become engineers and factory technicians.
Focus: Hope now has 800 employees, thousands of volunteers and an annual budget of $71 million. It even operates its own factory, which serves as a training ground but also sells auto parts used by Detroit's Big Three, including engine intake manifolds on the Ford Tauruses driven by millions of Americans. Among the factory program's many prominent admirers are President Clinton and former President George Bush, each of whom visited the plant while in office.
The General Motors Corporation, the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation all became active supporters of Focus: Hope, although Father Cunningham periodically criticized the auto industry until the end of his life for not doing even more to promote racial harmony. Speaking at a conference of almost 1,000 executives from auto makers and auto parts manufacturers last August, he denounced the scarcity of blacks in his audience.
William Thomas Cunningham, a Detroit native, began his studies for the priesthood at Sacred Heart Seminary here and continued them at St. John's Provincial Seminary in the nearby community of Plymouth. He was ordained in 1955.
Later he taught English literature, notably Chaucer, at a local Catholic high school. He wore a leather jacket, sometimes used salty language and rode a big Harley-Davidson motorcycle, said Carl J. Bidleman, who was one of the first high school students to join Focus: Hope as a volunteer and is now a video production expert in San Francisco.
Father Cunningham's life was changed in July 1967, when he saw clouds of dark smoke rising from burning neighborhoods a few blocks from the school, signaling the onset of race riots that would last a week and leave more than 40 people dead, 2,000 injured and 5,000 homeless.
During the following school year, when many other whites were stockpiling guns and food for a possible renewal of rioting, he began working with his students and neighborhood leaders to promote racial harmony and relieve poverty.
After gathering donations of food and clothing for riot victims, he began looking for more permanent sources of assistance. He was one of the first to persuade the Agriculture Department to donate large quantities of stockpiled food to the indigent in big cities. Focus: Hope still distributes Agriculture Department commodities to 20,500 low-income mothers and children each month and, in another program, provides a monthly food supplement to 27,000 elderly poor.
When foreign competition and high interest rates in the early 1980's forced auto makers to close many of their huge factories here, shattering Detroit's economy, Father Cunningham began spending most of his time on job training programs. He enlisted the support of city leaders, who provided grants, and auto executives, who gave money and expertise and began hiring the programs' graduates even in a weak job market.
"He was the son of a real estate developer," Mr. Bidleman said. "He could pull deals together."
Talented inner-city high school students can now enter Focus: Hope's intensive seven-week summer course in manufacturing skills like math. And successful summer students can go on to the organization's Machinist Training Institute, for a full-year course in precision machining.
Many of these students are hired away before they complete the course, because manufacturers in the area are desperate for skilled industrial workers, who can earn as much as $70,000 a year with overtime. But students who finish the course are eligible to pursue a bachelor's degree in engineering at the Center for Advanced Technologies, which Focus: Hope runs in conjunction with six local universities.
For years, Father Cunningham was critical of the Catholic Church in the Detroit area for not taking a greater role in encouraging mostly white parishes to work with mostly black parishes to prevent racial violence. The church has since become very active in trying to help the inner city, and the archdiocese has kept its offices downtown even as many businesses have moved out to the suburbs.
Adam Cardinal Maida, the Archibishop of Detroit since 1990, said in a telephone interview today that Father Cunningham's dynamic personality and deep religious convictions had given him a rare ability to bring people together. "He was able to do things, because of the credibility he had in the community, that other priests and maybe even the church leadership couldn't do," Cardinal Maida said.
Father Cunningham is survived by a sister, Betty Horning of Dryden, Mich., a Detroit suburb.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Rev. William T. Cunningham. (Associated Press, 1996)

HEADLINE: DECISION FOR NATO: IN RUSSIA;
From Public, a Collective Ho-Hum

SERIES: EXPANDING ALLIANCE: Russian Voices

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, May 27

 BODY:
Seeking to defend the new accord with NATO he signed today in Paris, President Boris N. Yeltsin is telling voters that he still opposes the expansion of the Western alliance and is trying to minimize the threat to Russia.
But try explaining that to Dimitri Beletsri, the 34-year-old manager of an electronics store. "Actually, I have not seen any threat from the West since I graduated from school and the brainwashing stopped," he said today. "Personally, I see a much greater danger in the incompetence of our leaders. Our tax policy encourages crime and corruption. That is the real threat to our country." 
 For all the storm and fury that NATO expansion has generated among the politicians, much of the Russian public has so far greeted the debate with a collective yawn.
Public opinion surveys taken before Mr. Yeltsin agreed to sign the accord indicated that few Russians were happy about the North Atlantic Treaty Organization's decision to expand. But they also indicated that the issue ranked relatively low on the average citizen's list of concerns, already dominated by more pressing worries over unpaid wages, taxes and crime.
The schism between the NATO-bashing of Russia's political elite and relative indifference of much of the public reflects the deep distrust many Russians have of their leaders and of the news media, which are increasingly viewed as a captive of special interests.
The gap also has important consequences for Western policy makers. It reduces the risk that an anti-reform demagogue will rise to power because of a popular backlash over NATO expansion. The concern that NATO expansion will cause the West to "lose" Russia is not well supported by the polls. Nationalist politicians will continue to try to stir up anti-NATO sentiment as the Parliament debates the accord in the weeks ahead. And much will depend on whether NATO and Russia manage to form a constructive working relationship as sketched out in the agreement signed today.
But the temperate public response to date does not mean that NATO expansion will be cost-free. In the nascent Russian democracy, it is Russia's political establishment, not the public, that decides national security issues.
NATO expansion has jeopardized the Parliament's ratification of the Start 2 strategic arms treaty, a high American priority. It has also forced the Clinton Administration to use up political capital with Moscow that might have been expended on other Russian-American issues, such as arms control and dealing with weapons proliferation.
"In general, this is not such a serious problem for rank and file Russians, especially as compared to such problems as wages and Yeltsin's health," said Yuri A. Levada, the director of the Russian Center for Public Opinion and Market Research. "The politicians' speech is quite another thing."
The politicians' bombast over NATO expansion is discounted by many Russians as an effort to pander to nationalists or distract attention from economic ills, polling experts say. Russians worry about foreign adversaries, but are more likely to see them in Afghanistan or Chechnya than in the West.
But in the Foreign Ministry meeting rooms NATO still summons up ghosts for Russia's ruling elite.
Throughout the cold war, the West saw the alliance as a defensive organization, resolute but extremely cautious. But Russian leaders viewed NATO as the latest in a long list of foreign foes that have sought to encircle Russia. NATO's plan to expand was another humiliation.
The Russian news media have often echoed the official anxiety. A recent Russian news service dispatch noted the vow of Russian prostitutes in Crimea not to offer their services to NATO sailors. It ran under the headline: Women of Easy Virtue to Show Patriotism.
But after concluding that he could not block NATO's decision, Mr. Yeltsin sought to fend off its hard-line critics by negotiating a face-saving agreement. Some of Mr. Yeltsin's bitterest rivals, like Aleksandr I. Lebed, the ousted security adviser and presidential hopeful, have denounced the accord as a sell-out.
Many politicians, however, basically accept Mr. Yeltsin's argument that the accord makes the best of a bad situation.
While the politicians squabble, much of the Russian public has tuned out the debate. Russian public opinion surveys indicate that about 40 percent of Russians polled oppose NATO plans to admit Poland, the Czech Republic and Hungary into the alliance.
That is more than twice as many as support NATO enlargement, but it is still a relatively modest percentage given decades of anti-NATO propaganda and Russia's tradition of suspicion of foreigners. The largest percentage of those polled do not have an opinion or do not care.
The percentage of those opposed increases when Russians are asked about their attitude of NATO expanding to include former Soviet republics, such as Ukraine or the Baltic states. NATO has not excluded this possibility, but it is also not currently considering such steps.
Significantly, the percentage of those opposed has changed little in recent months, despite the drumbeat of anti-NATO statements by the politicians.
"The public sees the struggle against NATO expansion from a skeptical viewpoint," the Public Opinion Foundation, a Russian polling organization, concluded in a recent analysis of its results.
According to a February survey conducted by the foundation, only 18 percent of those polled believed the Government's opposition to NATO expansion was "an attempt to protect Russia from the West." Another 14 percent believed that the leaders' real concern was "fear of losing control over Russia's former allies."
By contrast, 38 percent saw the politicians' attack on NATO expansion as a bargaining tactic to get more foreign aid, an attempt to divert attention from economic problems or an effort to score political points at home.
Significantly, a sizable number of those surveyed, about 30 percent, offered no opinion at all.
Public opinion also split along generational and occupational lines. The younger and more successful strata of Russian society was less concerned with NATO expansion than the elderly and those who work in military industries. But NATO expansion has already aggravated the suspicions of an older generation and of the military, which was already wary of free market changes and nostalgic for Soviet days.
Valentin A. Tarasov, a 63-year-old pensioner, said: "I think NATO affects the life of everybody in this country. I don't think America cares that much about us. We will have to pay back everything they give us now." While Moscow is not always representative of attitudes throughout Russia, the polls do not show enormous regional variation. Russians who live in small villages are the least likely to have an opinion.
"NATO doesn't affect my life," said Elizabeth V. Apakinfieve, a 22-year-old soft drink seller. "I don't even know what it is. I don't think people won't drink soda whatever happens in politics."
Russia's new power elite insists that NATO expansion will still have consequences.
"It is the elites who define routine defense and security policy," said Alexei Arbatov, a Member of Parliament who belongs to the liberal Yabloko faction. "It is not public opinion. That's important only during elections and referendums."
One possible casualty is the Start 2 treaty to slash long-range nuclear arms. Mr. Yeltsin has vowed to work for its ratification by the Communist-dominated Parliament, but top Kremlin officials said it was a difficult fight and that NATO expansion had not helped.
Still, Yeltsin officials said the Kremlin had no interest in trying to appease nationalists by undertaking military countermeasures, such as establishing bases in Belarus.
The Kremlin has, however, pursued deeper integration with Belarus to make NATO expansion easier to swallow by giving the impression that Russia is also strengthening its political influence with the remnants of the Soviet empire.
Many Russians are looking for a piece of the economic pie, not to recover Russia's great power status.
"To tell you the truth I cannot understand why NATO still exists?" said Aleksandr I. Chernetsky, 25, an official at one of Moscow's new commercial banks. "But the main thing I care about is my family. After that, it's business. Only then do I care about politics. As for our politicians, they only care about themselves."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Elizabeth V. Apakinfieve, a 22-year-old soft drink seller, above, says, "NATO doesn't affect my life." But Valentin A. Tarasov, a 63-year-old pensioner, takes the opposite view: "I think NATO affects the life of everybody in this country. I don't think America cares that much about us." (James Hill for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: N.H.L. PLAYOFFS;
Messier Says He'd Like to Stay

BYLINE: By JOE LAPOINTE 

DATELINE: RYE, N.Y., May 27

 BODY:
In his sixth year as a star performer for Madison Square Garden, Mark Messier has learned a little about the timetable of his corporate employer.
"With the way the Garden works, you have to be patient," said Messier, the captain of the Rangers, who will become an unrestricted free agent on July 1. "When your number's up, they'll take care of business." 
 And the word, number, applies to many things in the case of Messier, including age, goals and salary. After making $6 million as the highest-paid player on the team this season, Messier is free to try the free market to seek better wages. But his leverage fell with his limited production during the Stanley Cup playoffs.
In addition, Messier discussed loyalty today as the team formally broke up for the summer and the players cleaned out their lockers in Playland Ice Casino.
Messier hinted that he might accept a lower salary to continue dressing in a blue shirt alongside Wayne Gretzky, Brian Leetch and other players he admires. At least, he suggested, he will not aggressively market himself elsewhere.
"I don't even foresee that much negotiation going on with other teams," Messier said. "I don't know how much interest is out there. I haven't thought about that. I haven't even thought about playing anywhere else."
The Messier subject dominated the day. It was addressed by Neil Smith, the president and general manager, and Colin Campbell, the coach. Messier and the Rangers finished their surprising playoff run on Sunday in Philadelphia where the Flyers beat them by 4-2 to take the Eastern Conference finals in five games.
Although the Rangers ousted Florida and the Devils earlier in the playoffs, Messier scored only 3 goals in 15 games. He scored 36 in the regular season, but only 4 after Feb. 8.
Smith said he wants Messier to return for a seventh season as he approaches his 37th birthday. But Smith also used phrases like "economics of our situation" and "ceiling" and "use your budget wisely" and "trying to hold the line, fiscally" when discussing Messier.
"We'd love to have Mark back," Smith said. "We want him back. It is still a business at some point. You have to have a perspective of that." He said he would have loved to have kept Pat Verbeek after last season, but that he was out-bid for Verbeek's services by the Dallas Stars.
"I'm going to go into the summer process with the assumption we have Mark onside," Smith said.
Campbell used words such as "delicate" and "important" regarding the Messier question.
"Hopefully, for everybody, it's a happy ending," Campbell said. "How many dollars will it take to do this? It isn't going to be an easy thing. Lots of things will be difficult."
Campbell said Messier's reduced effectiveness in the post-season may have been a result of increased ice time due to injuries to other players. But, even before the injuries, Messier took a regular shift when teams were at full strength, occasionally double-shifted, worked most of the power plays and killed most of the penalties.
In order to make room for young players such as center Christian Dube, it might be necessary for players such as Messier to accept fewer minutes. This is easier said than done for Messier, an 18-year veteran.
"There always comes a day for any athlete and a time for an athlete to take a reduced load," Campbell said, adding that Messier did this until his minutes increased during the playoffs.
Messier seemed in high spirits today. He said he was fortunate to go through the playoffs without injury. He said the preseason World Cup did not wear him down and that he would like to play for Team Canada in the Olympics in Japan next winter when the league takes a midseason break for the international tournament.
He said money would not be a big issue in his negotiations with the Rangers because he has earned plenty and is satisfied to play on a year-to-year basis for the enjoyment of playing to win. He even joked that he would be willing to work on a game-to-game contract.
"Throw the crutches away and get out there," Messier said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mark Messier laughing with reporters yesterday as the Rangers cleaned out their lockers. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: An Active Regulator Will Step Down

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 27

 BODY:
Reed E. Hundt, who has presided over the most turbulent era in the Federal Communications Commission's 63-year history, announced today that he intended to step down as chairman as soon as a replacement was named.
In a letter to President Clinton, Mr. Hundt, a combative former communications litigator, said that it was time for others to assume the task of supervising the nation's fast-growing telecommunications sector, which now accounts for nearly one-sixth of the United States economy. 
 Mr. Hundt's three and a half years at the helm of the F.C.C. have indeed been eventful. The commission has supervised the beginning of a sweeping deregulation of the country's telephone system, issued the first licenses for advanced digital television broadcasting, tried to bring greater competition to cable television service, attempted to harness an explosion in wireless communications and negotiated a ground-breaking international telecommunications agreement.
Mr. Hundt, using a combination of his office's high public profile, the powerful sponsorship of the White House and shifting alliances in Congress, has been able to push through a number of carefully calibrated compromises on those matters that have satisfied the overall intent of lawmakers, while leaving many of the details to others.
He takes pride in the $23 billion he has brought into the Federal Treasury through auctions of rights to the wireless spectrum and in having trod, sometimes seemingly with glee, upon influential toes.
Mr. Hundt called the F.C.C. chairmanship "the most exhilarating and gratifying experience of my career," but said he was departing to spend more time with his wife and three school-age children.
"I don't feel there's anything that needed doing in the time I've been here that we didn't do," Mr. Hundt, 49, said in an interview in his office six blocks from the White House. "We've repeatedly been handed the ball with an opportunity to make the game-winning shot and I hope we put the ball in the net more times than not."
Working with a commission deeply rent along partisan and ideological lines, Mr. Hundt has made enemies in some of the most powerful and well-heeled precincts of American business -- local telephone monopolies, the broadcast industry, cable operators and newly rich cellular-telephone enterprises.
Mr. Hundt, to a greater degree than any F.C.C. chairman since Newton Minow more than 30 years ago, has seen his office as a public pulpit. He has traveled widely and given more than 160 speeches on the societal obligations of broadcasters and telephone companies.
Vice President Al Gore, Mr. Hundt's prep-school classmate and chief political sponsor, called the departing official "the best chairman in the history of the Federal Communications Commission."
"He has shepherded this country into the information age and presided over the implementation of some of the most complex and historic changes in our communications law ever passed," Mr. Gore said in an interview.
Mr. Hundt will be remembered for his assertive approach, particularly on matters relating to children's television programming and other presumed public-interest issues, such as his effort to keep liquor advertising off the airwaves.
Over the last five years, Congress has passed a number of epochal laws deregulating the nation's television, cable and telephone networks and delivered to the F.C.C. the thankless task of carrying them out.
The laws have contained a number of contradictory provisions, both protecting local telephone companies from rapid deregulation while mandating increased competition for local phone service. Congress has also demanded lower cable television rates but left to the regulatory agency the job of figuring out how to impose them.
Among the more difficult balancing acts Mr. Hundt has faced has been to carry out Congress's mandate in last year's telecommunications act, in which the commission was ordered to dismantle the complex structure of subsidies that have governed local and long-distance telephone rates for decades while creating a large fund to insure basic telephone service for low-income and rural customers.
But Mr. Hundt, whose byword has been to try to achieve the economically defensible within the confines of the politically possible, chose to take a relatively small step toward reducing the rates long-distance companies pay local phone companies for access to their networks. At the same time, he persuaded his fellow commissioners to establish a $2.3 billion fund to provide subsidized access to the Internet for schools, libraries and health clinics.
Mr. Hundt's often-difficult relations with the entities he regulates have been mirrored inside the walls of the F.C.C. headquarters.
Perhaps his toughest adversary has been a fellow commissioner, James H. Quello, a conservative Democrat who has served on the F.C.C. since 1974 and was its interim commissioner for nearly a year before Mr. Hundt was confirmed in November 1993.
Mr. Quello described his differences with Mr. Hundt as philosophical rather than political.
"He is very regulatory in his approach," Mr. Quello said in an interview today. "He has been responsible for the greatest case of F.C.C. regulatory micromanagement in the 23 years I've been here. I resist Government telling broadcasters how to run their stations in detail."
The chairman's most frequent industry sparring partner has been Edward O. Fritts, the president of the National Association of Broadcasters.
In a curt and dry-eyed valedictory, Mr. Fritts said, "While we haven't always agreed with Chairman Hundt, we have enjoyed his competitive spirit and the robust debate that he engendered by bringing a full marketplace of ideas to the table."
Consumer groups and children's advocates offered praise for Mr. Hundt, a graduate of Yale University and Yale Law School who practiced law at the Washington office of the Los Angeles firm Latham & Watkins before being named to the F.C.C. job.
"He fought for the underdog in a number of key areas," said Gene Kimmelman, co-director of the Washington office of Consumers Union. "He led a charge to bring down cable rates, he kept the lid on telephone rates and fought to wire schools to the Internet."
Mr. Hundt said he had few plans for the future, but indicated he would be active in supporting Mr. Gore's Presidential ambitions.
He said he would stay on the job until President Clinton named a successor and would then devote time to writing a book about telecommunications. Its working title: "You Say You Want a Revolution: How Communications Can Change the World."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Coca-Cola Enterprises To Buy 2 Big Bottlers
The world's largest bottling company, Coca-Cola Enterprises, plans to get even larger. It agreed to acquire bottling companies from the Coca-Cola Company in two of its strongest markets -- Canada and metropolitan New York -- for $1.01 billion in cash plus $650 million in assumed debt. 
 Analysts said the acquisition would let Coca-Cola Enterprises, which is 44 percent owned by the Coca-Cola Company, cut costs, control its distribution and market its products more efficiently in those areas. [Page D2.]
 
Consumer Confidence Rises
Consumer confidence rose to a 28-year high in May, the Conference Board said. But a separate report showed that home resales fell more than expected in April as borrowing costs continued to creep up. [D2.]
 
30-Year Bond Yield Above 7%
Treasury prices fell, pushing the yield on the benchmark 30-year bond to 7.02 percent from 6.98, marking the first time in nearly a month that it crossed the psychologically important 7 percent threshold. [D17.]
 
Stocks advanced to new highs in a rally led by computer issues. The Dow industrials rose 37.50 points, to a record 7,383.41, as I.B.M. set an all-time high and AT&T rose on word of its talks with SBC Communications. [D8.]
 
News Corp. in Satellite Swap Plan
The News Corporation has agreed in principle to hand over some satellite assets in return for a minority stake in Primestar Partners, a satellite broadcasting service that is headed by Time Warner and Tele-Communications. [D5.]
 
Toy Giants Walk Away From Deal
Sega Enterprises, the video game maker, and Bandai, the toy giant, abruptly called off their planned $1 billion merger. The collapse of the agreement -- which would have linked Bandai's Mighty Morphin Power Rangers and Sega's Sonic the Hedgehog -- came after Bandai middle managers practically rose up in revolt, illustrating the importance of consensus at Japanese companies. [D7.]
 
High Court Rebuffs Ernst & Young
The Supreme Court turned down an appeal by Ernst & Young, the accounting firm, of a ruling that entitled partners who did not exercise management responsibility to enjoy the legal protections that Federal labor and anti-discrimination laws give to ordinary employees. [A18.]
 
Nissan Swings Back to Profit
Nissan, Japan's second-largest auto maker, reported its first annual profit in five years, helped by the yen's weakness, cost reductions and stronger sales in Mexico. [D7.]
 
Interpublic Invests in Internet
Interpublic is expanding its presence in the realm of on-line advertising by investing in Tripod, an interactive marketing and media company that focuses primarily on consumers in their 20's. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D5.]
 
Smith Barney Official Suspended
Smith Barney has suspended Robert Maloney, the director for its Northeast region, pending an investigation of his role in soliciting campaign contributions, according to people familiar with the matter. [D8.]
 
Bank Buys Historic Wall St. Building
Credit Suisse First Boston has bought 55 Wall Street in Manhattan for $21 million, executives involved in the deal said, and plans to develop it into a ballroom and possibly apartments or a hotel. Four months ago Donald J. Trump decided not to buy the historic building. [B6.]

HEADLINE: Supreme Court Roundup;
Court Agrees to Decide if U.S. Customs Can Seize All Undeclared Money

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 27

 BODY:
The Supreme Court agreed today to decide whether the Government can constitutionally seize all the money that someone has tried to take out of the country without first filing the required report with the Customs Service.
A Federal law, intended to trace the money laundering of drug proceeds and other illegal transactions, forbids the export of more than $10,000 in undeclared cash. The penalties for failing to file a report are a fine of up to $250,000, a prison sentence of up to five years and forfeiture of all the money involved in the offense. 
 The Federal appeals court in California refused last year to enforce the forfeiture provision, ruling that it violated the Eighth Amendment's prohibition against "excessive fines."
That decision affirmed a 1995 ruling by the Federal District Court in Los Angeles, which held that forfeiture of the $357,144 in cash that a man had tried to smuggle to Syria through the Los Angeles International Airport would be "grossly disproportionate" to the offense and therefore unconstitutional. The court ordered a forfeiture of only $15,000.
In upholding that decision, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in effect declared the currency-reporting law unconstitutional. The appeals court suggested that no forfeiture at all should be permitted. It noted that a violation of the reporting law "merely deprives the Government of information, in contrast to violations of the law against smuggling undeclared items into the country." Jewelry or other undeclared items were subject to forfeiture, the court said, because their actual possession constituted the offense.
The Government appealed to the Supreme Court, warning the Justices that unless the Ninth Circuit's decision was overturned, the nine Western states within its jurisdiction "could readily become a haven for drug dealers, money launderers and tax evaders intent on conducting non-traceable currency transactions." In the year since the decision, there has been "a sharp and precipitous drop" in seizures of unreported currency, the appeal said.
"The economic power of much criminal activity depends on money laundering that secretly moves cash into and out of the country," the Government's appeal said, adding that "tracing the flow of currency plays a vital role in attacking global criminal activity."
The appeal noted that the Federal appeals court in New York had rejected a similar constitutional challenge to the forfeiture provision.
The Government said that forfeitures under the law were "not excessively punitive" because they were "perfectly calibrated to the seriousness of the offense." In other words, smuggling large amounts of money results in large forfeitures.
There was no proof of money laundering or other underlying criminal activity in the case at hand, United States v. Bajakajian, No. 96-1487. The defendant, Hosep K. Bajakajian, testified that he was taking the money, which was hidden in his luggage, to pay debts to friends and relatives in Syria and Cyprus.
There were also these other developments at the Court today:
 
Land Use
The Court ruled, 9 to 0, that a landowner whose property had been declared ineligible for development for environmental reasons could try to establish in court that the restriction was an unconstitutional taking of property without compensation.
The decision vacated a ruling by the Ninth Circuit, which last year dismissed the lawsuit against the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency as not ripe because the landowner had not tried to find a market for the development rights she had been awarded when she lost her right to build a home on her residential lot in the Lake Tahoe watershed.
Like a growing number of other land use agencies, the Lake Tahoe agency assigns to owners of restricted property a right to build elsewhere, which they may use themselves or sell to an owner of property that is appropriately zoned for development. The buyer would then be entitled to build a bigger structure than would otherwise be permitted.
In a majority opinion by Justice David H. Souter, the Court said today that although the market value of the development rights at issue was undetermined, that was a matter on which the trial court could take evidence and was not a reason to deprive the landowner, Bernadine Suitum, of her day in court.
Although property rights advocates hailed the decision, Suitum v. Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, No. 96-243, as a victory, the Court's essentially procedural ruling sidestepped the basic question in the case: what role the existence of the development rights should play analyzing whether the zoning restriction was an unconstitutional taking.
Property rights groups supporting Mrs. Suitum had urged the Court to rule that the rights were irrelevant, and that a prohibition on development on one piece of land was a taking regardless of the theoretical right to build elsewhere.
 
Partners or Not
Without comment, the Court turned down an appeal by the Ernst & Young accounting firm of a ruling that entitled members of the firm who had the title of "partner" but who did not exercise management responsibility to enjoy the legal protections that various Federal labor and anti-discrimination laws give to employees. Partners, as co-owners of their businesses, do not get the legal protections of employees.
A group of large law firms joined Ernst & Young in its appeal of the 1996 ruling by the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, in Cincinnati, which permitted an age discrimination suit by a 46-year-old accountant who had been dismissed from the partnership. The appeals court analyzed the structure of the firm and decided that the great majority of its 2,000 partners were functionally no better off than ordinary employees.
The case, Ernst & Young v. Simpson, No. 96-1553, has drawn intense interest in the legal and accounting professions. The appeal said it was "wholly inappropriate" for a court to "second-guess the business judgment" of people who sign partnership agreements.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 596 words

HEADLINE: European Disunion

 BODY:
Just as Americans are getting accustomed to the idea that Asia is the new crucible for American economic and political interests, Europe commands attention again. The confluence of two great shifts now planned in Europe, the eastward expansion of NATO and the adoption of a common currency, could change the Continent in ways that may undermine European stability in the decades ahead.
That danger was not much in evidence yesterday as President Clinton and NATO's European leaders welcomed President Boris Yeltsin to Paris to sign an accord that will govern Russia's relationship with the alliance. This agreement, the leaders declared, will speed the advent of a democratic, undivided Europe. It may also make it easier to sell NATO expansion to a wary Senate, which has the final say. Mr. Yeltsin sweetened the deal by announcing that Russian nuclear missiles would no longer be aimed at Europe, an easily reversible step Moscow took some years ago for American targets. 
 But the pleasantries in Paris were a distraction. The common denominator of NATO expansion and a single European currency is the division of Europe into two camps, the prosperous democracies and those not so strong or fortunate. NATO expansion would give that division political and military form, and the common currency would reinforce it economically. Russia, on all counts, would be left squarely in the excluded camp.
That may not seem worrisome from the many standards of success outlined by Mr. Clinton and the other architects of change. Adoption of a common currency will indeed enhance commerce among the countries allowed to use it and give that group greater international financial clout. The inclusion of Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic in NATO, the probable first phase of expansion, would help them feel secure and part of Western Europe. Russia will have a consultative role in NATO.
But the broader picture is less reassuring. Just as the security of Europe after World War II depended on the transformation of Germany into a peaceful, democratic nation, the future stability of Europe now rests heavily on the consolidation of democracy and free markets in Russia.
Neither NATO growth nor a common currency will make it easier for Russia to see itself as part of Europe. Countries like Italy fear second-class status if they do not qualify for the common currency. Eligibility is limited to members of the European Union, and, among other things, requires budget deficits so low that even Germany and France may have a hard time qualifying. Russia is ineligible now, and likely to remain so for years.
NATO expansion at its core is an effort to erect a Western security zone in Eastern Europe while Russia is too weak to protest. That would be appropriate if Russia were rearming and becoming belligerent, and might prove a necessary step in the future, but it seems ill advised while Russia is trying with some pain to become a democracy.
What Russia most needs today is help in turning around its anemic economy. Historians may someday look back at yesterday's meeting and wonder why the leaders of the United States and European democracies seemed so intent on expanding NATO in 1997 instead of working more closely with Mr. Yeltsin to solve Russia's economic problems. Russia needs a new tax system much more than it needs a new relationship with NATO. There is nothing wrong with tinkering with the shape of Europe now that the Soviet Union is gone, provided the adjustments are more likely to bring the Continent together rather than drive it apart.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section A; Page 11; Column 1; Foreign Desk 

LENGTH: 950 words

HEADLINE: DECISION FOR NATO: WHITE HOUSE MEMO;
Clinton Plays to History, With Renewed Panache

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: PARIS, May 27

 BODY:
Bill Clinton was chatting animatedly with Prime Minister Tony Blair of Britain under the glittering crystal chandeliers of Elysee Palace today when his turn came to sign the new accord between Russia and NATO.
He did so quickly, without any of his usual self-conscious flourishes. Yet the moment carried the sense of real occasion that so often eludes Mr. Clinton at home. 
 For foreign policy gives Mr. Clinton a broader canvas these days when the constraints of budget balancing and a Republican Congress have reduced him to small-scale announcements and sheer bully pulpitry in domestic policy. Also, the arena is far removed from the controversies that dog him.
Even with the Supreme Court ruling on the Paula Jones case reaching Paris to cast a shadow, Mr. Clinton could portray the reconciliation he had helped nurture between two cold-war adversaries as something historic.
President Jacques Chirac of France called the accord the elimination "of the last remnants of the cold war." President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia said, "We are determining the face of the future European environment." And Mr. Clinton said, "We look toward a new century with a new Russia and a new NATO working in a new Europe of unlimited possibility."
Four years ago Mr. Clinton would not have shown as much ease in this role as the catalyst for the expansion of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. He came into office promising to "focus like a laser" on the economy, to be the domestic policy President that George Bush had not been. His attention to foreign affairs was episodic, his approach uncertain.
But much has changed since Mr. Clinton's early days in office. America's economic expansion and low unemployment rates are now the envy of Europe. Mr. Clinton's signature politics of unapologetic moderation has found something of an imitator in Mr. Blair.
The idea of expanding NATO is not new for Mr. Clinton. He took his first steps in 1994 at a NATO summit meeting in Brussels, where he helped begin the Partnership for Peace program, which granted the new Eastern European democracies a new relationship to NATO.
It was well short of the full membership and security guarantees that Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic wanted. But Mr. Clinton promised that this would come with time.
But the program was overshadowed by finger-pointing among the NATO allies over the war in Bosnia. Neither the United States nor any Western European country seemed willing to take decisive action to stop the carnage. The question in the air was not about NATO's expanding its reach, but whether NATO had lost its bearings.
By the summer of 1995, as Bosnian Serb troops overran so-called safe areas of the United Nations, NATO's very future seemed at stake. "We and our allies were on the verge of losing faith in the institutions we had created," an Administration official recalled.
With humiliation in Europe looming and a re-election campaign before him, Mr. Clinton acted with a decisiveness he had not shown before. The United States rallied its allies to carry out air strikes against the Bosnian Serbs, and undertook the diplomacy in Dayton, Ohio, that brought a peace agreement.
The fruits of those actions were on display today as leader after leader gathered here mentioned Bosnia not as a crisis for the Western military alliance, but as one of its successes, noting that Russia had joined NATO in policing the peace in Bosnia.
Mr. Clinton has seen other foreign policy vindications as well.
He strode into the Northern Ireland conflict and gave a world platform to Gerry Adams, the president of Sinn Fein, the political wing of the Irish Republican Army, several years ago. His act helped prod peace negotiations, which later collapsed when the I.R.A. stopped honoring a cease-fire. But now Mr. Blair's election raises hopes that the talks can begin again.
Mr. Clinton also took criticism for his decision to stick with Mr. Yeltsin through thick and thin, with some experts saying he was linking himself too closely to just one ailing Russian leader. But Administration officials say their strong relationship paved the way for the Russian President to cast aside his strong doubts and sign the pact with NATO today.
And so Mr. Clinton was a confident member of the club of presidents and prime ministers today. He flashed a big grin at Mr. Blair and gave him a broad thumbs-up as the new British leader finished speaking at the ceremony.
The President went along gamely when Mr. Yeltsin, during a group photo session, grasped Mr. Clinton's hand on one side and Mr. Chirac's on the other, pulling them into the air in a victory gesture.
Mr. Clinton's press secretary, Michael D. McCurry, was left to fend reporters' questions about the Supreme Court ruling. ("I believe that the opinion appears to have distracted all of you, but the President continued to conduct the nation's business today," Mr. McCurry said.)
Of course, history will determine if the expansion of NATO will turn out to be as important as the Marshall Plan after World War II.
"The last time Europe was this peaceful is when the Roman Empire existed," said Dan Plesch, director of the British American Security Information Council, a research organization on military issues. "How do you consolidate that? You do it by economic development and much more ambitious arms control."
Pointing to the unrest in Albania, he said: "Albania is a member of the Partnership for Peace and what good did it do it? It fell apart because of economic collapse."
But for Mr. Clinton, the course is clear. "NATO will promote greater stability in all of Europe, including Russia," he said confidently today.

HEADLINE: True Equality Comes When Race Isn't a Factor;
A Loss of Ideas

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Anthony Lewis (column, May 23) laments the loss of diversity in higher education, and I agree completely. In 1993 I went to Harvard Law School because it was Harvard and because it had more black law students than any other school except Howard University Law School and maybe the law school at Georgetown University. 
 Why did that matter? Backup. As an undergraduate at Cornell, I knew that I would be expected to provide the (mythical) "black perspective" on every point and that it would be the same at Harvard.
The only difference is that I would have to have better arguments. I was not, however, prepared for how narrow the debate could truly become if students of differing backgrounds didn't speak up.
For instance, to some students, who will one day be mayors, judges and law professors, police are guardians to be trusted. To others, they are overseers who must be controlled with rigid procedural rules.
The same kind of vast perspective differences exist in tax policy, banking regulation and family law. The removal of some applicants from law school will remove ideas. So in addition to the tremendous setback the affirmative action rollback has dealt to the economic prosperity of people of color, this rollback will seriously injure the quality of legal education.
 
EULAS BOYD JR.
New York, May 23, 1997

HEADLINE: Trump's Bank Making Deal He Declined

BYLINE: By DAVID M. HALBFINGER 

 BODY:
Four months after Donald J. Trump walked away from buying 55 Wall Street, a subsidiary of the investment bank that was to have financed his purchase of the historic building has bought it and is discussing developing a ballroom and possibly a luxury hotel or apartments.
The 155-year old building, originally built as the Merchants Exchange and later used as New York's customs house and headquarters of National City Bank, forerunner of Citibank, was purchased by a subsidiary of Credit Suisse First Boston for $21.15 million, executives involved in the deal said. 
 The Cipriani restaurant family, owners of Harry Cipriani in the Sherry-Netherland Hotel and Harry's Bar in Venice, are now negotiating for partial ownership and plan to open a banquet hall and ballroom in the building's rotunda hall. The Ciprianis have also approached Credit Suisse about developing a luxury hotel on the upper floors, an investment banker there said.
On Friday, the Credit Suisse First Boston subsidiary completed the purchase of the building from an American subsidiary of Kajima Corporation, one of Japan's largest construction companies, which took a heavy loss on the sale. Kajima had paid $69.1 million in 1990, but several major tenants left soon thereafter.
Brian R. Harris, head of the commercial trading desk at Credit Suisse First Boston, said it was possible that 55 Wall Street's nine stories could remain office space, but that a conversion to apartments or to a hotel was more likely. "It's a question of tolerance for risk," he said. "Apartments would be a safe deal, especially given the tax breaks for downtown development."
A residential development would create about 150 apartments, mainly studios and one-bedrooms, and a dozen duplexes, Mr. Harris said. Among those interested are Rockrose Development and Steven Witkoff, a frequent Credit Suisse First Boston partner.
As a hotel, the building could accommodate only about 200 rooms, he said, meaning a high construction cost per room. As a result, "it would have to be a fairly high-end hotel," Mr. Harris said. "We're talking to several high-end, niche players." Among them is Orient-Express Hotels, which owns the Cipriani Hotel in Venice and is being represented by the Cipriani family, he said.
At a minimum, Mr. Harris said, the Ciprianis will be a tenant of the ornate rotunda, a 70-foot-high, domed space that has been used several times in recent films.
The sale closing ended several months of dealmaking marked by changing players, perplexing delays, and a brief bout of litigation. Last September, according to court documents, Richard Fischbein, a prominent real estate lawyer, negotiated a contract for a client, the House of Blues chain of live music clubs, to buy 55 Wall Street for $23 million.
When House of Blues canceled the deal, Mr. Fischbein turned to another client, Mr. Trump, to whom he assigned the purchase agreement. With closing set for December, Mr. Trump, who had the finacial backing of Credit Suisse, praised the building as "one of the most magnificent structures in the world."
By December, however, Mr. Trump's opinion of the building appeared to have soured. After the closing was postponed twice, according to court documents, Mr. Trump -- who had put down a $1 million deposit -- demanded that Kajima, represented by Commercial Development International/East, lower the price to $18 million. Mr. Trump said he had learned that, after he had signed the purchase contract, water had caused $5 million worth of damage to the building's 161-year-old wood pilings.
Kajima sued in February to keep Mr. Trump's deposit, but under a settlement the details of which have been closely guarded, Mr. Trump was "made whole," people familiar with the terms said.
Mr. Trump, who is negotiating with several hotel chains to fill the base of his own 72-story tower at 40 Wall Street, praised the sale yesterday. "I think First Boston will do a good job," he said. "The most important element in this transaction, to me, was to have someone of quality directly across the street from 40 Wall."

GRAPHIC: Photo: 55 Wall Street, which may now be developed to include a ballroom, a luxury hotel, and apartments, was sold at a loss. (*Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Lawmakers Question Loosening of Technology Export Controls

BYLINE: By JEFF GERTH 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 27

 BODY:
The chairman of the House Judiciary Committee has asked the Justice Department to look at whether there are any links between Asian donors to the Democratic Party and the Clinton Administration's loosening of export controls on sensitive American technology, which has benefited China.
In a letter last week to Attorney General Janet Reno, two Republican members of Congress cited four "questionable decisions" by the Administration, made over the objections of some national security officials, to approve the sale of American technology to China. 
 They include the 1994 sale of machine equipment to a Chinese aviation company, some of which was diverted to a military plant; the sale of 46 supercomputers over the last 18 months, giving the Chinese possibly more supercomputing capacity than the entire Department of Defense; a decision to shift jurisdiction over sales of certain jet engine technology, and the 1994 sale of sophisticated telecommunications equipment.
The letter sent by Representatives Henry J. Hyde of Illinois, the Judiciary Committee chairman, and Tillie K. Fowler, of Florida, is part of an effort by some Congressional Republicans to combine two contentious issues: investigations into questionable Asian donations and concerns about the Administration's policies on exports to China. Part of the Justice Department investigation into possible campaign finance violations includes evidence that Chinese officials talked about an illicit plan to funnel money to American politicians.
The letter offers no evidence that the donations and policy decisions are linked, but asks the Department of Justice to see "whether improper outside influence was brought to bear on Administration officials -- including the President -- and if that influence has resulted in decisions and policies that have liberalized the transfer of defense-related technologies."
Today, Lanny J. Davis, White House special counsel, said that "the President's decisions on trade and export control issues were made on the merits and not because of campaign contributions."
The Administration has explained its relaxed controls on sales abroad of sensitive American technology as part of its plan to make exports an engine of economic growth. This delighted technology company executives, some of whom increased their financial support for the Democrats, and benefited the Chinese, who were eager to acquire sophisticated technology made in the West.
Mr. Hyde and Ms. Fowler, like many Pentagon and intelligence officials, criticized the policy as providing a significant increase in China's military capabilities. The lawmakers focused some of their concern on the Commerce Department, which serves as the promotional vehicle for the Administration's export strategy as well as the enforcement arm for sales abroad.
For example, the 1994 sale to the Chinese of machine equipment, manufactured by McDonnell Douglas, was approved by the Commerce Department despite warnings by Pentagon officials that it might be diverted to military use. The sale and subsequent diversion are under review by a Federal grand jury here, but the letter asked that this matter also "receive full scrutiny in the context of the Justice Department's investigation of campaign finance improprieties."

HEADLINE: Heartland Emerges as Leader in Global Trade

SERIES: ECONOMIC PULSE: The Mountains and Plains

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: DENVER, May 27

 BODY:
Though ports historically have tied foreign trade to seacoasts, this interior city, 1,000 miles from the sea, will play host next month to world leaders at their annual economic summit meeting. The choice of Denver as the gathering place is symbolic of how America's heartland is becoming a major player in the world economy, leading the country in foreign trade growth and drawing investment from around the globe.
Of the 10 states that recorded the fastest rates of export growth from 1988 to 1996, seven were landlocked Western states. Arizona, the region's star performer, exports more per capita than New York. Colorado, once viewed as cow country, recently saw the value of its manufactured exports surpass that of its farm and ranch production. 
 Traditional heartland industries like farming and mining are rapidly growing and joining the world economy. With exports of processed food soaring, Plains farmers are riding a boom that is forecast to double American food exports over 15 years, to $80 billion in 2005.
At the same time, technological advances have allowed new industries, like financial services, computer services and telecommunications, to flourish in the world economy from bases in the nation's interior.
"In terms of integrating into world markets, the heartland has played catch-up at a very fast clip in the 1990's," said Mark Drabenstott, vice president for research at the Federal Reserve Bank in Kansas City, Mo.
But economic growth has brought new constraints, as state officials struggle with labor and housing shortages and with the historic barrier to growth in the West: water.
Still, statistics bear out a remarkable economic surge for almost all the states in the mountain time zone, a geographically disparate area that includes all or part of Arizona, Colorado, Idaho, Kansas, Montana, Nebraska, New Mexico, North Dakota, South Dakota, Utah and Wyoming.
In 1988, these 11 states exported, on a per capita basis, at half the national average of $1,246. By last year, the region's average had risen to two-thirds the nation's new average, $2,348. The increase came during a period when the nation doubled its total exports, to $623 billion in 1996.
In Idaho, for example, 75 percent of the 100 million bushels of wheat grown annually goes overseas.
In Nebraska, Gov. Ben Nelson has become a Midwestern Marco Polo. Since 1991, he has led state trade missions to Japan and China three times each, to Taiwan four times and to Hong Kong five times. In February, he ventured south, taking Nebraska exporters to Argentina and Brazil.
"We just opened offices in Shanghai and Porto Alegre, Brazil," Tony Raimondo said from Columbus, Neb., where he runs the Behlen Manufacturing Company. Behlen, which makes metal storage silos and grain dryers, has tripled exports since 1990, Mr. Raimondo said.
In Colorado next month, the region's new international character will be clearly visible to foreign participants at the "Denver Summit of the Eight" -- Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Italy, Japan and Russia.
Japan is the largest trading partner of Utah, Colorado, Nebraska and Kansas. Britain accounts for a major part of the $400 million invested in Colorado by 200 companies from 22 foreign countries. One of the state's ski areas is owned by a Japanese company, another by a Canadian company and a third by a Brazilian company.
Prime Minister Jean Chretien of Canada, which is Colorado's second-largest trading partner, will be able to fly to the meeting here directly -- 11 flights leave Canada daily for Denver's new $5 billion airport. Boris Yeltsin's advisers will undoubtedly tell him that from Denver, the center of the American gold mining industry, Colorado companies mine gold in Tajikistan, Uzbekistan and the Russian Far East.
"Mining, aerospace, telecommunications -- they are all strong in Denver, and they are all investing in Russia," said Deborah Anne Palmieri, president of the Russian-American Chamber of Commerce, a national business group based in Denver since 1993.
The increasingly international flavor of the economy has also affected the region's traditional mainstay, agriculture. Today, Plains states ship record amounts of pasta, boxed steaks and frozen chicken to a range of new markets: Russia, Latin America and the Far East.
"Russia is by far our biggest volume market; we're selling everything from hot dogs to top-of-the-line T-bone steaks," Scott Sanem said by telephone from Moscow, where he recently opened a regional sales office for Conagra Refrigerated Foods of Greeley, Colo. "We have more than doubled sales over the last year."
In parts of the region, economic growth has triggered significant growth in jobs and population. Since 1990, the nation's fastest population growth, 18 percent, has occurred in the "Mountain" region: Arizona, Colorado, Idaho, Montana, Nevada, New Mexico, Utah and Wyoming.
But in the Plains states -- Kansas, Nebraska and the Dakotas -- economic growth is causing severe labor shortages. In general, these states appeal less to migrants than Colorado, say, because of the cold weather and the relative lack of recreational and cultural opportunities. And in recent years, Nebraska, South Dakota and North Dakota have recorded the nation's lowest unemployment rates, sometimes as low as 2 percent.
"Even though we have economies doing just as well, people don't think of moving to Iowa, Nebraska, North Dakota," said Ernest Goss, who edits a newsletter on the region's economy at Creighton University in Omaha.
In Colorado, which has absorbed 400,000 newcomers since 1990, rising real estate prices present other constraints.
Since 1990 in greater Denver, home sale prices and rents have increased by 50 percent, while wages have risen by 29 percent. Today, Denver is the sixth most expensive housing market in the nation.
As subdivisions have sprawled across what once was farmland, highway traffic has slowed to a crawl during rush hours in Denver, Phoenix and Salt Lake City. And suburbanites in faux leather cowboy boots may run up against the age-old shortage of water in the West.
"All these people coming in do not realize how vulnerable the region is for water," said William Stockton, an investment fund manager who grew up on a New Mexico ranch. "The newcomers have no notion of how bad things can get."
In part because of the shortage of water, the combined population of the 11 states in the region is only 18 million, less than the population of New York State.
 
Fewer Barriers In a Smaller World
Technological change has been the key to the growth of internationally competitive industries in the Rocky Mountain states and the Southwest.
"Back in the 30's and 40's, manufacturing for export would have been unthinkable in Arizona because of the low value-to-weight ratio," said John Lucking, a Phoenix economist. "Today, you can move computer chips economically by putting them on an airplane. You can't do that with diesel engines."
And so with manufacturing plants opening in the last two years in Arizona, New Mexico and Utah, the United States has regained the world lead in semiconductor production.
Advanced communications have also cut financial services from their traditional anchors on the coasts.
Helen Hayes, who manages two international mutual funds for Janus, said that being based in Denver was not an obstacle for the company. "With fax, video and Internet connections, it is becoming less important where you are located," she said. The company's two funds, Worldwide and Overseas, have a combined value of $10 billion. "Denver is now a stop on almost every road show for companies trying to raise capital," Ms. Hayes said.
Daniel W. Yohannes, an Ethiopian immigrant who is chief executive officer of Colorado National Bank, the state's largest, said, "We have tripled our foreign exchange operations in the last three years."
At the same time, high technology -- telephone, cable television, computers and computer services -- has become a pillar of the Colorado economy. Backed by strong research universities, high-technology employment in the Denver area has grown by about one-third in the 1990's, to about 110,000 people today, roughly the same number as in the Boston area. And according to a recent survey, 57 percent of the 3,000 high-technology companies in Colorado earn at least 10 percent of their revenue from foreign trade.
The US West telephone company, which is based in Denver, now operates cellular or cable telephone companies in Argentina, Belgium, Britain, the Czech Republic, Hungary, India, Indonesia, Malaysia, the Netherlands, Poland, Russia, Singapore and Slovakia.
At Professional Travel Corporation, Denver's largest agency specializing in corporate travel, Ed Adams has watched international air ticket sales jump to 10 percent of his billings today, from 2 percent in 1990.
"It's creeping up each quarter as the companies start to expand more and more overseas," Mr. Adams said, adding that for a coastal city the figure would be about 20 percent.
Foreign travel from Denver is expensive because even after two years of operation, the city's airport has no direct flights to Germany or Japan. But in a sign that Denver's air isolation is breaking down, Korean Air inaugurated a three-times-a-week flight on Friday from here to Seoul.
With these changes, one is just as likely to hear a Russian accent on the street in downtown Denver as to see a cowboy hat.
"When I came here five years ago, Denver seemed kind of like a small town, very slow, very country," said Mikhail L. Andelman, one of an estimated 10,000 Russian emigres living here. The director of American operations for Interfax, a Russian news agency, Mr. Andelman said from his Denver office, "Now it is more international."
 
More Jobs, More People
The region's rapid integration with the world economy is taking place as much of the area is still riding a larger economic boom that started in the early 1990's.
Although job growth is slowing slightly, it is expected to be 5 percent this year in Arizona and Utah and almost 4 percent in Colorado -- the region's three largest economies. Arizona has been adding 100,000 jobs a year since 1994.
Since 1990, Colorado's population has swelled to almost four million. In that time, Colorado has had 11 of the 35 fastest-growing counties in the nation, including the top four -- Douglas, Elbert, Park and Custer.
Virtually a city-state, greater Denver now has two million people, slightly less than Nebraska and Wyoming combined. Some in Colorado fear that state officials are taking growth for granted.
"Economic activity is easy to turn off but hard to turn back on," said Ross Boyle, an economic development consultant based in Vail. "Much of Colorado's population growth and job growth can be attributed to the problems in California. That outflow is now subsiding."
Historically, Colorado, like much of the Rocky Mountain West, has endured booms and busts -- gold in the 1860's, silver in the 1880's, military spending during World War II, and oil and gas in the 1970's.
Now, construction may be vulnerable -- the state has one-third more construction jobs than the national average. Last year, greater Denver's commercial real estate sales hit $855 million, double the level of five years ago.
"Construction is more important to us than nationally," acknowledged Nancy J. McCallin, a state economist here. "But we didn't build in Colorado for 10 years. We may just be in a different building cycle."
Others say that as Colorado's economy has diversified, it has become crash-proof. For example, Denver, once addicted to oil and gas, now depends on the energy sector for less than 1 percent of its jobs.
In a sign of the times, Denver's once exclusive Petroleum Club was rebaptized last fall with a more generic name: Top of the Rockies. During the international economic meeting June 20 to June 22, this club, on the top floor of a 37-story downtown high-rise, will be a gathering place for finance and foreign ministers.
The new participation in world markets contributes to the region's economic diversity and stability.
"There used to be an advantage to being on the coast," Mr. Goss, the economist, said from Omaha, about 175 miles from the nation's geographic center. "But technology has made distances much shorter. We are not talking about ships, we are talking about financial services."

HEADLINE: Metro Business;
Mid-Atlantic Region Gains in Optimism

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
Residents of the Middle Atlantic region that includes New York and New Jersey are becoming increasingly convinced that better economic times are on the way, a nonprofit business group said yesterday. But the region still lags far behind the rest of the nation in overall optimism. 
 The Conference Board, which is based in New York, said that the region's three-month average index of consumer confidence in March, April and May is up 56.1 percent compared with the same three-month average last year, and that the index of future expectations rose nearly 35 percent. In both categories, those increases were the sharpest of any of the nine national regions tracked by the group.
Nationally, the Conference Board's consumer confidence index reached a 28-year high of 127.1 in May. By contrast, the confidence level in the Middle Atlantic region, which also includes Pennsylvania, stood at only 85.7. The index was set at 100 in 1985. KIRK JOHNSON

HEADLINE: The Hamptons Bell Rings for Round 1

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER MASON 

DATELINE: EAST HAMPTON, N.Y., May 25

 BODY:
As a convoy of gleaming Range Rovers, Mercedes-Benzes and Bentleys inched along Main Street on the Friday before Memorial Day, heralding that inexorable rite of summer -- the lemminglike dash by prosperous New Yorkers to their overpriced East End rentals -- Jack Kehoe, a local sign painter, heaved a deep sigh.
"I got three hours' sleep last night," he said from a ladder propped against Cynthia Rowley's boutique-in-progress, as he added a final lick of black paint to the ultimate letter, "y." Mr. Kehoe had had only 48 hours to produce signs for six new stores, after they were approved by the town's exacting design review board. Inside her store, Ms. Rowley, the fashion designer, appeared similarly dazed, awaiting the arrival of mannequins. "We got our lease just 20 days ago," she said. 
 A chilly spring and an earlier-than-usual Memorial Day weekend conspired to send summerers, and those who bill them, into a frenzy. "Judgment Day was never like this," said David Seeler, the owner of the Bayberry Nursery in Amagansett, who had made 49 house calls in two days to insure that gardens were looking sufficiently vernal.
At Sotheby's International Realty in East Hampton, John Golden, a broker, bemoaned the dearth of houses for rent in the $350,000-for-the-season price range. Sotheby's had just five. "I only wish I had more $10 million houses to rent," Mr. Golden said. "We can't get enough of them." (The commission on rentals is 10 percent.)
Mr. Golden told a cautionary tale of a tenant who tried making do with a $150,000 rental. The man called on his first Thursday evening in the home to report that he was having trouble locating the switch for the pool heater.
"I hated to have to break the news to him," Mr. Golden said. "The house didn't come with a heated pool."
In what passes for economy class in the Hamptons -- the $15,000-to-$30,000-a-month range -- there seemed to be a rental surplus. "We're getting around 100 desperate calls a day from people who haven't yet rented their houses for the summer," Mr. Golden said. "They're telling us to lower the price or just accept any offers we can get. All of the major deals were done by the end of January."
A rent of $350,000 for a 90-day period works out to $3,888 a day, or $162 an hour, roughly the price of a session with a psychiatrist. And judging by the hurl of insults exchanged in the parking lot at Lynch's garden supply store in Southampton on Saturday afternoon, therapy might have been the wiser investment.
"I thought they were going to duke it out, they were screaming so loud," said Patrick West, who directs traffic at Lynch's busy parking lot, as he watched two New Yorkers trying to beat each other to the only parking space.
"It's wild how aggressive New Yorkers can be when you consider they probably think they're here to relax," said Elizabeth Yastrzmski, a landscape designer, as she dragged a red cart laden with plants through the parking lot at Lynch's. "It drives me crazy when they steal all the great-looking plants from your cart when you turn your back."
At Mecox Gardens, an elegant garden store on Route 27 in Southampton, the scene was positively serene by comparison. "Our customers are not the do-it-yourself crowd," explained the owner, Mac Hoak, whose ubiquitous green-and-white trucks deliver instant gardens to those who delight in nature but find themselves uninspired by the prospect of digging in all that dirt.
Chris Becker, a local landscape architect, explained that the urge to get plants in the ground this time of year has less to do with any Druidic cycle than with a conveniently timed holiday. "I find that a major part of my job is counseling my clients and reassuring them that their plants are just as big and healthy as other people's," he said.
Those who rent their ground turn to people like Kristen Zimberg, a florist, who said her clients pay her an average of $1,000 a weekend to fill their houses with fresh blooms.
The interior designer Noel Jeffrey, who created the first in a series of garden-room displays for Mecox Gardens, spoke of the difficulty of corralling local tradesmen during the summer season. Even the ostensibly mundane task of hiring a window washer, he said, can be as difficult as getting an appointment with a top surgeon. "I called in January for Memorial Day, but they told me they were already booked," he said. "So we'll have to wait until June."
Ben Krupinski, contractor to the stars and a local legend, who labored on Martha Stewart's house on Lily Pond Lane in East Hampton, seeks refuge at this time of year in one of his two boats at the East Hampton Point Marina.
He laughed when Ina Gartner, the owner of the Barefoot Contessa, the fancy-food shop where feverish crowds were shopping as if for a creme-fraiche famine of biblical proportions, cautioned that Wall Street titans obsessed with getting their boats into Long Island Sound so early in the season might hit an iceberg or two.
"I'm like everyone else," he said. "I become obsessed with getting my boat in the water by Memorial Day weekend. Of course, it's irrational because the weather's usually too cold. But once it's in the water, you can fool yourself that summer's begun.
"We've had crews working double shifts," added Mr. Krupinski, who is a part owner of the marina. "Unfortunately, mine is one of the last to go, because I think people might find it a little distasteful if they learned that the owner's boat made it into the water before theirs."
But the ire of summer residents rarely has any effect, said Al Wilson, a slipcover cutter based on the North Fork. "You get a lot of folks yelling over the phone that they want everything done by Memorial Day," he said, "but it doesn't do them much good if the slipcovers aren't ready."
Tugging immaculate white covers over dining chairs in the 1820 Sagaponack farmhouse of the architect Alison Spear and her husband, Carlos Gomez, a financier, he said:
"This house is right out of a magazine, but it's nothing compared to Chuck Scarborough's place in Southampton."
Asked if the NBC newscaster was also a client, Mr. Wilson grinned. "You bet," he said. "What a guy. Three wives, three houses, and I've slip-covered them all."
