HEADLINE: Canada's Vote Focus: 'Jobs, Jobs, Jobs'

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: FREDERICTON, New Brunswick, May 28

 BODY:
With his bookstore sandwiched between the election headquarters of two political parties, Eric Aubanel cannot get to work without being bombarded by campaign promises to lower Canada's chronically high unemployment rate.
"It's a big issue here," Mr. Aubanel said from behind the register at Kingfisher Books. "But there's not much that any government is going to do about it." 
 Most opinion polls show that unemployment and job security are the most important issues in the elections on Monday, when Canadians elect a new House of Commons, and also the most difficult for candidates to address.
It was the same in the last national election in 1993 when Prime Minister Jean Chretien and his Liberal Party ran on a platform of "jobs, jobs, jobs." But while Mr. Chretien claims to have created more than 770,000 new jobs since he took office, 1.4 million Canadians are still out of work, the same number as in 1993.
Canada's unemployment rate has dropped to 9.6 percent, from more than 11 percent when Mr. Chretien took over. But it remains far higher than the 4.9 percent unemployment rate in the United States.
So when candidates promise to create jobs, voters like Mr. Aubanel turn skeptical.
"The Government can't really do anything about jobs," said Mr. Aubanel, 33, a research scientist who started his bookstore with his wife, Frances, two years ago because he could not find a job teaching. "I almost wish voters would stop bringing it up because it only forces the politicians into contortions trying to come up with a plan."
The national leaders of all five major parties bend over backward promising to reduce unemployment, but none have a convincing plan for creating jobs.
Jean Charest of the Progressive Conservatives wants to further reduce the $75 billion national budget while at the same time cutting taxes to increase consumer spending and thus help create jobs. Preston Manning of the Reform Party thinks that by eliminating government regulation, businesses will be freer to expand. The Liberals and the other two parties talk vaguely about reducing the deficit.
All the politicians know the dangers of being too frank about any government's limited ability to actually create anything other than civil service jobs.
On the day in 1993 when the national election was called, Kim Campbell, who was then Prime Minister, in a blunt response to a reporter's question, said she foresaw three or four more years of unacceptably high unemployment in Canada, and no substantial reduction in the jobless rate until the year 2000.
Mr. Chretien hammered Ms. Campbell throughout the 1993 campaign for resigning herself to high unemployment. That hurt her party, the Progressive Conservatives, which went on to suffer the worst defeat in its history.
Tom Kierans, president of the C. D. Howe Institute, a conservative policy organization in Toronto, said that despite the electoral trouble Ms. Campbell's statement caused her, she was right about the near impossibility of sharply reducing Canada's unemployment.
"I think 7.5 percent to 8 percent would have to be considered full employment in Canada," he said.
Several factors keep Canada's unemployment rate from dropping as low as the rate in the United States, Mr. Kierans said. Canada has a broader definition of unemployment, including people who look for jobs casually by checking want ads. In the United States, only people who report to employment centers and actively seek work are counted.
Another factor is the higher rate of incarceration in the United States, which removes from jobless lists many people Canada would consider unemployed.
The rest is more difficult to explain. Canadians are less willing to move to find a new job, especially if they live in Quebec or the Atlantic provinces like New Brunswick, said David M. Gray, a professor of economics at the University of Ottawa.
Mr. Chretien promises that the jobless rate will shrink as Canada's budget deficit disappears by the year 2000. He reasons that if the Government does not need to borrow money to cover the deficit, interest rates drop along with the cost of mortgages and auto loans. That increases demand for houses and cars, putting more people to work.
But Mr. Kierans said that is not necessarily what would happen. As the economy grows, he said, more people come out to look for work, driving the rate back up.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Radio spectrum sales seem a success. Why the attack?

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
WASHINGTON'S three-year-old experiment in auctioning access to the radio spectrum is widely seen as a raging success, accelerating the telecommunications revolution and raising more than $20 billion for Uncle Sam. Why, then, is renewal of the Federal Communications Commission's broad authority to sell spectrum rights as quickly as possible under attack?
One reason is that Congress has been seduced by what Peter Pitsch, a communications consultant in Washington, calls "the dark side of the Force." Easy revenues from auctions are distracting lawmakers from the central objective of getting underutilized spectrum into productive use. 
 The other is muscle-flexing by communications companies -- notably, cellular phone service providers -- that already have the slots they need and are loath to see their profits eroded by added competition. Their concerns evoke no sympathy from Reed E. Hundt, the free-market-minded chairman of the F.C.C. who announced his resignation on Tuesday. "Spectrum licenses are tickets to ride the information highway," he argued. "We ought to be handing out as many spaces as there are drivers."
In the not-so-good old days the F.C.C. judged applications for spectrum use one by one, rewarding the "best"-- read, "best connected" -- with free licenses. But as spectrum grew incredibly valuable, this cozy system led to endless challenges and counterchallenges: the last big proceeding, in which the lawyers fought trench warfare over some $100 billion of free cell-phone licenses, delayed the start-up of cell-phone service in big cities by more than a decade.
In desperation the F.C.C. started handing out licenses by lottery in the 1980's. But that approach, too, became a vulgar joke, as tens of thousands of Americans with a $100 application fee and a dream vied for the right to become instant millionaires. So in 1993, Congress finally gave the green light to auctions.
Some $23 billion has thus far been committed by high bidders in 15 auctions. And from start to on-the-air finish, the process takes just a few years. Moreover, multi-round auctions adopted by the F.C.C. allow bidders to efficiently package both frequencies and geographic territories, maximizing the value of the licenses from the get-go.
Thrilled with found money that could not be labeled a tax, Washington has budgeted $26 billion more from spectrum sales over the next five years. So what's the problem?
Start with the fact that established spectrum owners -- and Wall Street -- are waking up to the fact that the F.C.C. wants the supply of scarce spectrum services like wireless phones to grow faster than demand. In April, Bernstein Research warned investors of probable "price discounting and promotions as carriers aggressively seek market share to maximize the contribution toward their fixed-cost base."
The cell-phone operators -- mostly multibillion-dollar corporations -- will not go down without a fight. "A lot of incumbents are working hard to insure that spectrum remains scarce," confirmed James Gattuso, vice president for policy development at the free-market-minded Citizens for a Sound Economy in Washington.
Now add the broadcast lobby to the political pot, and stir. Broadcasters have no direct stake in auctions because they have already muscled the right to free spectrum for long-awaited high-definition digital television service. But they are eager to discredit auctions, apparently in hope that they will not be forced to give back blocks of spectrum now allocated for ordinary television signals in time for their resale in 2002.
Washington inadvertently gave them a target when a hastily arranged auction in April, originally "scored" by the Congressional Budget Committee to generate $1.8 billion in revenue, yielded a paltry $13 million. "Clearly, spectrum auctions have reached the point of diminishing returns," wrote Eddie O. Fritts, president of the National Association of Broadcasters, in a letter to Senator Pete Domenici, Republican of New Mexico, chairman of the Senate Budget Committee.
Congress has only itself to blame: by setting tight deadlines to meet budgetary needs, potential bidders were unable to iron out technical problems in using the new frequencies before the auction. Nonetheless, the apparent fiasco set off alarms in the ranks of the deficit hawks. Senator Bob Kerrey, the Nebraska Democrat, is talking about setting "reserve prices" below which spectrum could not be sold. And Senator John McCain of Arizona, an influential Republican conservative, is floating the idea of restricting sales in order to maximize Government revenues -- an initiative cheered by incumbent licensees.
The backlash does not surprise Robert Crandall, a regulatory economist at the Brookings Institution. "With the government in the business of selling public property, the temptation is always there to increase scarcity," he said.
This new confluence of interest between anxious license holders and a fiscally challenged Congress has shaken the departing champion of the auction system. "I'm proud we've collected billions for the Treasury," Mr. Hundt said, "but that's just a bonus."

LENGTH: 927 words

HEADLINE: Harran Journal;
Restoring the Fertile Crescent to Its Former Glory

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER 

DATELINE: HARRAN, Turkey

 BODY:
From the ruins of the ancient citadel that towers above this remote and dusty town, the plains of Mesopotamia stretch in every direction. Many great visionaries have reigned here, but perhaps none has had grander dreams than a 40-year-old engineer who stood atop the windswept bluff on a recent afternoon and surveyed his domain.
The engineer, Olcay Unver, heads the Southeast Anatolia Project, one of the most ambitious development projects ever attempted. His goals are nothing less than to transform an arid tract of land the size of Austria into a fertile and prosperous garden, to break down a feudal social structure in which most of its 6 million residents live in poverty and to provide so much work and opportunity that people will flow into this region from other parts of Turkey rather than fleeing in despair. 
 In his seventh year of running the project, Mr. Unver is beginning to succeed. Applying in part the lessons he learned while studying for his doctorate in engineering at the University of Texas and his experience as a water engineer for the Lower Colorado River Authority in Texas, he is slowly turning this backward and violent region into a model of rising expectations.
The scale of the $32 billion project, which envisions the construction of 22 dams and 19 hydroelectric plants on the Tigris and Euphrates Rivers, is vast enough to fulfill any engineer's fantasies.
Three of the dams and adjacent power plants are completed, including the giant Ataturk Dam, the centerpiece of the project. Six more complexes are under construction, work on two others is about to begin, and the rest are being designed.
Under Mr. Unver's direction, the project has broadened far beyond its original scope. It now includes forestry, fish farming and urban planning programs, rural health clinics, literacy classes, handicraft courses, schools for farmers and centers for the promotion of women's rights. Dozens of such programs and centers are already functioning and more are opening every month.
"A lot of people have the misconception that this is an irrigation project or an energy project," Mr. Unver said as he looked out over the blossoming Harran Plain. "Actually it's an integrated socio-economic development project which aims to enhance the standard of living in this area. Maximum yield is not the goal. A good yield in an equitable social context is a better goal."
The project has roots as far back as the 1930's, when President Kemal Ataturk, the father of modern Turkey, proposed the construction of dams to harness the power of the Tigris and Euphrates. The region through which the rivers flow, one of the cradles of early civilization, was once known as the fertile crescent, but it dried up over the centuries and became a miserable backwater.
In 1989 the Turkish Government decided to consolidate the project within a single agency. Mr. Unver, then a young Government planner, became the agency's on-site director, and in 1991 he took over the leadership of the program. He has survived through seven Governments under five Prime Ministers.
Much of the region has been wracked by civil and military conflict for more than a decade. Kurdish separatists here are fighting the Turkish Army, and four of the nine provinces in the project area are under emergency rule.
When the war was at its peak in the late 1980's and early '90's, guerrillas singled out the project, attacking several machine shops and killing at least one group of engineers. But the guerrillas are not known to have attacked any of the project's sites in the last two years.
Scores of new factories are under construction, and scores more are already open. Most are producing textiles from the cotton that can now be grown in irrigated local soil, or processing the fruits and vegetables that are sprouting from green fields. All are fueled by the cheap electric power that is becoming available. A surprising number of the factories are owned by local entrepreneurs who had until now preferred to invest their money in other parts of the country.
"Everywhere in the region, we see a great deal of private investment, mostly by local people," said Elizabeth W. Shelton, the American Consul in nearby Adana. "I'm also struck by the amount of infrastructure investment the Government is putting in. There's tremendous road construction under way. Airports are being built. We're telling American businessmen that now is the time to come into the southeast."
Guerrillas are not the project's only problem, though. The two countries that lie downstream in the Tigris-Euphrates basin, Syria and Iraq, have complained bitterly about Turkey's plan to divert large amounts of the rivers' water.
Because of this conflict, major lenders like the World Bank have refused to contribute to construction of the dams or irrigation projects. Their reluctance has slowed the project and forced the Turkish Government to come up with almost all the money itself, fueling the country's 80 percent inflation rate.
The project is less than half finished, and its effects are only beginning to be felt by the local population. No one, not even Mr. Unver, will venture to guess when it might be completed. For years to come, people in this region will probably remain Turkey's poorest.
"Development levels here are comparable to those in Africa," said Mehmet Sirin Yigit, who heads the chamber of commerce in Diyarbakir, the region's largest city. "That has to change."
He added that what the project signifies, "we hope, is that the change has begun."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Olcay Unver, 40, is head of a program to turn an arid area of Turkey into a prosperous garden. He stood atop a citadel overlooking the Harran Plain. (Staton R. Winter for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Elections Near, Mexican Money Supply Grows

BYLINE: By JULIA PRESTON 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, May 28

 BODY:
The amount of cash in circulation in Mexico has recently been rising, increasing suspicion that the Government may be moving to prime the economic pump before legislative and local elections this summer.
But independent analysts say that despite the intense political pressure in advance of the elections, scheduled for July 6, the Mexican Government has largely refrained from relaxing its economic policies to curry voter favor. 
 "I can't say there is absolutely no election spending," said Deborah Reiner, chief economist at the American Chamber of Commerce here. "But I don't see anything that indicates the Government has deviated in any important way. It's pretty impressive that they have stayed so much on track."
The Institutional Revolutionary Party, or PRI, which has ruled Mexico for decades, is facing the most competitive voting in modern times. Polls show that the leftist opposition leader, Cuauhtemoc Cardenas, is ahead in the race for Mayor of Mexico City, and the PRI could lose its six-decade-long majority in the lower house of Congress.
Given that pressure, and the history of misleading status reports and behind-the-scenes fiddling by Mexican officials in electoral seasons, analysts said it might not have come as a surprise if President Ernesto Zedillo had strayed from the tight economic policies that restored Mexico's fiscal stability after the financial crisis of 1995, which eroded Mexicans' everyday living standards.
"Over the years the Government has earned the right to have everyone doubt it," wrote Jonathan Heath, an economist who has been critical of Mr. Zedillo, in a newspaper column published this week. "Nevertheless, in this specific case, I tend to believe the official explanation."
Central bank officials abandoned their customary silence last week to respond to reports suggesting that they were injecting cash into the economy. The amount of money in Mexicans' pockets increased more than the bank forecast because the economy grew by an unexpected 5.1 percent in the first quarter, explained Jose Julian Sidaoui, a deputy governor of the bank.
Several unusually long holidays, including Mother's Day on May 10, came close together this year, making the demand for cash especially high, the officials said.
"We like to give presents and flowers on Mother's Day," Mr. Sidaoui said. He acknowledged that the political campaigns had also affected demand for pesos, but only because more Mexicans were being paid in cash for political work than the bank anticipated.
But none of this, he said, is intended to artificially stimulate the economy.
Mr. Sidaoui said the central bank could react if inflationary pressure arose, "exclusively based on economic conditions."
The Government's economic program has gotten an endorsement from the currency markets, where the peso has been unusually stable in recent months, and from a generally rising stock market. The peso has been hovering just below 8 to the dollar since the beginning of the year, settling in New York late this afternoon at 7.9030 to the dollar. If money had been artificially pumped into the economy, a decline might be expected in the value of the peso, and then a surge in inflation, which has been held in check.
The peso has been sustained in part by a big influx of foreign capital, economists said. The American Chamber of Commerce estimates there had been $2.5 billion in direct foreign investment in Mexico in the first quarter of this year.
"We believe the currency is appropriately valued at this stage," said Lawrence Goodman, chief of Latin American research at Salomon Brothers.
By no means has President Zedillo remained aloof from the electoral fray. In his frequent travel outside the capital, he trumpets his success in pulling Mexico out of its deepest economic slump in decades and in restarting growth.
His Government planned a 9.3 percent social spending increase in real terms for 1997. It is building schools, roads, electrical grids and health clinics at a brisk pace -- the most generous social spending as a percentage of the budget that Mexico has seen in the last 10 years. Government officials have said privately in recent days they were projecting a slightly larger budget deficit than previously expected.
Nor has the central bank stayed completely out of the peso market. In a series of auctions, the bank has been buying dollars to build up Mexico's reserves, which have reached $21.7 billion, the highest level since the '95 crisis. But that does not seem to have undermined the peso's value, and the healthy reserves suggest that the Government is in an improved position to stanch any run on the currency.
Still, the recovery, which has taken hold mainly in northern regions where thriving export industries are centered, has not yet reached the majority of voting Mexicans. "People are still in crisis," said Sergio Martin Moreno, economist for ING Barings in Mexico City.
The Government has done "a very good job in stabilizing the economy," he said. "But for people in general, that's very difficult to recognize."

HEADLINE: Pentagon Spurs 1.4% Rise In Orders for Durable Goods

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28

 BODY:
Orders for big-ticket manufactured goods rose 1.4 percent in April, in part because of unexpectedly large orders by the Pentagon, Commerce Department figures showed today.
The rise in orders for durable goods, items from air-conditioners to rocket launchers that are expected to last at least three years, erased about two-thirds of a March decline. Most types of products posted gains, but electronic equipment slumped for the second month, causing some concern. Over all, orders rose by $2.4 billion, to $175.6 billion. 
 The biggest contributor to the rise was a 4.5 percent jump in transportation equipment, much of it for the military, that more than offset the 3.9 percent March drop in that category.
Though orders for durables are a closely watched, forward-looking indicator, month-to-month swings are so wide that analysts rarely use the data to draw firm conclusions about the near-term economic future. And when military business is so prominent, still greater allowance is made because these orders respond to political rather than economic factors, and are filled over long periods.
The gain in orders in April was slightly larger than expected, said Joel L. Naroff of the First Union Bank in Philadelphia, "but there was nothing in the report to suggest that the economy is either further accelerating or softening greatly." The chief implication, therefore, "is that the early spring slowdown has not yet been reversed," he said.
Oscar Gonzalez, an economist at John Hancock in Boston, said, "The numbers suggest pockets of weakness, yet still a growing manufacturing sector."
Orders for industrial machinery and equipment advanced 2.2 percent, the fifth consecutive increase, while the primary metals category jumped 2.8 percent.
On the other hand, new orders for electronic and other electrical goods tumbled 5.3 percent after a 6 percent drop in March, a combination that Mr. Naroff said "raises some questions" about the sector but did not arouse fears of a major slowdown.
Military orders soared 28 percent following double-digit declines a month earlier. Without the Pentagon's business, the overall gain in orders would have been only six-tenths of 1 percent instead of 1.4 percent.
Orders for nonmilitary capital goods other than aircraft, a gauge of future investment in plant and equipment that strips out products that tend to be ordered in bunches, edged down six-tenths of 1 percent in April following a nine-tenths of 1 percent March decline.
Edward F. McKelvey, senior economist at Goldman, Sachs & Company, said this might represent "a bit of softening" but from a pace that remained quite rapid.
Over all, he said of today's figures that "they don't reveal any clear change in trend." The decline for March, initially put at 2.6 percent, was narrowed today to a 2.2 percent decline.
The department's report also showed that shipments of durable goods rose 1.1 percent, to $177.3 billion last month, while backlogs of unfilled orders eased four-tenths of 1 percent, to $493.4 billion, matching a March decline that was the first since August.

HEADLINE: Scandal Stains Nomura, But Will It Stick?;
Future of Brokerage Firm Is a Test for Japan

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO, May 25

 BODY:
He lived for his job as a middle manager at the Nomura Securities Company, the world's largest securities firm. Then the latest scandal hit, and his son, who had not bothered to telephone all year from college, called, worried that the firm might not survive.
"Many of us are starting to feel that even our children may not be proud to say their father is working at Nomura," said the manager, a tall man in a silvery gray suit, who refused to be identified. "The company is in a kind of anarchy now. It's like a ship that can't decide which way to go. While it can't decide, it's starting to sink." 
"The Gulliver," the employee added, "could become a Lilliputian."
It is a long way to fall for Japan's most powerful securities house: from the pinnacle of financial and political influence a few months ago, poised to lead Japan into a new era of financial liberalization, to a twilight zone as a scandalized pariah with which few want to deal openly -- all because of accusations that Nomura has had ties to gangsters.
Now, some employees and analysts say, the firm faces the challenge of its life: to shed its old ways, shake off the current disgrace and brace for a rough-and-tumble competition in the run-up to the Big Bang, Japan's grand plan to remake its markets to look like New York and London by 2001.
But the scandal is not just about Nomura and payoffs to gangsters. When prosecutors press formal charges against Nomura, as they are expected to do next month, that could snowball into an indictment of Japan's entire business tradition -- the circle of influence that creates shadowy alliances among politicians, bureaucrats and business executives. That tradition has long hindered Japan from freeing its markets and competing efficiently in the world of finance.
Among the documents prosecutors are scrutinizing are records for a couple of hundred special accounts -- which carried the designation "01" -- for leading politicians, ministers and bureaucrats in addition to gangsters. These clients are so important to Nomura that they have been handled secretly only by top Nomura executives, say employees and others with close ties to the company.
These special accounts gave the owners extraordinary benefits and perhaps undeserved profits that allowed Nomura to spread its tentacles into Japan's corridors of power. A similar scandal six years ago exposed the existence of the accounts and shook Nomura; their persistence underscores how deeply such shady ties reach into the Japanese system.
"The leaders of political factions collect funds through the manipulation of stocks," said Eitaro Itoyama, a former member of Parliament and Nomura's largest individual shareholder, with 10 million shares. "If the super-V.I.P. accounts are disclosed, it could be found that past prime ministers and vice ministers of the Finance Ministry were involved."
For Nomura, the pressure to uproot such traditional practices is mounting. In many ways, its rebirth is a test for Japan's financial industry as it strives to prove to the world's financial community that it can perform and behave like any other international player.
"We are now carrying out the realization of a real liberalization of the market," said Takao Toshikawa, who writes a political newsletter in Tokyo. "So we are in a sensitive time, politically, economically and culturally."
But Nomura always seems to have had luck on its side, ever since it began more than a century ago in a small shop run by a money changer born out of wedlock who took his mother's name, Nomura, and often drank himself into a stupor. His clever son, with barely a grade school education, catapulted the company into stocks by risking the firm's capital on a bet that the fledgling Tokyo stock market would crash in 1907. He was right and made the equivalent of millions in today's dollars.
Since then, Nomura has amassed an enormous pyramid of knowledge, contacts, sophistication and secrets. Its expertise is so far ahead of its regulators in Kasumigaseki, where the Finance Ministry is, that it is regularly consulted on drafts of new rules. During the booming days in the 1980's, brokers often referred to the ministry's securities bureau as the "Kasumigaseki branch of Nomura."
Still, Nomura's layers of secrets are haunting the company. Around Kabutocho, Tokyo's version of Wall Street, there is talk that the confidential numbered accounts were used to channel special compensation, rights to buy new issues and financial knowledge about companies.
Nomura's employees, described by one Japanese newspaper, as "pitiless, professional and cold-hearted" men who paid their dues by pounding the pavement peddling stocks to ordinary families, are virtually in revolt. Talented young employees have already left the company, and Nomura resumes are flying around Tokyo.
Morale is low, and these days, when Nomura salespeople make client calls, they often find the doors shut. Foreign and domestic companies, even Nomura's own affiliates, have been halting business with the company. The Finance Ministry banned Nomura from underwriting Government bonds, and regulators may suspend some of Nomura's business for three months.
[Setsuya Tabuchi, Nomura's former chairman and a towering figure in Japanese finance, and Hideo Sakamaki, its former president, who resigned in March, testified Wednesday before Parliament. Mr. Sakamaki acknowledged he had met with a gangster, and some employees fear that he may be arrested.]
Nomura's new president, Junichi Ujiie, who took over May 1 after 15 directors resigned to improve Nomura's image, acknowledged the challenges.
"What we are short of is pride for our company," he said in a speech broadcast throughout the company. "I hereby pledge to all of you that I will change this company into one that everyone can be proud of."
"The time to act is now," he added. "This is our last chance."
Mr. Ujiie also suggested that while the company could not collapse because of a financial loss, it could disintegrate because of a scandal and loss of morale, two employees said. Most people expect the company to bounce back, but precedents abroad, names like Kidder, Peabody and others consigned to history after financial scandals, are stark reminders of the risks.
For many employees, who were kept in the dark about the firm's ties to gangsters, there is a sense of betrayal and of growing distrust of top management.
"People are really disillusioned because those who were enforcing the rules were dealing with gangsters," said a Nomura employee who resigned last week to join another firm and who refused to be identified.
The scandal, first disclosed in February, led to the arrest on May 14 of a Nomura general manager and two former board directors. They reportedly paid 49.7 million yen ($418,000) to compensate for trading losses of a client linked to sokaiya, gangsters illegally paid by Japanese companies not to disrupt shareholder meetings by asking embarrassing questions or making demands of the management.
Then last week, prosecutors raided Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank, Japan's second-largest bank, on suspicions that it lent 26 billion yen ($260 million) to the Nomura client, the brother of Ryuichi Koike, a prominent sokaiya, to help finance stock purchases at Nomura and other brokerage firms.
Nomura and Dai-Ichi Kangyo officials have acknowledged wrongdoing but have not elaborated.
So far, it is unclear why Nomura and Dai-Ichi Kangyo were paying the sokaiya. Some people say Nomura felt threatened by Mr. Koike, who reportedly owned 300,000 shares in the company.
"Ties between the sokaiya and securities companies, they're as close as the chicken and the egg -- and you can't tell which comes first," said Kaoru Ogawa, a former sokaiya who says he worked for Nomura preserving order at shareholder meetings.
The origins go way back, he said. Sometimes a company preparing to list on the stock exchange would turn to Nomura, which would introduce a sokaiya to protect management at shareholder meetings. Mr. Ogawa said that many sokaiya, including himself, then expanded their ties with securities companies into investing.
"For Nomura, the regular ordinary people who buy 1,000 shares or so are trash," he said. "If you buy two million yen worth in stocks, the Nomura man might shoo you out the door. They much more appreciate the two billion yen from Koike."
Two billion yen is worth about $17.2 million. Thus, the special accounts illuminate what the little investor in Japan has feared for years -- that he is squeezed for commissions and sometimes sacrificed for the sake of favored clients.
In a scandal exposed in 1991, Nomura was caught manipulating the stock price of the Tokyu Corporation to allow a special client -- one of Japan's leading gangsters -- to sell at a large profit while small investors were taking a bath in shares of the railway and property company.
The scandal rocked Nomura -- its chairman and president were forced to resign, and half of its Japanese branch offices were ordered shut for a month -- but the company recovered. This time, however, some say that Nomura may emerge weaker and that the Finance Ministry is considering several actions, including the possible spinoff of parts of Nomura.
Some insiders, however, say the doomsday talk is exaggerated.
"Nomura has power and money," the employee who recently left the firm said. "It's a company that can just bide its time as other players exhaust themselves in the course of Big Bang deregulation. If Nomura collapses, the others will, too."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Setsuya Tabuchi, Nomura's former chairman, left, and Hideo Sakamaki, its former president, who resigned in March, testified yesterday before the Japanese Parliament. Mr. Sakamaki has acknowledged meeting with a gangster. (Associated Press)(pg. D1)
 
Chart/Photo: "A Sullied Giant"
Nomura Securities, the world's largest securities firm, was exposed in 1991 as having manipulated the stock price of a Japanese railway company by talking up its stock to help a gangster sell shares at a large profit. With the company now caught in a second scandal involving special deals for gangster associates, Nomura's business in Japan and abroad may be seriously harmed.
 
ALLEGATIONS
Nomura reportedly maintained confidential numbered accounts used to offer special compensation to favored clients such as politicians, ministers, bureaucrats and gangsters. The firm allegedly paid $418,000 to compensate for trading losses of a gangster's brother.
 
LOST BUSINESS
The California Public Employees' Retirement System, Nippon Telegraph and Telephone, and many Japanese pension funds, bond issuers, municipalities, insurance companies, and even Nomura affiliates have withdrawn business from the firm. Japan's Postal Ministry has suspended investing money through Nomura, and the Finance Ministry has banned the firm from underwriting Government bonds.
 
CONSEQUENCES
Prosecutors are expected to press formal charges against Nomura that could give unwelcome scrutiny to Japan's tradition of alliances among politicians, bureaucrats, business executives and gangsters.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Flat on Signs Of Slowdown

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Treasury securities ended little changed yesterday after a day of choppy trading.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond ended unchanged at 94 1/32, leaving its yield at 7.02 percent, also unchanged. Prices of shorter maturities were also essentially flat. 
 Bond prices were bolstered early in the session after the Commerce Department reported that orders for durable goods rose 1.4 percent in April. While the increase was slightly higher than market expectations, much of the month's gain came in orders for the military. As a result, the report provided further evidence that economic growth was slowing and that interest rates might steady.
"I think we're seeing a little relief here as the data we've gotten this morning was a little to the weak side," said Richard Berner, executive vice president and chief economist at the Mellon Bank Corporation.
Most gains, though, were erased in the afternoon after an auction of new two-year notes was met with disappointing demand.
The Treasury sold $16.5 billion of two-year notes at a yield of 6.328 percent, higher than the forecasts of 6.31 to 6.315 percent.
Today, the Treasury sells $12 billion of new five-year notes. In when-issued trading late yesterday, the five-year note was being bid at an average yield of 6.65 percent, up from 6.63 percent late Tuesday.
Few market analysts expect significant price movement before the release of the May employment report on June 6.
The Federal Reserve's policy-making panel, the Federal Open Market Committee, next meets on July 1 and 2, and a large amount of data is scheduled to be released before then. Alan Greenpsan, the Federal Reserve chairman, has said that he favors further tightening of monetary policy if the economy continues to expand rapidly, even in the absence of overt inflationary statistics.
"There's a lot of information to come out before the next F.O.M.C. meeting," Mr. Berner of Mellon Bank said. "The economy would have to turn decisively stronger or inflation higher" for the Fed to move, he added.
Despite the market's caution, trading volume was very heavy yesterday. More than $114 billion in securities were traded yesterday, according to Govpx, a bond pricing service, about 42 percent higher than on a typical Wednesday in the second quarter. But much of the activity was in the when-issued two-year notes.

HEADLINE: Blair's Muted Helpmate: Look at Her Now!

BYLINE: By WARREN HOGE 

DATELINE: LONDON, May 28

 BODY:
The face was the same one that recently figured in hundreds of photo opportunities and front-page pictures around the world. But where there had been a designer suit there was a black robe, on top of the softly layered hairstyle there was a tightly curled white wig, and in place of the perpetual broad smile there was a judicious scowl.
Cherie Blair, Queen's Counsel, was back at work. 
 For two months she had been the constantly beaming consort of her husband, Tony Blair, as he took his Labor Party campaign around the British Isles, and in a memorable moment she joined him in a hand-in-hand promenade along Whitehall while thousands of flag-waving people cheered their arrival at the official 10 Downing Street residence the day after the May 1 election.
A high-powered London lawyer with young children and a host of friends, she was often compared to Hillary Rodham Clinton, just as her husband, a telegenic young politician who reformed his party and thereby captured the political center, was being compared to Bill Clinton.
The Labor campaign just as persistently kept saying that, unlike the American First Lady, whom she will meet on Thursday for the first time, Cherie Booth, as she is known professionally, harbored no political ambitions and would play no role in the new administration.
So last week she returned to her high-backed leather armchair behind the elevated desk in the Mayor's and City of London Court and presided over the case of Dixon v. British Aerospace.
Even though the details sound awfully small-bore for the wife of a head of government -- a worker is seeking redress from British Aerospace for a hernia he suffered when a machine tool drill slipped -- the fact that Cherie Booth is paying attention to them represents a historic moment in this tradition-minded country. No spouse of a Prime Minister has ever held a full-time job before.
In her precedent-setting pursuit, Ms. Booth profits from two other English traditions. The politics-free nature of the judiciary insulates her from suspicion of any conflict of interest and she can move about relatively unmolested by autograph seekers and paparazzi because of the privacy permitted non-royal participants in British public life.
"This is not like Hillary Clinton going into Federal District Court in Washington," said Michael J. McNulty, an American lawyer who has practiced here for 28 years.
Ms. Booth, 42, a highly regarded lawyer in London, had a dazzling academic record at the London School of Economics, topped all candidates in her bar exams, entered practice in 1976 and remarkably swiftly was made a Queen's Counsel in 1995, a title held by only the top 10 percent of English barristers. While these remain predominantly male, a growing number are women.
Indications of the independence of the English judiciary abound, but one is that she was given her Q. C. suffix by a Conservative Lord Chancellor at a time when her husband had already become the high-profile new leader of the Labor Party.
She qualified last year to be an Assistant Recorder, a part-time judge in lower court civil cases, and the first rung on the ladder to becoming a High Court judge, her ultimate ambition. In countries like the United States where judges are often either directly elected or produced from the political process it would seem untenable to have the wife of a national leader rendering judgment in cases that turn on governmental conduct and national law.
Voice that concern in England, and people are taken aback.
"There's absolutely no thought she could be biased," said Denise Kingsmill, a colleague and friend of Ms. Booth's. "Our whole system is designed to eliminate bias."
"It's part of why we put them in wigs and gowns," she continued. "I know some people think it's all a lot of antiquated rubbish, but it has a function. It puts the emphasis on the role the persons are performing, not the persons."
Over the last few months Cherie Booth, a busy mother of three young children, committed lawyer and jurist and an outspoken feminist, underwent a startling change in public personality, suppressing her opinionated side to become Cherie Blair, the adoring and silent partner of her 44-year-old husband as he led his party to its overwhelming victory.
She stood to the side when he spoke, flashed her signature lopsided smile, vigorously pressed the flesh along the police barricades, embraced and kissed her husband at photographers' behest and uttered not a single quoted phrase.
In one uncharacteristic venture, she was guest editor for an issue of the women's magazine Prima, and the public learned of her 30-minute dinner solutions, her warnings about letting the mystery go out of marriage and her assessment of her husband's household skills ("He's very good at polishing shoes and has been known to cook a meal -- I wouldn't say he is intimate with our washing machine, but he does know where it is.") Whoever in the slickly managed Labor campaign thought up this gambit thought better of it. It was the last Cherie Blair was heard from.
Did the women among her friends fault her behavior? "Not at all," said Kathy Lette, a London-based Australian writer. "She had to take the Hillary antidote. The press here is very virile and anti-feminist. We were all supportive of what she needed to do."
Her ability to keep her private persona from the public one lapsed only once, and not until the morning after her husband's election. A delivery man bearing flowers came to the door of the Blairs' Islington home, and she sleepily opened it, forgetting the world press was on the street outside. The picture of her standing barefoot in a wrinkled nightgown, her black hair an unruly bird's nest, was on every front page the next day.
She is the child of touring actors, and her father, Tony Booth, left home when Cherie (the name is pronounced as if it were spelled Sherry) and her sister, Lyndsey, were young girls. The girls were raised by their mother, Gale, in the Lancashire town of Bury. Cherie inherited the Catholicism of her mother and the Socialist views of her father. Tony Blair, by contrast, came from a lifelong Conservative family.
She married Mr. Blair in 1980, and in 1983 made her own foray into politics, losing as the Labor candidate for Parliament in a Tory district in southeastern England while her husband was winning in Sedgefield in the north.
She resolved to focus on the law, not politics. She made her legal specialty employment law and involved herself in issues of sex, race and disability discrimination, domestic violence, child abuse, trade union disputes, sexual harassment, health and safety workplace standards, personnel problems, wrongful dismissals and equal pay claims. She has long been the family breadwinner, and her annual income these days is estimated at more than $350,000.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Cherie Blair, wife of Britain's Prime Minister, at her new home, and dressed for her job as a Queen's Counsel. (Photographs by Associated Press)

HEADLINE: President Urges Aid for East In Spirit of the Marshall Plan

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: THE HAGUE, May 28

 BODY:
Celebrating the 50th anniversary of the Marshall Plan to rebuild Western Europe after World War II, President Clinton said today that the West "must complete the noble journey" and bring the former Communist nations of East Europe into a prosperous, united Europe.
The President did not propose a new American aid program, noting that private business and international financial institutions had invested almost $100 billion in the former Eastern bloc countries since the fall of Communism. Instead, Mr. Clinton said, Western nations should encourage more private investment, help these nations establish the rule of law, and teach entrepreneurial skills. 
 "Our generation, like the one before us, must choose," Mr. Clinton said. "Without the threat of the cold war, without the pain of economic ruin, without the fresh memory of World War II's slaughter, it is tempting to pursue our private agendas -- to simply sit back and let history unfold. We must resist the temptation."
Mr. Clinton praised the European Union's plans to open its doors to new member states, a move the formerly Communist nations complain is proceeding far too slowly.
Prime Minister Wim Kok of the Netherlands, speaking before Mr. Clinton, said assistance to the new economies in Central Europe "is needed on a massive scale." He called for a public-private initiative to link East and West Europe and estimated that as much as $100 billion of investment is needed.
"If only because of the sheer magnitude of these needs most of the money will have to come from private investment," Mr. Kok said. But he said government investment guarantees and public money would have to play a role.
Mr. Clinton was escorted by Queen Beatrix into the 13th-century Ridderzaal, or Hall of Knights, where heads of state or government from across Europe gathered to mark the postwar economic assistance formally known as the European Recovery Program.
Over four years, the United States provided nearly $13 billion -- the equivalent of $88 billion in today's dollars -- to help Western Europe rebuild.
Mr. Clinton used the occasion to promote the expansion of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, a day after Russia and NATO signed their own new charter. He called the signing an "extraordinary milestone."
It was 50 years ago next week -- on June 5, 1947 -- that Secretary of State George C. Marshall proposed an economic recovery plan for Western Europe -- the vanquished and the victors alike -- in a Harvard University commencement address.
With Europe having just survived the harshest winter on record and with the fear of Communist expansion as a spur, Mr. Marshall said the United States was prepared "to assist in the return to normal economic health to the world without which there can be no political stability and no assured peace."
For the Dutch, Marshall Plan money amounted to $109 a year in assistance for every man, woman and child. And Rotterdam, the Netherlands' second largest city, was raised from the rubble of war with American foreign aid money.
At the commemorative ceremony here trumpets and drums played while scenes taken from the historic highlights of the 20th century -- from war devastation to the moon shot -- played on large video screens. And children danced in leotards designed in the colors of the flags of Europe and North America.
In one emotional moment, Mr. Clinton brought the German Chancellor, Helmut Kohl, to tears as he spoke of a teen-aged German boy who still recalled "the American trucks driving onto the schoolyard bringing soup that warmed hearts and hands." The boy was Mr. Kohl, whom the President called "the first Chancellor of a free and unified Germany."
Another speaker, Gustaaf Sedee, recalled the first American he had ever seen -- a paratrooper of the 82d Airborne Division fighting to liberate his home in 1944.
He described the wrappers on bread that said the food was produced with the assistance of the Marshall Plan. And he told of winning a 1948 essay contest on the Marshall Plan sponsored by The New York Herald Tribune that won him a trip to America and a visit in the Oval Office with President Truman.
"I hereby confess I sort of borrowed a matchbook from that office," he admitted.
Mr. Clinton later attended a rollicking thank-you ceremony in Rotterdam. Thousands of people cheered as Mr. Clinton unveiled a plaque in honor of Mr. Marshall. A children's chorus sang Mr. Clinton's 1992 campaign theme song, "Don't Stop (Thinking About Tomorrow)" as red, white and blue balloons -- the colors of the Dutch flag as well as the American -- poured into the air, in a scene worthy of a campaign rally.
Earlier in the day at the European Union, United States and European officials signed two agreements intended to improve cooperation in fighting customs fraud and the smuggling of chemicals used in drug production.
Negotiators for the United States and the 15-nation European Union also reached preliminary agreement on coordinating standards for testing pharmaceuticals, telecommunications equipment, medical devices and other products.
Officials said that while American regulatory agencies will continue to have final say on what can be sold in the United States, the accord could cut costs by avoiding safety testing on both sides of the Atlantic.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Clinton and his wife, Hillary, applaud a group that sang his 1992 campaign song in Rotterdam, a city rebuilt with Marshall Plan aid. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Fund to Revive Broadway Fare Is Taking Hold

BYLINE: By WILLIAM GRIMES 

 BODY:
In an effort to bring back the serious dramas and small musicals that have almost vanished from Broadway, an unusual coalition of theater owners, producers, and union officials is about to propose a fund of $10 million a year that it says can foster the further revitalization of the theater district.
Seeking to halt a long-term trend that threatens to turn Broadway into a theatrical waxworks dominated by long-running megamusicals and play revivals, the new organization, called the Broadway Initiative, would provide grants or loans to bring in commercially promising work from Off Broadway and regional theaters. 
 The plan, whose success may depend on New York City's ability to provide financial incentives to the theater industry, has set several numerical targets for the next five years: to produce at least 25 plays on Broadway that otherwise would not be there; to increase theater attendance by 3,000 people a day; to boost theatergoing by residents of the New York region by 10 percent, and to bring 75,000 more students into the theaters.
Organizers of the plan said they had kept the city informed about their proposals, and that they were told by representatives of the Giuliani Administration that possible city involvement would be considered when a final proposal was submitted. "It would be premature for the city to comment on any specifics," said Colleen Roche, a spokeswoman for the Mayor's office. "We are very encouraged, however, that the Broadway coalition has come together as tightly as it has." Jack Goldstein, an assistant to the president at Actors Equity, who has served as the informal leader of the coalition, said Broadway was facing "a chronic decline in production."
"There is no single cause or villain or action taken by anyone that caused this," he said, "and there is no single solution. But we are trying to come up with a long-term, thoughtful, comprehensive set of procedures to improve the environment for play production."
Merely as a symbol, the plan carries substantial weight. With the possibility of municipal support dangling before them, representatives of a notoriously fractured and contentious industry have taken the rare step of sitting down together and, moving beyond the finger-pointing stage, addressing serious problems afflicting the Broadway theater.
The timing of the plan, too, has symbolic value. As the Broadway theater gears up for the Tony Awards on Sunday night, and the seasonal migration of tourists begins to gather force, theater in New York City presents every outward sign of vitality. Nearly all 35 Broadway houses are lit. This season, the nominators for the Tony Awards had seven new American musicals to choose from. Gross receipts and attendance are up.
With a week to go until the end of the 1996-97 season, the League of American Theaters and Producers estimates that total gross receipts will be a record $485 million, up from $436 million last year, and attendance will be the second-highest in Broadway's history (exceeded only the 1980-81 season), 10.1 million, up from 9.4 million last year.
But all is not well on Broadway, and has not been for decades. Few new plays make it to Broadway, and those that do, even when welcomed with strong reviews, face long odds of recouping their investment. Christopher Plummer, a heavy favorite to win the Tony for best actor in a play, "Barrymore," performs before a half-empty house at the Music Box Theater. Horton Foote's "Young Man from Atlanta" filled less than a third of the Longacre's 1,096 seats last week.
Broadway has become a showcase for long-running British musicals, musical revivals like this season's "Chicago," and revivals of straight plays, like the hit British production of 'A Doll's House." The shows making the most money today are, for the most part, the same ones that were making big money five years ago: "Cats," "The Phantom of the Opera," "Miss Saigon" and "Les Miserables."
As part of its play-development program, the initiative would provide grants covering up to half the cost of mounting a production that has not reached its full potential audience because of theater size or a repertory schedule that limits its run. Theoretically, because the money would be provided as soon as a decision was made to move to Broadway, theaters would have an easier time attracting investors.
The play-development fund would also provide temporary operating subsidies (up to 40 percent of production costs) for shows in the 19 Broadway houses with fewer than 1,200 seats as a way to help them survive the preview period and the early weeks of a run, when most plays struggle to build an audience, or a seasonal slump. Subsidies could take the form of deferred wages by union workers, reduced theater rent, or help in advertising and marketing.
In an effort to widen the investor pool, the plan proposes a fund to indemnify producers and investors or set a cap on maximum losses an investor can incur.
Negotiations between the city and representatives of the Broadway theater have proceeded very quietly. With only a few exceptions, city officials, theater owners, producers and union leaders involved in planning the initiative declined to be interviewed on the subject until the coalition formally announces its plans after the Tony Awards.
But a draft of the Broadway Initiative's strategic plan obtained from a coalition member provides some insight into the coalition's philosophy and goals, as well as specific proposals and innovations being discussed. The data underlying the plan are contained in a detailed economic assessment of the theater industry carried out last year by Bain & Company, a Boston consulting company.
As outlined in the plan, which is labeled a "discussion draft," the coalition will try to bring about long-term changes in the theater industry by providing the financial incentives and support to draw new plays and small musicals to Broadway. "Development of a more active and vibrant theater environment over a 5-to-10-year period is as much a goal as immediate increases in the number of jobs on Broadway and the higher utilization of theater space," the plan says.
Most of the annual spending, about $6 million, would go toward transferring new plays and small musicals to Broadway from Off Broadway and regional theater. About $3 million would be devoted to programs aimed at expanding and diversifying the theater audience and $1 million to the improvement of theaters and the creation of theater-related buildings like rehearsal spaces.
"At a minimum, the initiative expects to increase average employment within the theater trades as well as theater utilization by 10 to 15 percent within a 3-to-5-year time frame," the plan says. "This would result in increased ticket receipts of $50 million to $60 million a year."
Revenue would come from public and private sources. The plan says these could include a per-ticket contribution by the theaters to generate a minimum of $5 million and contributions by union members actually working in a given week to generate a minimum of $1.2 million. The plan also suggests that money could be raised by the sale of rezoned air rights in the theater district.
Mr. Goldstein said that many details would have to be negotiated before a final version of the initiative is announced in June.
Although the plan provides no details about rezoning or transfers of air rights, people familiar with the negotiations between the city Planning Department and the theater owners say that the city, which would like to encourage development on Eighth Avenue in midtown, is considering a rezoning approach in which anyone wanting to develop sites along Eighth Avenue that exceed current zoning limitations would have to buy air rights from a Broadway theater. Joseph B. Rose, the director of the Planning Department, declined to comment on the rezoning.
Many of the theaters have landmark status and are subject to strict zoning rules governing the sale of air rights. In return for the windfall from air rights, the theaters would have to put a percentage of the money realized into the initiative. Any theater that sold air rights would have to pledge to remain a legitimate theater for the next 25 years.
The city has made no promises to the coalition, and the coalition has asked for none, said Mr. Goldstein, who described the group's approach as coming up with a workable plan that the city, out of enlightened self-interest, would want to accept.
"The city has played an important part, not in making financial commitments, but in encouraging the industry to sit down and address its problems," Mr. Goldstein said.
As described in the plan, the initiative would not try to create hit shows. Rather it would try to improve the quality and range of plays being offered to "widen the stream flowing into Broadway."
"With something like the Broadway Initiative in place, perhaps Paula Vogel's 'How I Learned to Drive' could come to Broadway rather than the Century Theater" on East 15th Street, said Jed Bernstein, executive director of the League of American Theaters and Producers.
"Any fund that can support more productions will lead to better theater,' said Robert Whitehead, a producer who helped foster earlier efforts along these lines. "Our volume of production has fallen to nothing. In Broadway's heyday, you'd have 60 or 70 new productions each year, of which 12 to 15 would survive, and of those several would be pretty good and 2 would be brilliant. The failures gave birth to the successes, and out of it came some of the best, most exciting theater in the world."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Broadway still has its hits, like the long-running musical Cats, above (Martha Swope/"Cats"), but many new productions, like The Young Man From Atlantastarring Rip Torn, right, are faring poorly. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)(pg. B2)
 
Graphs: "Where What's Old Is New, and What's New Often Closes"
Even as the theater industry prepares for the Tony Awards this weekend, and box offices report brisk business, a new report shows that the theater district is in trouble because the pipeline of new productions is drying up. New works are declining, and often the only productions making money are revivals of past hits, making them more likely to be produced. Graphs show average number of Broadway productions each year, from 1920 to 1990 (by decade) and percentage of new productions and revivals recouping initial investment, from 1950 to 1990 (by decade). (Source: Bain & Company Inc. from Variety magazine data)(pg. B2)

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL A3-14
 
Leading French Candidate Disavows Budget Austerity
The leading conservative candidate for Prime Minister of France disavowed the policy of the incumbent, Alain Juppe, of cutting deficits to get ready for a common European currency. The candidate, Philippe Seguin, said cutting France's 12.8 percent unemployment rate would be his top priority. Mr. Juppe plans to step down after Sunday's second round of legislative elections. A12
 
Controlled Voting in Indonesia
President Suharto of Indonesia said in an election-eve speech that democratic development in his country would be "a long process." Today's elections will be won by the ruling Golkar party; Megawati Sukarnoputri, the leader of a banned opposition party, said she would not vote. A3
 
Clinton Toasts Marshall Plan
President Clinton celebrated the 50th anniversary of the Marshall Plan, the effort to help Western Europe recover from World War II. Mr. Clinton and Prime Minister Wim Kok of the Netherlands both stressed the need for a similar program in Eastern Europe but said private investment should play the leading role. A12
 
Russia and Ukraine in Navy Deal
Russia and Ukraine announced that they had reached an agreement on the Black Sea fleet. Under the accord, concluded in Kiev by Prime Minister Viktor Chernomyrdin of Russia and Prime Minister Pavlo Lazerenko of Ukraine, Russia will be able to keep its portion of the former Soviet fleet for 20 years at the Crimean port of Sevastopol. A9
 
Swiss Arms Trade Revealed
A Swiss historian revealed archival evidence detailing Switzerland's wartime sale of weapons to Nazi Germany, further weakening the Swiss case against charges that its self-described neutral wartime conduct prolonged World War II. A8
 
Protest Broken Up in Kinshasa
Soldiers in Kinshasa broke up a small, peaceful demonstration against President Laurent Kabila, arresting and beating dozens of protesters and firing guns in the air. Far fewer people turned out to support the main opposition leader, Etienne Tshisekedi, than had been expected. A13
 
NATIONAL A16-19, B9-10
 
Bombing Jury Hears Tape As Defense Case Ends
Timothy J. McVeigh's defense lawyers closed their case by playing a tape on which a major Government witness, Michael J. Fortier, boasted to his brother that he could "tell stories all day long" to Federal agents. Mr. Fortier told jurors that he and Mr. McVeigh had cased the Oklahoma City Federal building a few months before the bombing and that Mr. McVeigh had told him of the bombing plan. A1
 
In Texas, Shock at Being Alive
Texans who survived the tornadoes that devastated the small town of Jarrell said they were amazed to be alive. At least 27 people died in Jarrell alone and concrete foundation slabs were all that was left of dozens of homes. Two dozen people were missing. A1
 
United States v. Joe Camel
The Federal Trade Commission charged that R.J. Reynolds illegally aimed its Joe Camel advertising campaign at children. The agency is seeking to ban the widely recognized cartoon figure and to force the company to fund a nationwide anti-smoking campaign aimed at teen-agers. The company said it did not target under-age smokers. A1
 
Florida officials decided to sell off the state retirement fund's $825 million in tobacco stocks. They said they had become too risky as investments. B10
 
C.I.A. Records Destroyed
The Central Intelligence Agency said it could not fulfill its pledge to make public the files from its secret mission to overthrow the Government of Iran in 1953: almost all the documents had been destroyed or lost decades ago. A historian who was a member of the C.I.A.'s staff in 1992 and 1993 said he believed records on other major cold war covert operations had also been burned. A19
 
Earhart Imitator Arrives Safely
A Texas businesswoman flying a restored plane of the type used by Amelia Earhart completed a round-the-world flight. The pilot, Linda Finch, is believed to be the second woman ever to complete the path Earhart was following when she disappeared 60 years ago. A16
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-8

LENGTH: 1283 words

HEADLINE: MCVEIGH'S DEFENSE WRAPS UP ITS CASE AT BOMBING TRIAL

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

DATELINE: DENVER, May 28

 BODY:
Five weeks after testimony began in the trial of Timothy J. McVeigh, his lawyers today unleashed their final salvo against the prosecution's most powerful witness, and then rested their case.
The chief target on this final day of evidence was the credibility of Michael J. Fortier, Mr. McVeigh's old Army buddy, who testified for the Government two weeks ago that Mr. McVeigh had told him of plans to build a truck bomb and that both of them then cased the Federal Building in Oklahoma City a few months before just such a bomb demolished it. 
 In attacking Mr. Fortier today, the defense played recordings of a series of his telephone conversations that were wiretapped by Federal agents in the weeks immediately after the bombing, when Mr. Fortier himself was considered a possible suspect. In those recordings, turned over to the defense as part of the pretrial process, he boasted that he could mislead Federal agents and make a million dollars through book rights from his connection to Mr. McVeigh.
"I can tell a fable; I can tell stories all day long," Mr. Fortier said in one conversation, with his brother, John.
Not long after the jury had heard the tapes, Stephen Jones and the rest of the defense team concluded their three-and-a-half-day presentation of evidence, without having called Mr. McVeigh to testify. Both the prosecution and the defense are to make closing arguments on Thursday, and the jury's deliberations could begin as early as that afternoon.
Surprising many with its speed, the trial will have moved from jury selection to jury deliberation in slightly less than two months.
"Come to us tomorrow morning ready to receive this case," Judge Richard P. Matsch told the jury at the end of today's session.
The jurors are expected to be sequestered by Judge Matsch throughout the deliberations, in which they will determine whether Mr. McVeigh, charged with murder and conspiracy, is responsible for the worst terrorist attack ever on American soil: the blast that killed 168 people and wounded hundreds of others on April 19, 1995.
If convicted, he could face execution.
Today, in their final effort to punch holes in the Government's case, defense lawyers used not only four wiretapped conversations but also one witness to portray Mr. Fortier as a lying, conniving drug dealer.
Instead of the clean-shaven, business-suited witness who testified two weeks ago, the jurors today heard the slurred voice of a young, unemployed man who complained of back pain and who glimpsed gold in his connection to Mr. McVeigh, who not long earlier had been taken into Federal custody, charged with the bombing.
"The less I say right now, the bigger the price later -- there will be books, book rights," Mr. Fortier said to another Army friend, Lonny Hubbard, who called Mr. Fortier's trailer home in Kingman, Ariz., three weeks after the bombing.
"I'm the key, the head honcho, General Crank," Mr. Fortier chortled. "I hold the key to it all."
Apparently expecting to be called as a trial witness at some point, Mr. Fortier also joked, "I could pick my nose and wipe it on the judge's desk."
As Mr. Fortier's recorded voice echoed through the still courtroom, Judge Matsch sat sphinx-like, not batting an eye.
The witness called by the defense today was Deborah Brown, who has known Mr. Fortier since grade school and who employed his wife, Lori, at her Kingman tanning salon, the Beach Club, from May 1993 through August 1994. Mrs. Fortier, like her husband, was an important Government witness, testifying two weeks ago that Mr. McVeigh had told her that he planned to bomb the Federal Building.
Ms. Brown testified that she had bought amphetamines "5 to 10 times" from the Fortiers. "It ranged from $80 to $100 a gram," she said, "depending on how quickly they were trying to get rid of it."
She said the Fortiers were so poor that "their baby was on some kind of state assistance to get formula and diapers."
On cross-examination, the prosecution tried to minimize the damage to the Fortiers' credibility, getting Ms. Brown to describe methamphetamine as little more than pep pills.
It "gives me more energy," she said, adding that its use had never caused memory loss or hallucinations in her.
Ms. Brown said that through the Fortiers she had got to know Mr. McVeigh, who, during his intermittent stays in Arizona in the years preceding the bombing, regularly picked up his mail at a commercial post office in Kingman, two doors from her salon in a strip mall.
Ms. Brown, who now lives in Seattle, noted that she had no guarantee she would not be prosecuted for the drug use to which she testified today. Nonetheless, she said, "I feel Tim should get a fair trial, whether I incriminate myself or not."
Turning to television monitors for their final evidence, defense lawyers then played a videotape of a CNN interview conducted with Mr. Fortier one week after the bombing.
"My friend Tim McVeigh is not the face of terror that is reported on the cover of Time magazine," Mr. Fortier said in the interview, which Mr. McVeigh watched intently from his seat in the courtroom. "I do not believe that Tim blew up any building in Oklahoma."
Mr. Fortier has pleaded guilty to charges that include failing to tell Federal law-enforcement officials of the bombing plot, and faces up to 23 years in prison. But he is hoping for a lesser sentence after the trials of Mr. McVeigh and of Terry L. Nichols, also charged in the bombing.
In court two weeks ago, Mr. Jones, the lead lawyer for Mr. McVeigh, tried to get Mr. Fortier to say he changed his story a month after the bombing in an effort to win a reduction in prison time. Mr. Fortier replied then, "Before May 17, I was lying to the F.B.I. and to the media and other people."
After the defense rested today, the prosecution called three rebuttal witnesses in rapid succession. With the end of the evidence phase, the prosecution had called 137 witnesses, the defense 25.
The modest defense followed Judge Matsch's refusal to allow Mr. Jones to present theories of a larger bombing conspiracy that involved foreign and domestic terrorists but not Mr. McVeigh. The defense team then tried to show that the real bomber had died in the explosion, that sloppy work by the Federal Bureau of Investigation had tainted evidence and that the Fortiers were drug-taking liars.
"The evidence is now closed," Judge Matsch said shortly before noon today. Turning to the 12 jurors and the 6 alternates, he explained, "That means you have heard all the testimony you are going to hear in this trial."
In the bright summer sunshine outside the downtown Federal courthouse here, the parents of a bombing victim said they were pleased with the handling of the case by both the judge and the prosecution.
"Judge Matsch has done a fantastic job of controlling the court," said Charles Tomlin of Enid, Okla., whose son, Rick, was killed in the bombing. Referring to the handling of the O. J. Simpson criminal trial, Mr. Tomlin added: "He has brought justice back in the courtroom. He has stopped this Mickey Mouse stuff."
Mr. Tomlin, who is retired, said he had attended every court session since testimony began on April 25. "I didn't want anybody sentenced who was not guilty," he said. "Nobody was guilty until the evidence started flowing in."
Confident of a guilty verdict leading to an execution, his wife, Blanche Tomlin, volunteered, "I'd like to push the button."
A few steps away, another man from Enid, Mr. Jones, seemed to exude confidence in an acquittal.
"We have done everything we came to Denver to do," he said, his summer fedora set at a jaunty angle. "We've done our best."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Buying by Military Drives Durable Goods Orders
Orders for durable goods rose 1.4 percent in April, in part because of unexpectedly large orders by the Pentagon.
The rise, which was led by gains in transportation equipment, much of it for the military, erased about two-thirds of a March decline. But orders for electronic equipment slumped for a second month. [Page D5.] 

Dow Falls, but Nasdaq Inches Up
Stocks closed mixed, with the Dow Jones industrial average falling 26.18 points, to 7,357.23, even though gainers exceeded losers on the Big Board. A continuing rally in computer companies helped send the Nasdaq up 0.97, to 1,410.18, a third straight high. [D8.]
 
Yields above 7 percent on the 30-year Treasury bond lured away some investors from the stock market, though the bond's yield finished unchanged at 7.02 percent. [D15.]
 
F.T.C. Steps Up Joe Camel Fight
The F.T.C. charged that R. J. Reynolds Tobacco illegally appeals to minors with its Joe Camel advertising campaign. The agency contended in an administrative complaint that the company violated fair trade practice laws by promoting a lethal and addictive product to children, who cannot legally buy or use it. The company denies the charges. [A1.]
 
Florida's $61 billion public retirement fund will sell its $825 million worth of tobacco stocks because of concerns that the proliferation of lawsuits against cigarette companies and the prospect of tighter regulation make the shares too risky as investments. [B10.]
 
Seagram Cuts Time Warner Stake
Seagram has sold more than half its stake in Time Warner for $1.39 billion. The sale increases Seagram's war chest. It also means that a competitor is no longer Time Warner's second-largest shareholder. [D2.]
 
Novell to Cut 1,000 Jobs
Novell, the troubled maker of computer networking software, said that it would cut its work force by 18 percent, and it reported disappointing quarterly results. [D5.]
 
S.E.C. Case Against Mutual Fund
The S.E.C. accused the portfolio manager of the Parnassus Fund, a mutual fund that practices "socially responsible" investing, of the illegal use of brokerage commissions generated by the fund and of overvaluing a security in the fund's portfolio. [D5.]
 
Former Murdoch Aide Joins ABC
Preston Padden, a lawyer and former Washington lobbyist, was named president of the ABC Television Network, a month after ending his seven-year tenure as one of Rupert Murdoch's top aides. [D6.]
 
Donna Karan Plans Cost-Cutting
Donna Karan International said that it would cut its work force in the United States by 4 percent and reduce its annual number of runway fashion shows, in an effort to cut costs and bolster profits. [D2.]
 
Intel Fights Back Against Digital
Intel countersued Digital Equipment, which accused it of patent infringement two weeks ago, and suggested that it might curtail chip sales to Digital. Analysts said Intel stood to lose little in the dispute, while struggling Digital had jeopardized the quarter of its computer line that uses Intel's chips. [D4.]
 
LVMH Plans to Buy Guinness Stake
LVMH Moet Hennessy Louis Vuitton, the French luxury-goods concern, said that it planned to buy Guinness's half of a jointly owned distribution venture. The step could complicate the planned merger of Guinness, the British brewing and liquor company, with Grand Metropolitan, the British liquor and food giant. [D3.]
 
Politics and Mexico's Money Supply
The amount of cash in circulation in Mexico has risen, causing suspicion that the Government of Ernesto Zedillo might be priming the economic pump before critical elections. But analysts say that despite the pressure, the Government has not relaxed its economic policies. [D3.]
