HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Put Up, Shut Up, Hang Up

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
As the Arab-Israeli peace process continues sliding downhill, American officials say they may soon have to opt for one of two extremes: either "The Plan" or "The Phone Number." Either the President lays down, publicly, exactly what steps the U.S. believes Israelis and Palestinians must take to get the process back on track, or he gives them both the White House phone number, 202-456-1414, and tells them to call when they're serious. As one top White House official put it: "Either we step forward or we step backward, but where we are now is not working."
The temptation to walk is great. It would leave Yasir Arafat and Benjamin Netanyahu both standing stark naked before their own publics, without any U.S. mediators to hide behind or blame. But such strategic neglect would come at a price the U.S. may not want to pay. Here's why: 
 Mr. Arafat is not and has never been a democrat. He is at heart a typical Arab autocrat, for whom corruption, censorship and abuse of political opponents come naturally. Yet as a result of the Oslo process, Mr. Arafat has also presided over the creation of a Palestinian parliament, in the freest election ever in the Arab world. Palestinian elites were hopeful that as Mr. Arafat shifted the basis of his authority from leader of an armed struggle to leader of a peace process bringing Palestinians land and prosperity, he would be willing to -- indeed would have to -- tolerate more democracy within Palestinian life. In Jordan, too, King Hussein began fostering a freely elected Parliament and freer press to broaden his legitimacy for making peace with Israel and reforming Jordan's economy.
With the collapse of the Oslo process these baby steps toward democratization are being quashed, and Mr. Arafat and King Hussein are reverting to form. This is, in part, because both men had made huge bets on Oslo. As it crumbles, they feel increasingly politically exposed. Their critics are now out in full force, declaring, "I told you this would never work." And so their natural inclination is to silence all critics before they become a serious political challenge.
Mr. Arafat's outrageous arrest of one of the finest Palestinian journalists, Daoud Kuttab, is a good example. Mr. Kuttab's sin was that his infant Palestinian educational television station, a direct outgrowth of the peace process and the only real competitor to Mr. Arafat's official station, was broadcasting debates of the Palestinian legislature, another outgrowth of the peace process. The Palestinian legislators often criticize Mr. Arafat's rule. When things were going well he was ready to ignore much of it, but with his peace strategy in shambles he sees this sort of criticism as a more serious threat, and so he arrests Mr. Kuttab. (He finally released him Tuesday.) In Jordan, King Hussein, also feeling besieged, has been clamping down on the press and tightening rules for who can run for office.
Mr. Netanyahu loves to lecture the Arab states about how they must become democracies. But he is just trying to score propaganda points. Because Bibi is utterly blind to the relationship between the peace process and the process of internal change in the Arab world. Yitzhak Rabin chose not to build in Jerusalem because King Hussein wrote him and said such a move would badly undercut the King at home. With Bibi, King Hussein comes to Israel, asks him not to build in Jerusalem, and Bibi announces -- at a joint news conference with the King -- that he is going ahead anyway. Hello?
It would be absurd to suggest that the peace process is a cure-all for Arab politics. Democratization will only come to the Arab world after fundamental changes in Arab political culture. But the peace process helps promote internal changes, opens Arab societies and business to more outside contacts and puts some power in the hands of Arab democrats, like Daoud Kuttab. That is exactly why Syria's Hafez al-Assad fears the peace process. It's also why fledgling Arab democrats in Palestine or Jordan will be much worse off without it.
Which is also why, as much as I'd love to stick Mr. Arafat and Mr. Netanyahu with the phone number, that would be too easy on them. They might never dial the phone. The U.S. needs to come up with a clear-cut plan that engages every Israeli and Palestinian, presents them with some real choices and, ideally, enlists their pressure to move forward.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: New Jersey Daily Briefing;
Plan to Freeze Property Taxes

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
Assembly Speaker Jack Collins said yesterday that he planned to introduce legislation to freeze property tax rates for an estimated 212,000 low-income elderly and disabled homeowners.
Mr. Collins, a Republican of Pittsgrove, said those who qualify for the program, which would cost $18.5 million in the first year, would never have to pay more than they are paying this year. 
 State Senator Bernard F. Kenny Jr., Democrat of Hoboken and the assistant minority leader, dismissed the Speaker's initiative as an election-year gimmick.
"The Whitman fiscal policies have been disastrous for all homeowners who feel the constant pressure of upward-spiraling property taxes," he said, referring to the policies of Gov. Christine Todd Whitman.

HEADLINE: Restive Indonesians Find Little Hope in Vote

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: JAKARTA, Indonesia, May 28

 BODY:
Something happened on the way to the thoroughly engineered Government landslide that is widely expected on Thursday in a parliamentary election here in the world's fourth most populous country.
An often violent monthlong campaign produced an outpouring of disaffection with the 31-year rule of President Suharto, a tenure that has brought Indonesia increasing prosperity but little political openness. 
 Although the Government has sought to control every aspect of the vote -- restricting campaign activities, mobilizing Government workers, vetting candidates for all three parties and reviewing their speeches -- a widespread restiveness and frustration have made themselves felt in rallies, riots and a broad and illegal campaign to boycott the vote.
"I want to see change in Indonesia," Mohammad Feli, a 21-year-old art student, said as he eyed the soldiers guarding his alley here in the capital as the campaign ended last week.
"There is too much unemployment in Indonesia," he said. "I want justice and equality for everyone. I want human rights. I want a system like overseas, like in America."
But in an election-eve speech today, Mr. Suharto said democratic development in Indonesia would be "a long process." He asserted that a strictly controlled political system was necessary to assure stability and economic development.
Indeed, the riots that have shaken the country since last July, when mobs rampaged through Jakarta, appear only to have strengthened the Government's sense that Indonesia is too volatile to allow openness.
"What we have seen in the streets is the revenge of the poor," said Juwono Sudarsono, vice governor of the Indonesian defense college.
"A lot of my colleagues argue for American-style openness," he said. "They talk of the cut and thrust of democracy. Well, you get a real cut and thrust here, with people using knives to kill each other. Openness among the poor is like giving them a lethal weapon."
Elections in this nation of 200 million people, which the Government calls "festivals of democracy," are more ritual than substance, offering a rare forum every five years for political expression but producing little effect on the leadership.
The 500-member Parliament, made up of 425 elected representatives and 75 members of the military, is largely ceremonial. If Mr. Suharto, 75, chooses to stay on for a seventh term, as most people expect, he will be endorsed next year by a People's Consultative Assembly in which Parliament will be joined by 500 additional delegates selected by the Government.
And even if Mr. Suharto, known for his surprises, chooses to retire, there is no strong opposition figure in a position to replace him. When people speculate about the future, the guessing game is over who the President might select to carry on his legacy.
Over the years he has seen to it that no potential successor emerged. He has named a new Vice President with each election, and a chief virtue of the current incumbent, Try Sutrisno, is his blandness.
A year ago, when the leader of one of the non-governmental parties, Megawati Sukarnoputri, showed signs of presenting a real opposition, the Government engineered her ouster as party chief.
The split that resulted has in effect neutered her party, the Indonesian Democratic Party. The Government's election board did not approve either Mrs. Megawati or her supporters as candidates, and Mrs. Megawati -- the daughter of Sukarno, the first President -- has announced she would not vote.
But despite the restlessness born of his long tenure and of the country's stunted democratic system, Mr. Suharto has stated repeatedly that "we will not change a system that has proven effective."
Some foreign and Indonesian political experts suggest that the recent violence was as least in part a result of the frustration and political immaturity bred by Mr. Suharto's closed political system. An Indonesian election monitoring group that is not sanctioned by the Government asserted this week that the campaign violence resulted from "inadequate political room and inadequate socialization of nonviolent values and the accumulation of social frustration."
With shocking suddenness, a variety of local conflicts have exploded into convulsions of vandalism, arson and looting often aimed at the Chinese and Christian minorities, who are seen as symbols of affluence in Indonesia, the most populous Muslim nation. Banks, shopping centers, car dealerships and Government offices have been attacked, as well as churches and Buddhist temples.
In one of the worst incidents, as many as 130 people died on the last day of the campaign in the remote town of Banjermasin when they were trapped in a shopping center that was set on fire by rioters.
[Violence continued during the five-day cooling off period after the campaign officially ended. On Thursday, as the polls opened, the police in East Timor reported 12 campaign-related deaths the day before, Reuters reported, quoting the police chief, Col. Yusuf Muharam.]
But although the Government system has hardly changed over the last three decades, Indonesia has undergone broad transformations that are straining the social fabric.
The economy has continued to grow at 7 to 8 percent a year, and the inflation rate is less than 7 percent. Jakarta has swollen into a vast and increasingly modern metropolis.
But economic growth has brought sharp disparities in wealth, widespread unemployment and underemployment, rapid urbanization and social disruption and unredressed seizures of land for new factories, supermarkets, golf courses, housing developments and whole new towns carved out of the rice fields.
One of the main problems Mr. Suharto's Government faces is an income gap. A thin layer of society has grown very wealthy very quickly. A broad underclass remains very poor. In between, only about 18 million people make up a middle class that Mr. Juwono said might form the basis of a healthy democracy.
Another crucial problem is a generation gap. The violence and the most passionate opposition rallies have mostly involved the young and the poor, a fast-growing sector that some analysts say is the greatest threat to stability.
Of the nearly 125 million eligible voters, fully 20 million have reached voting age only since the last election. Many are unemployed and angry, with some 2.1 million seeking to enter the work force each year.
More than 80 million people are too young to remember the poverty, hunger and political chaos of the 1960's, when Mr. Suharto came to power. They did not experience the purge of Communists in 1965 that led to the deaths of 500,000 people.
They take for granted the near-universal schooling, health care and basic services the Government has provided over the last three decades.
Newly connected to the outside world through mass communications, they are demanding more say in local and national affairs. They are protesting widespread corruption, Government abuses and an often unresponsive local government and judiciary system.
These themes distilled themselves into a generalized mood of defiance and dissatisfaction in the final days of the campaign.
"We are the party of the poor!" a demonstrator in Jakarta shouted, waving the green banner of the United Development Party.
An unemployed bus driver named Ambrosius looked on from among the soldiers who guarded the streets.
"The future is gloomy," said Ambrosius, 47, who like many Indonesians uses only one name. "So many people have no job. There has to be change."
His friend Hilarius, who is also unemployed, saw little hope of that.
"Whoever is in charge, we are still going to be coolies," he said. "That's the reality. Change is going to take many years."

HEADLINE: Metro Business;
Job Increase Reported

BYLINE: AP 

 BODY:
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey said yesterday that the state would gain more than 10,000 new jobs over a two-year period, thanks in part to her business incentive program.
Flanked by executives of four large companies that are expanding in New Jersey, Mrs. Whitman declared her program a success. The program provides tax breaks and cash to companies. "This is what the Business Employment Incentive Program was meant to do: create jobs and increase opportunities for New Jersey families," she said.
Under the program, the state has paid 48 companies about $37 million. 
 Executives from Hoechst Marion Roussel, a pharmaceutical company in Bridgewater; KPMG Peat Marwick L.L.P., a business services firm in Bergen County; the Hewlett-Packard Company, which has a lab in Florham Park, and the Pershing division of Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette in Jersey City, the securities unit of the Equitable Companies, all said that the state's labor market and the incentive program were key factors in their decisions to remain and expand in the state.

HEADLINE: Skilled or Not, Immigrants Aid U.S. Economy

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Your May 27 editorial "Immigrants and the Economy" makes a number of important points, but its policy recommendations could undermine the goals you espouse. 
 You call for new policies that stress the admission of "highly educated" or "highly skilled" immigrants. Yet newcomers, especially in New York, integrate into the economy through kinship and family networks. Current policies stressing family unification have created the very portals to the economy that have produced the benefits you praise.
You note that despite their positive national economic impact, immigrants are costly to localities like New York. But this is true only in the short run. After a period of adjustment, immigrants and their children move up the economic ladder, creating new enterprise and filling economic voids.
Maximizing immigrants' contributions requires full integration into American society. Congress has focused only on internal management issues in the naturalization program and not on the slowdown that has more than doubled the waiting time for a citizenship application, to more than a year in most large cities. When immigrants want to contribute to their new country, we should welcome them rather than erect new barriers to full membership in our society.
 
GARY E. RUBIN
New York, May 27, 1997
 
The writer is director of public policy for the New York Association for New Americans.

HEADLINE: Help Congo Now

BYLINE: By Jeffrey D. Sachs and Robert I. Rotberg; Jeffrey D. Sachs, who has advised Poland and other countries on economic policy, directs the Harvard Institute for International Development. Robert I. Rotberg coordinates the institute's southern Africa programs.

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass.

 BODY:
The United States, France, Belgium, the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank helped create the disaster of Mobutu Sese Seko's Zaire. Today the West has a moral duty to help rebuild Congo, as the country is now called, even if the new leader isn't making the task easy.
So far, Laurent Kabila has shown little interest in sharing power with the opposition. It seems likely that he will rely more on direct military support from Uganda, Rwanda and Angola to consolidate his rule than on the allegiance of his countrymen. 
 Yet, all uncertainty about Mr. Kabila aside, this is the moment when well-directed Western efforts can have their greatest effect in nudging the new Government toward observing basic human rights, stabilizing the economy and restarting rudimentary public services.
Our good will should be demonstrated first; we can hope that Mr. Kabila's can follow. How do we insure that we aren't creating another Mr. Mobutu? All aid should be provided step by step, conditional on the gradual development of economic order, political freedom and the rule of law.
First, the United States should abandon its calls for early elections. These will have to wait. The experience of neighboring Uganda is instructive. Since President Yoweri Museveni came to power in 1986, Uganda has recovered from the most harrowing depths of violence and economic collapse to become one of Africa's fastest-growing countries, while improving its human rights record.
Restoring basic public order -- demilitarizing, inoculating against infectious disease, resettling refugees, rebuilding roads and power plants -- is a surer underpinning of peace and future political reconciliation than holding elections in the midst of chaos. This gradual approach has also worked for Mozambique, which was locked in civil war until 1992 and held free elections two years later.
The West must also work with the new Government to recover the Mobutu clan's stolen loot. Mr. Mobutu pocketed much of the $10 billion in loans he received over the years, yet Western governments, needing a cold-war ally, looked the other way. Now the West can seize and sell his real estate holdings in Europe and Africa, while European countries must step up efforts to freeze his bank accounts. Any money recovered should go to the Government of Congo.
The least of Congo's worries should be the servicing of Mr. Mobutu's debts. In the short term, the country should be given legal protection to avoid harassment by creditors and the seizure of its assets abroad. In the longer run, Congo needs assurances that the West will cancel most or all of its $14 billion in debts (almost three times its gross national product).
Debt relief in such cases is vital. In the early 1990's, Poland, for example, was destitute, so Western governments and banks forgave about $15 billion in outstanding loans; last year Poland had Europe's highest growth rate.
Congo also needs money to pay the police, buy vaccines, support war refugees and rebuild roads. The Government has no taxation system, and inflation has rendered the currency worthless. The World Bank and especially the Europeans -- historically the biggest meddlers in Zaire's affairs -- should provide the bulk of the hard currency of about $900 million in the next year. For its part, the United States should provide some money as well as food and medicine. It can also do more to help relief agencies fight disease.
There can be no illusions of quick progress in Congo. Its wealth has been pillaged for decades, and the Belgian colonizers did nothing to build the foundations for self-governance before they left in 1960. Today the Western nations, once agents of despair in Zaire, can help to start the rebuilding of one of the most troubled parts of the world.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Helped by Weak Data In Japan and Outlook for Euro

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar recovered from early losses to end mostly higher yesterday, strengthening on surprisingly weak Japanese economic data and growing fear about the credibility of Europe's planned single currency.
The dollar was further helped by a report from the Bridge News Service that Japan's central bank feels the Japanese economy is too fragile for an increase in interest rates. 
 "There had been speculation the Bank of Japan was on the verge of raising rates," said Stephen Jury, managing director of foreign exchange at the Union Bank of Switzerland's New York branch. "So the dollar gained."
Japan's interest rates have hovered at record lows for more than one and a half years, which has depressed demand for yen. But recent talk that Japan's economy is strengthening, coupled with rising yields for Japanese bonds, had encouraged optimism that the period of low rates was ending.
In New York, the dollar settled at 116.28 yen, up from 115.81 late Wednesday. The dollar traded at 1.6966 marks, up from 1.6943. The pound rose to $1.6404 from $1.6399.
Traders said the main impact on the dollar came earlier in Tokyo trading when the release of weaker-than-expected Japanese industrial production figures for April diminished optimism that interest rates would rise soon. The data showed production declined four-tenths of 1 percent from a month earlier. Analysts had expected an increase of seven-tenths of 1 percent.
The dollar was also supported by uncertainty over the outlook for the single European currency, the euro, scheduled to begin in January 1999.
Traders said the possibility of a Socialist-led government in France after parliamentary elections this weekend strengthened the view that the strict fiscal criteria for participation in the euro might be diluted.
Another reason for the uncertainty stemmed from an unusual dispute between the Bundesbank and the Government of Chancellor Helmut Kohl over revaluing gold reserves, which would help Germany meet the criteria for euro participation. The Bundesbank says that such one-time accounting maneuvers will undermine the euro's credibility. Mr. Kohl has said he will press ahead with the change anyway.

HEADLINE: In London, 2 Young Guys Sit Talking About Democracy

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: LONDON, May 29

 BODY:
Perhaps it was predestined that America's "New Democrat" President and Britain's "New Labor" Prime Minister would give fresh life to the idea of a "special relationship." For Bill Clinton and Tony Blair were having such a baby-boomer love fest today that Mr. Blair virtually issued a generational political manifesto on their behalf.
Standing beside Mr. Clinton at a news conference in 10 Downing Street's own Rose Garden, Mr. Blair ticked off subjects of agreement. The two men picked up on each other's thoughts and phrases. They lingered long on their efforts to pull their respective political parties away from old orthodoxies. When Mr. Clinton spoke, Mr. Blair nodded emphatically and smiled. 
 And, brashly, the 44-year-old Laborite, who brought his party its first general victory in two decades, proclaimed, "We agreed too, and have for some time, that this is a new era which calls for a new-generation politics and a new-generation leadership."
"This is the generation," he pressed on, "that prefers reason to doctrine, that is strong in ideals but indifferent to ideology."
Next to him Mr. Clinton, 50, grayer and softer spoken, looked strangely like the elder statesman.
Mr. Blair has been called a "Clinton clone" because of his embrace of unabashed moderation and his makeover of his left-leaning party. But the two telegenic politicians -- both lawyers who studied at Oxford -- reveled today in the frequent comparisons between them. At times their words were so similar that listeners were hard pressed to tell which man was speaking, if not for the contrast between Mr. Clinton's soft southern tones and Mr. Blair's staccato enthusiasm.
For example, guess which man said of his political thinking, "I don't think it's the end of ideology but I think it's the end of yesterday's ideology"? (Mr. Clinton said it; Mr. Blair nodded in agreement.)
The two leaders also displayed a meeting of minds on restarting the Northern Ireland peace talks. In a clear message to the Irish Republican Army, Mr. Clinton, who has staked much of his prestige on the effort to end the bloodshed, said peace talks "can only succeed if there is an unequivocal cease-fire in word and deed."
Mr. Clinton saw Mr. Blair on Tuesday in Paris when they were among 17 leaders signing an agreement between Russia and the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. But the President is a politican who loves a good campaign, and he has paid close attention to Labor's landslide victory, so he asked for the full-scale meeting with Mr. Blair on his way back to Washington.
Mr. Blair's troops and the Clintonites have been cross-pollinating for years as each sought to drag their political party into a post-ideological center: Jonathan Powell, Mr. Blair's chief of staff, watched the 1992 Presidential campaign as an official of the British Embassy in Washington; another aide to Mr. Blair showed up in San Diego last summer to watch the mechanics of a Presidential debate; Stanley Greenberg, Mr. Clinton's 1992 campaign pollster, does surveys for Labor.
But aside from their moments on Tuesday in Paris, this was their first meeting since an April 1996 session in the Oval Office. It was also Mr. Clinton's first chance to sample the energy of the Labor Government, which has taken off with a much faster start and sweeter honeymoon than the President in 1993 when he too was being hailed as the emblem of generational change.
When a British reporter reminded Mr. Clinton that he was "rather unpopular" in his first term after a short honeymoon, Mr. Clinton said wrly, "It was a brief honeymoon; it lasted about 35 seconds."
Mr. Blair showed his admiration for the President by shepherding him into a session of the Cabinet, an honor last accorded to President Nixon in 1969. After Mr. Blair professed his admiration for the "courage and strength, the leadership" of Mr. Clinton, his ministers pounded the table, shouting "Hear! Hear!"
Mr. Clinton declined to give the Cabinet any tips, well aware that Labor came into office with a huge mandate, which he never achieved in either of his victories. "I think they're doing very well," he said. "I'd like to have a 179-seat majority, and I'm not going to give any advice. I'm going to sit here and take it as long as they'll let me do it."
To which Mr. Blair, solicitous of Mr. Clinton, replied, "And I would like to make sure that we have a second term in office." Then he shooed journalists from the room.
Later he and Mr. Clinton dismissed their aides so they could meet alone for about an hour before they had a working lunch. Hillary Rodham Clinton and Cherie Blair, both lawyers too, went to see the Globe Theater, a copy of the one from Shakespeare's days. The two couples met again for dinner at one of London's fanciest restaurants, Le Pont de la Tour. The crowds on the street shouted "Tony!" as the foursome passed.
The strange thing about the day was that for once Mr. Clinton, silver haired and subdued in the face of Mr. Blair's triumphant youth, no longer seemed quite so fresh. His cane slowed his pace, and sometimes, as Mr. Blair talked animatedly, the President appeared tired.
He must have known it too, for he made a gentle joke when a reporter asked if he was sick of being compared to the Prime Minister: "Yes, I'm sick of it, because he's seven years younger than I am and has no gray hair."
On the economy, both Mr. Blair and Mr. Clinton contended that their parties must stand on a platform of free markets, economic growth and reins on spending.
"I want us to be in a position, as the progressive party, where we can't launch a big new program unless we raise the money for it or grow the economy to fund it," the President said.
Mr. Blair agreed: "The progressive parties of today are the parties of fiscal responsibility and prudence."
The two men also discussed ways to accelerate reconstruction of Bosnia. Mr. Clinton warned that the efforts to stablize the country were running behind schedule.
"We can't play around with this," he said. "We can't just sort of hang around and then disappear in a year and expect the Dayton process to go forward. We have a lot of work to do in the next year."
But he sidestepped a question on whether American troops stationed in Bosnia would leave a year from now, as he has promised.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 4; National Desk 

LENGTH: 490 words

HEADLINE: U.S. Moves to Prohibit Home-Loan Abuses

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29

 BODY:
In its latest effort to eliminate fraud in Federal housing programs, the Department of Housing and Urban Development proposed a new regulation today to prohibit building contractors from arranging home-improvement loans.
Secretary of Housing, Andrew M. Cuomo, said the department had received hundreds of complaints in recent months from people who had applied for Government-insured homeowner loans through contractors. The homeowners said the contractors steered them to high-interest loans, performed shoddy work, falsified documents and circulated deceptive advertising. 
The Federal Government insures loans under Title I, a 63-year-old program intended to help low-income people who would otherwise have trouble securing a private loan for renovations and repairs.
Mr. Cuomo said that from 1987 through last year, 43 percent of Title I loans had been made through contractors, and the Housing Department paid out $114 million as a result of defaults on those loans. That rate, he said, is almost twice as high as the rate when homeowners borrowed directly from lending institutions.
Over all, the Government insures about 100,000 Title I loans a year for amounts up to $25,000.
Under the proposed regulation, which must be reviewed by Congress, contractors would be barred from playing any role in helping homeowners secure a Title I loan.
"We've reviewed this program and found a high level of fraud and waste," Mr. Cuomo said. "This is a crime waiting to be perpetrated, and we have promulgated regulations that will end the program."
The Title I initiative is the latest in a series of recent efforts by the Housing Department to eliminate the kind of fraud and abuse that has marred the department's reputation and caused some members of Congress to call for its elimination.
Since his confirmation in February, Mr. Cuomo has promised action against landlords of subsidized housing who allow their properties to decay and against marketers who take a percentage of second-mortgage loans that are otherwise available without a middleman.
Mr. Cuomo said department officials had recognized problems with the Title I program for years and had often appealed to Congress to have the regulations changed. But each time, he said, lawmakers said they would rather see the Housing Department make changes than pass any new laws to prevent contractors from helping with loans.
"What we're saying is, enough is enough," Mr. Cuomo said. "Let's end it and stop the hemorrhaging."
In announcing the proposed change, Mr. Cuomo also said that the department was barring four contractors in Texas from conducting business with the Federal Government and barring 10 other contractors in five states from participating in the Title I program for a year. The 10 are also being fined $5,500 each.
Mr. Cuomo said that additional enforcement actions would be taken against other contractors as the department continued its review.

SECTION: Section B; Page 5; Column 3; Metropolitan Desk

HEADLINE: Kravis Fund Invests $2 Million With Hospitals

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
A venture capital fund established last year by the financier Henry R. Kravis to encourage job growth and economic development in New York is investing $2 million with two distressed New York City hospitals to help them go head-to-head with giant health maintenance organizations like Oxford and U.S. Healthcare.
Health care experts believe the investment, with Jamaica Hospital Medical Center in Queens and Brookdale University Hospital Medical Center in Brooklyn, represents the first time in the region that private funds are being used to help struggling nonprofit urban hospitals aggressively enter the profit-making market of H.M.O.'s. 
 But the investment, the single largest since the $58 million fund was announced last year, also provides a dramatic illustration of how Mr. Kravis's exercise in capitalist civics -- using the tools of the market to foster economic growth -- is tearing down many of the old walls between private profit and the public good and between the worlds of charity and capital.
The administrators from the two hospitals say, for example, that they would use any profits to improve health care in the poor neighborhoods they serve: Jamaica in Queens, and Bushwick and East New York in Brooklyn.
But the New York City Investment Fund, which Mr. Kravis runs as an affiliate of the New York City Partnership and Chamber of Commerce, also stands to make a profit if the Brookdale-Jamaica project goes well, as does Mr. Kravis himself. A company Mr. Kravis controls, Merit Behavioral Care Corporation, owns 50 percent of the business that got the loan, Royal Health Care.
The loan will also benefit Royal's sister company, Neighborhood Health Providers, which is wholly owned by the hospitals.
Mr. Kravis said his initial impulse was to reject the loan proposal because of the appearance of a conflict of interest. But he said that fund administrators and investors agreed that Royal was a near-perfect opportunity: it had strong potential growth -- up to 850 new jobs in the next five years, from about 100 now -- and was located in an area of the city where banks and traditional venture capitalists would probably not go.
Some large private health management companies, including Oxford, have also been inching away from the Medicaid market in New York, saying that reimbursement rates set by the state are too low. That is creating an opportunity -- both economically and socially -- fund administrators said, to encourage an expansion by a company that has as its central mission the serving of poorer residents.
Mr. Kravis ultimately recused himself from consideration of the loan, and said that the terms were structured so that any profits would flow up to Merit only after the hospitals' $2 million obligation to the investment fund was repaid.
The fund is charging 11.5 percent on the $2 million loan, payable over four years. It also has an option to acquire a 25 percent stake in Royal Health Care. Neighborhood Health Providers, which is a profit-seeking H.M.O. for Medicaid patients, will remain owned by the hospitals.
The chief executive officer of the fund, Kathryn Wylde, said that the potential conflict of interest -- which was dealt with openly by the fund, and mentioned in the news release announcing the hospital loan -- is positive in that it underlines one of the key premises of the investment fund: that there is private profit to be gained in investing in poorer New York neighborhoods.
"It says that investing in New York City can be good business," she said.
Neighborhood Health Providers now has nearly 11,000 patients in Brooklyn and Queens, but with new rules that require Medicaid recipients to get their health care through an H.M.O., the company faces a potential client base of 200,000 Medicaid patients, company officials said.
Royal Health Care specializes in providing medical management services, aiming particularly at the physician groups that are springing up under managed care. Fund officials said this new and still emerging niche of health care also probably has the greater job-growth potential of the two.
But health care analysts say the new venture faces major obstacles that are purely a result of the marketplace.
Kenneth S. Abramowitz, a health care analyst at Sanford C. Bernstein & Company, estimated that only about 1 percent of hospitals in the nation start their own H.M.O.s, and that most fail because the agendas of H.M.O.s and hospitals are simply too different. H.M.O.'s generally measure success, and revenues, by controlling costs, which can mean, for example, shorter hospital stays. And that hurts hospitals.
The president and chief executive officer of the two managed-care companies, Steven Bory, said he was optimistic that a balance could be found. All the participants, he said, have agreed that the central priority is maintaining the health of the two hospitals and continuing their work in New York's poorer neighborhoods.
"When profits are generated, they will flow back to the nonprofit institutions, and then be reinvested back into the community," Mr. Bory said. "It's kind of a Robin Hood thing."

GRAPHIC: Photo: David Rosen, right, who heads Jamaica Hospital, with Steven Bory, who runs Royal Health Care and Neighborhood Health Providers. (Andrea Mohin/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NatWest Unit Threatens to Go to Stamford

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
NatWest Markets North America, the investment banking division of NatWest Group, the British financial institution, is threatening to move its headquarters and 500 employees to Stamford unless the city gives it a package of tax breaks and other incentives to move from Wall Street to a luxurious midtown office tower.
NatWest, which sold its building at 175 Water Street in lower Manhattan, has focused on a move to some of the most expensive office space in midtown Manhattan: 300,000 square feet at the top of the former Exxon Building, at 1251 Avenue of the Americas between 49th and 50th Streets, according to real-estate brokers in New York and Connecticut, city officials and executives at NatWest. 
 The investment bank has also explored several sites in Stamford and talked to Connecticut officials about providing an unspecified incentive package if the company relocates.
The Giuliani administration is negotiating with NatWest about providing a multimillion-dollar tax break if the company stays in New York.
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, like Mayors Edward I. Koch and David N. Dinkins, has granted hundreds of millions of dollars in tax breaks to retain companies. Some urban planners, good government and conservative antitax groups complain that the deals forgo scarce tax revenues while doing little to change the economic conditions of the region.
Some critics said they were particularly dismayed about a possible deal with NatWest because it would subsidize a move to a thriving midtown market and possibly undermine Mayor Giuliani's policy of using tax incentives and zoning changes to revive lower Manhattan, where more than 18 million square feet of space sit empty.
"Trying to pick winners and losers by doling out taxpayer-funded goodies is not only wasteful, but it's a losing economic strategy," said Brian Backstrom, vice president of Change-NY, a nonprofit antitax group. "In this instance, it's particularly shameful because it flies in the face of a stated economic policy to rejuvenate the downtown area. It stands as a clear example of what is so wrong about the government being in the business of offering corporate welfare."
The city's Economic Development Corporation, which is negotiating with NatWest, declined to comment. Charles A. Gargano, the State Commissioner of Economic Development, said the issue was "jobs," although he could not discuss the specifics of the NatWest deal. "Uptown or downtown, New York City is where NatWest belongs," he said.
Michael Alsop, president of NatWest Markets North America, stressed that "no deal has been done with the city's Economic Development Corporation," although the company had made the decision to leave downtown for either Connecticut or elsewhere in New York.
"Connecticut is an attractive option, especially for investment banks," Mr. Alsop said. "They are offering very attractive incentives. There has to be, in my mind, a very good incentive for our business to stay in New York City."
NatWest Group sold its retail banking unit to Fleet Financial Group for $3.5 billion last year and bought Greenwich Capital Holdings, a bond trading firm in Connecticut.
According to brokers, rents for 300,000 square feet downtown might run $8.7 million a year but would jump to $15 million at 1251 Avenue of the Americas. "It's a trophy property," Michael T. Cohen, president of Williams Real Estate, said. "I'd guess they're not paying a heck of a lot less than $50 a square foot."
Privately, city officials familiar with the NatWest negotiations denied that the incentives were corporate welfare. They say there is a real possibility that NatWest will move to Stamford. The company already has 500 employees in Greenwich, Conn., at Greenwich NatWest.
"This is not about making somebody rich," one person familiar with the negotiations said. "It's about trying to keep somebody."
According to local real-estate brokers and elected officials, NatWest has investigated two different development sites and an existing building in Stamford, but they said they believed NatWest would stay in Manhattan. "We are further along and are having more substantive discussions with other companies," said Dannel P. Malloy, the Mayor of Stamford, who declined to be more specific about NatWest.
It has become customary for companies looking for new space to take advantage of what public officials describe as the "border wars" among Connecticut, New Jersey and New York, as each state dangles tax incentives to retain companies or lure new jobs.
Last month, the Giuliani administration granted the Standard & Poor's Corporation an incentive package totaling $34.5 million to remain in lower Manhattan, after the company rejected what was described as a $50 million offer to move to Jersey City.
"The city is getting a reputation as soft a bargainer with business as it is with municipal labor," said Ray Horton of the Citizens Budget Commission. "The city better figure out what its policy is. They've had a policy of seeking investments in the Wall Street area. Now there's a possible subsidy to get a firm out of Wall Street."
Last year, NatWest sold its building at 175 Water Street to the American International Group, which received a $55.7 million subsidy package from the city.
The current downtown revival remains fragile, with the office vacancy rate hovering above 17 percent and asking rents averaging a modest $25.19 per square foot, according to Cushman & Wakefield, a real-estate company. In contrast, the midtown market is $37 per square foot and the vacancy rate has fallen to less than 10 percent. Moreover, NatWest's potential new home, the former Exxon Building, is in one of the priciest towers because of its prime location.
"They've put the city in a tough position," Mr. Cohen said of NatWest. "On the one hand, there is no need for economic subsidies for companies to move to the Exxon Building. On the other hand, the city is involved in a game of chicken that could cost it a lot of jobs."

GRAPHIC: Photo: NatWest sold its building at 175 Water Street last year. It threatens to move 500 employees to Stamford unless the city helps it go to midtown. (Linda Rosier for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Texan Faces Big Challenge in Shaping Tax Policy

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29

 BODY:
The White House and Republican leaders in Congress were moving into the final stages of their negotiations to balance the Federal budget late last month, and Representative Bill Archer was out to protect his turf.
Mr. Archer, the Texas Republican who is chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, the crucible for all tax legislation, telephoned President Clinton, eventually reaching him in his limousine, and objected to the Administration's effort to include in the budget deal detailed tax provisions that would effectively strip Mr. Archer of his considerable power to shape the tax package. 
 Mr. Archer was also sending the same message to Speaker Newt Gingrich and the rest of the Republican leadership, participants in the budget talks said. He repeatedly warned party leaders not to agree to a deal, as they seemed inclined to do at times, that would tie the committee's hands in writing tax legislation. So insistent was he in a delicate phase in the negotiations that the Speaker icily told Congressional aides that he could replace Mr. Archer as chairman of the tax-writing committee.
Nonetheless, Mr. Archer largely succeeded in his campaign, persuading both sides to give him and his committee considerable freedom in dividing the money that the agreement allocates for tax cuts.
But now he has to face the consequences of that victory, reconciling Republican demands for big tax cuts with a deficit-reduction package that does not provide nearly enough money to meet all promises, and to do so in a package that Mr. Clinton is willing to sign.
"The budget resolution gives the chairman a pair of shoes that's one size too small," said Clint Stretch, who tracks tax legislation for Deloitte & Touche, the accounting firm. "He'll get them on, but they're not going to be comfortable."
Much the same challenge faces Mr. Archer's counterpart in the Senate, William V. Roth Jr., the Delaware Republican who is chairman of the Finance Committee.
For Mr. Archer, 69, who is in his 14th term representing a district in Houston that is among the nation's wealthiest, the coming months will provide the best opportunity yet to put his stamp on the nation's tax laws. With Mr. Gingrich politically weakened and no longer able to dictate strategy to committee chairmen as he once did, Mr. Archer will have his first real opportunity to act in the tradition of past chairmen of the Ways and Means Committee.
That will involve wheeling and dealing to assemble a coalition for tax changes that affect the economic well-being of the general public and countless special interests.
Although Mr. Archer's desire to cut taxes is deeply held, and his antipathy for the current tax code is considerable -- just after the Republicans won control of the House in 1994 he pledged to kill the income tax system entirely and replace it with a tax on consumption -- he said he was bringing a very pragmatic view to shaping the legislation this year.
"I've always seen myself as a realist," Mr. Archer said. "To make this happen, we have to reconcile the wishes of the majority on the committee with the President's signing off on the bill."
That is a lesson that he said he and the Republican leadership learned the hard way in 1995, when their ideologically driven program ran up against Mr. Clinton's veto power and his success in portraying Republicans as extremists.
Last year Mr. Archer played a role in shepherding through the Ways and Means Committee several major compromise pieces of legislation, including the welfare law, an expansion of health insurance and tax cuts for small businesses.
But there is little spirit of bipartisanship on the committee, whose ranking Democrat, Representative Charles B. Rangel of Manhattan, is light-years from Mr. Archer in background, style and politics, and objects to the budget agreement's tax proposals as favoring the wealthy.
Rather than working with the Democrats as a group, an undertaking that he said would be fruitless, Mr. Archer is seeking advice and perspective from each Democrat on the committee individually, clearly trying to fashion a bill that will appeal to enough of them to give the legislation at least the veneer of bipartisan support.
With the tax bill, Mr. Archer will be up against not just the usual political difficulties, but also some unforgiving arithmetic. The budget deal provides for up to $85 billion in net tax cuts over five years, consisting of $135 billion in gross tax cuts offset by up to $50 billion in increased revenue from eliminating tax breaks or extending existing taxes that would otherwise expire.
The Republican wish list -- $500-a-child income tax credits for families, a deep cut in the capital gains tax, expanding Individual Retirement Accounts and slashing estate taxes -- would cost $200 billion over five years in the form that Senate Republicans proposed earlier this year.
The budget deal requires the committee to make an effort to agree on an additional $35 billion in credits and deductions for college tuition that the President supports.
Mr. Archer is making the task more difficult by objecting to most proposals to raise the offsetting $50 billion in new revenue, saying the suggestions are nothing more than tax increases by another name. Although he supports a $32 billion extension of the tax on airline tickets, he said, he may not be able to support much more than an additional $7 billion or so in additional revenue-raising measures.
That means that the total five-year gross tax cut may be limited to $125 billion rather than the $135 billion that the budget deal envisions.
"There's no way all these priorities are going to be fit in at the levels originally proposed," said Representative Robert T. Matsui, a California Democrat on the Ways and Means Committee. "It's going to be a real challenge for the committee, and particularly for the Republicans, to prioritize these in a way that doesn't create a lot of hard feelings."
Indeed, each proposed tax cut has a strong constituency. The child tax credit is a favorite of the religious right, which has mustered considerable support for it. Economic conservatives see a deep cut in capital gains taxes as their priority. Small-business owners and farmers, groups with strong support in both parties, are pushing hard for significant cuts in the estate tax, which they say leads to liquidations of family businesses and farms when the owner dies.
"Obviously, I would have preferred a larger number to work with, because I would have hoped to give tax relief to an even bigger group of Americans," Mr. Archer said. "But remember, this is the first time we've had tax reduction as part of deficit reduction in history. Our goal has to be to design a bill that will be accepted as the best we could do, and our best is going to be pretty good."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Representative Bill Archer of Texas, in a 1996 photograph. (Laura Patterson/Roll Call)

Without committing to details, the negotiators' initial positions included some versions of the following:
 
Credit for Families with Children
$105 billion -- Grant a new $500 tax credit for each child under age 18, to be phased out for joint filers with incomes above $110,000.
$46 billion -- Set lower income and age limits for phasing out the credit, and starting the credit at $300 per child, increasing to $500 in 2000..
 
Capital Gains Reduction
$26.4 billion -- Cut the top capital gains rate to 19.8 percent from 28 percent and to no longer factor in inflationary measures when calculating capital gains tax, starting in 1997.
$1.5 billion -- Reduce capital gains tax on the sale of a principal residence only.
 
College Tax Relief
$35 billion -- Grant a $1,500 credit for the first two years of college and allow deductions of up to $10,000 for tuition expenses subject to income limits. The proposals have almost no Republican support.
 
Estate Tax Relief
$18.4 billion -- Increase limit for exempting estates from Federal taxation from the current $600,000 to $1 million and to provide additional exclusions for family-owned businesses and farms.
$0.7 billion -- Reduce estate taxes for small businesses only.
 
Individual Retirement Accounts
$31.2 billion -- Vastly expand the I.R.A. program.
$5.5 billion -- A more limited plan.
 
(Source: Joint Committee on Taxation; Budget of the United States Government, 1998)

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Rise, Aided by a Good Auction

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Treasury securities rose yesterday, helped by foreign investors' overnight purchases and reinforced by an auction of five-year notes that went better than expected.
European and Japanese investors were active buyers of American Treasuries overnight, as recent events in both regions made the local bond markets less attractive relative to the United States market. 
 In Japan, signs that the economy was not rebounding as quickly as some had thought reduced the chances that interest rates there would be increased anytime soon, keeping the spread wide between Japan's low rates and those in the United States. In Germany, a dispute between the Government and the central bank over the Government's plan to revalue gold reserves to help meet criteria for European monetary union made money managers uneasy. And in France, the possibility of a victory by the left in elections this Sunday raised further concerns about the European currency union.
The 30-year bond rose 20/32 yesterday to a price of 95 21/32 , and the yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, fell to 6.97 percent, from 7.02 percent on Wednesday.
Contributing to the rebound yesterday, which followed several days of weakness, was the auction of $12 billion in five-year notes. There had been worries ahead of the auction about the results because an auction of two-year notes on Wednesday did not go well.
The fears proved unfounded, with the five-year issue awarded at a high yield of 6.616 percent, down from 6.745 percent last month and the lowest since 6.359 percent in February.
Despite the strong demand yesterday, analysts expect the Treasury market to bounce around considerably until next Friday, when the May employment report is issued.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said that concerns about interest rates remained, even though the Federal Reserve refrained from raising rates last week at the meeting of its Federal Open Market Committee.
While inflation has remained benign and economic growth has apparently moderated, he said, "an inflation-wary and pre-emptive F.O.M.C. is poised to tighten once again if market conditions become more conducive for a less accommodative policy."
"Indeed," Mr. Fine said, "economic data released thus far this week strongly suggest that while the recently rapid rate of the economic advance is most probably an overstatement of the longer-term trend, a recession or sharp downturn in the pace of business activity is simply not on the radar screen." Reports this week on consumer confidence and durable goods orders have showed strength in the economy, he said, though perhaps not enough to alarm the market.
But a report yesterday that initial claims for unemployment benefits were unchanged in the most recent week showed that the labor market continues to be tight. A number of other economic reports are scheduled for release today, including a revision of gross domestic product for the first quarter and new-home sales for April.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new five-year notes:

HEADLINE: Insurer Is Said To Near a Deal For Scudder

BYLINE: By PETER TRUELL 

 BODY:
The Zurich Insurance Group is close to acquiring Scudder, Stevens & Clark, the money management firm, for an estimated $1.5 billion, according to people familiar with the firm's talks with potential buyers.
With the prospective acquisition, the big Swiss insurance company would be trying to achieve two goals: broaden its asset management expertise -- particularly in developing countries and in the American bond markets where Scudder's strengths lie -- and provide a complement to its Zurich Kemper Investments subsidiary that would help bolster that unit's performance, the people with knowledge of the talks said. 
 "There are discussions going on to form a strategic alliance to create a world-class investment organization, which would offer clients Kemper's brokerage capability, Scudder's asset management and Zurich Insurance's global reach," said one person familiar with the matter.
Zurich Insurance completed its acquisition of the Kemper Corporation of Chicago in January 1996 for about $2 billion, but it is still trying to improve the performance of that company. Scudder, which is based in New York, is privately owned by its senior 200 executives. Altogether, it has about 2,500 employees.
A spokesman for Zurich Kemper Investments in Chicago referred questions yesterday about the planned acquisition to Zurich Insurance's head office in Switzerland. That office did not reply to messages left late yesterday. "As a matter of policy, we don't comment on these matters," Eleanor Mascheroni, a spokeswoman for Scudder in New York, said late yesterday.
Scudder, which has about $115 billion in assets under management, about a month ago retained Goldman, Sachs & Company to help it find ways to enhance the value of its business and also, perhaps, to find a buyer. With many big banks, insurers and brokerage firms scrambling to acquire large money management firms such as Scudder, there has been no shortage of suitors. One reason is that investment management helps assure more stable revenues.
Several institutions, including the Union Bank of Switzerland, the Chase Manhattan Corporation, the BankAmerica Corporation and the First Union Corporation have all been mentioned in recent weeks as potential acquirers or merger partners with Scudder.
The prospective sale of Scudder would offer further evidence of the increasing concentration in the asset-management business. A 1995 study of that industry by Goldman, Sachs, which is itself quickly building its own asset-management operation, predicted that there would be only 20 or so companies, each with more than $150 billion of assets, dominating the industry by the end of this century.
In a parallel trend, the asset-management business has become more capital intensive, and Scudder is apparently eager to find a well-capitalized partner with businesses that complement its own. Zurich Insurance fits that need, the people familiar with the takeover talks said, and also offers the Kemper brokerage network as a further potential distribution route for Scudder's mutual funds. Kemper is also stronger than Scudder in American equity markets, where Scudder is eager to expand its asset-management business.
On Wall Street, the largest firms, such as Merrill Lynch & Company, the Morgan Stanley Group and Goldman, Sachs, have all been swiftly building asset-management operations. Merrill, for example, acquired Hotchkis & Wiley for about an estimated $200 million last June, to build up its institutional bond management business.
Morgan Stanley -- which after its merger with Dean Witter, Discover & Company will have $270 billion of assets under management -- acquired Van Kampen/American Capital Inc., which had about $60 billion in assets under management, for more than $1 billion last year. It also bought Miller Anderson & Sherrerd of Philadelphia, which has $33 billion under management, in 1995.
Goldman acquired CIN Management, British Coal's pension fund manager, last July for an undisclosed sum that many analysts estimated at $65 million to $75 million. That transaction added some $23 billion to its assets under management.
Some are trying other routes. Citicorp in recent days said it was hiring Peter Carman from Putnam Investments to head its global asset-management business, and would seek to build its fund management internally.
And mutual fund companies themselves have become more acquisitive as they seek to build greater economies of scale: Franklin Resources Inc., for example, last year bought Heine Securities for an estimated $800 million.

HEADLINE: Tornado Toll Is Put at 30 as Fears Subside

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK 

DATELINE: JARRELL, Tex., May 29

 BODY:
The state authorities raised the death toll to 30 today as they concluded searches for the victims of a half-dozen tornadoes that ravaged Central Texas on Tuesday. Twenty-seven of the dead were in homes in a roughly two-mile square patch of land near this unincorporated community 40 miles north of Austin.
But the authorities said earlier fears that the death total would climb sharply had proved unfounded; many people who were counted as missing in the hours after the tornado struck here turned out to be alive and unhurt. Some had simply been out of town at the time, while others managed to escape the twister but were unaccounted for in the confusion after the disaster. 
 Surviving residents of the Double Creek Estates subdivision were allowed back in this afternoon to survey the scene and to look for any scattered belongings, a search that was mostly fruitless. Many who emerged said they were astounded at the completeness of the devastation, and some said they had found nothing but chunks of concrete near the sites of their homes.
This afternoon the National Weather Service officially upgraded the storm to its most severe classification, F-5, and said the tornado had carried winds up to 260 miles an hour as it ripped through a swath about 5 miles long and a half-mile wide.
Only four victims had been officially identified as of this afternoon, said Jimmy Bitz, the local justice of the peace. They were two teen-age brothers, a 5-year-old boy and his 44-year-old grandmother.
But almost everyone here in this small farming community, which also serves as a distant suburb of Austin, had an almost certain notion today of just who had been killed among its 1,000 or so residents on Tuesday afternoon. Nearly half of the victims were children or teen-agers, and at least one entire family was feared dead.
The members of that family -- Larry and Joan Igo, their daughter, Audrey, and twin sons, John and Paul -- apparently headed home, directly into the path of devastation, when they first learned that a tornado was bearing down on Jarrell. Just after a siren sounded here on Tuesday, Mrs. Igo left the Jarrell School, where she was a teacher, while Mr. Igo closed up his antique auto parts store near the center of town.
Mr. Igo drove with his sons and Mrs. Igo with her daughter to their house, about two miles away, said the Rev. Max Johnson, pastor of the First Baptist Church, where all five sang in the choir.
"They actually rushed home into danger," said Mr. Johnson, who said their house was soon leveled down to the concrete foundation.
The Baptist Church continued to function as a center for grief counseling as many people here waited for what seemed the inevitable, official word that a loved one was dead. At Mr. Igo's store, a note posted to the door took on an eerie tone; it came from a friend who pleaded for any member of the family to call him with word that everybody was O.K., and it offered a travel trailer as temporary shelter.
"They would not let me past Trudy's house to help," the note said. "Call me and tell me you made it. I'll bring you a place to stay."
At the same time, tempers were clearly fraying among people because they had been prevented from returning to their property by emergency officials.
"I've had it up to here with all this," said Al Clawson, who owns a recycling and waste-disposal company that was heavily damaged. " I can't get to my property. I can't run my business. The whole thing's a mess and I've had just about all I can take."
In addition to the 27 deaths here in Jarrell, 3 other storm-related deaths occurred closer to Austin, said Mike Cox, a spokesman for the Texas Department of Public Safety. The state continued to work on coming up with an official damage estimate, which it will then use to petition the Federal Government for disaster relief. In addition to the human deaths and the destruction of about 60 homes, the tornado is also believed to have killed 500 head of cattle and 250 sheep and devastated about 1,000 acres of farmland.
State officials said today that there was little that could have been done to head off the deaths and damage.
Gov. George W. Bush said in an interview: "We've had disasters since I've been Governor, obviously. Floods, fire. But you fight fire, and you try to fight flood, but the thing about this tornado, there's nothing you could do about it."
Al Dreumont, a forecaster for the National Weather Service, told the Associated Press, "It was too large to outrun and too strong to have survived unless you got away from the path."
Texas, largely because of its size, has more tornadoes than any other state, but most homes are here are not built with storm cellars or other protective shelters against tornadoes. Such shelters are much more common in parts of Tornado Alley, which includes much of Oklahoma and Kansas and where tornadoes occur more frequently on comparably sized land masses than they do in Texas, said Doug Smith, an assistant professor at the Institute for Disaster Research at Texas Tech University.
"It's not real common in the state of Texas to build" shelters, Professor Smith said. "It's probably a combination of things. There's a tradition not to build. Second is economics. A storm shelter or basement is comparatively more expensive to build. You have to remember the chances of being hit by a tornado is actually fairly small."
Here in Jarrell, disaster-relief officials say that they have been inundated with food and clothes for the victims and that they could now use financial donations instead. As survivors tried to assess the damage and return to some sense of normal, thoughts of the victims seemed to remain uppermost in their minds.
"I will never forget what it felt like that to have that storm pass over me," said Virginia Davidson, whose home was blown apart around her but who miraculously survived by crawling into a bathtub.
Mrs. Davidson added: "I thought for sure I was going to die, so I think I know what it felt like for those who didn't make it. I just hope it happened quickly."

NATO Majority Opposes U.S. on Expanding Alliance
The United States' desire to invite only three countries to join NATO ran into opposition from most of the alliance's other members. At a ministerial meeting in Portugal, only Iceland agreed with the American preference for offering early membership only to Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic. Many other NATO members also support Romania and Slovenia. A1

The day before, he had granted himself the authority to rule by decree without any form of legislature during the transition. A1

President Clinton met Prime Minister Tony Blair of Britain and attended a Cabinet meeting of Britain's "new Labor" Government. The two leaders warmly compared political notes, echoed each other's words and agreed that a new era called for a "new-generation leadership." A3

France's opposition Socialist leader, Lionel Jospin, campaigned with Communist and environmental candidates. He promised to reverse two years of conservative budget-cutting and tackle unemployment, currently at 12.8 percent. Public opinion polls indicated that Mr. Jospin could become the next Prime Minister after elections Sunday. A4

Voting was mostly peaceful in Indonesia after a campaign in which over 200 people were killed. With 64 percent of the vote counted, the ruling Golkar party of President Suharto was ahead of its own projections, receiving 74.42 percent of the votes. Voters were choosing 425 members to join 75 members of the military in a parliament to be complemented by another 500 Government-appointed delegates. A8

HEADLINE: U.S. NOW AT ODDS WITH NATO ALLIES ON NEW MEMBERS

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: SINTRA, Portugal, May 29

 BODY:
Just two days after NATO sealed a pact with Russia to ease Moscow's concerns about the alliance's expansion, the United States found itself sharply at odds with many of its allies today over how many nations from Central and Eastern Europe should now be invited to join.
Although Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic have long been the leading candidates, a majority of NATO foreign ministers -- 9 of the 16 -- made clear when they met here today that they favored including Romania and Slovenia as well, officials and diplomats said. Those countries were Canada, France, Belgium, Luxembourg, Italy, Spain, Portugal, Greece and Turkey. 
 As the ministers broached the matter directly for the first time over lunch at a golf resort here in the hills west of Lisbon, the United States found itself in the minority in supporting only the three front-runners, the officials and diplomats said.
Iceland was the only country to declare its support today for limiting the first group of new members to three. But the United States, Germany and Britain, while supporting three, left open their options, laying the groundwork for a compromise.
A larger group of potential members might complicate what Administration officials acknowledge could be a tough battle to win Senate approval because the American public seems reluctant to promise to defend many countries in Eastern Europe. It could also raise the costs of expansion, already estimated at $35 billion over the next decade.
The push for the additional members was stronger than expected, and the resulting division means that barely a month before NATO is to invite new members, there is no consensus on what the shape of the alliance should be. The United States led the drive to expand NATO and guided painstaking negotiations to win Russia's acceptance, but now finds itself advocating a slower expansion.
"It would be a mistake to say the consensus is on three," a senior NATO diplomat said. "The majority is in favor of five."
Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright said the United States had not yet decided how many countries should be invited to join when NATO leaders meet in Madrid in July. But an Administration official said the United States preferred "a smaller group" that had surmounted the "highest hurdles" of political, military and economic change. Slovenia's military is not considered ready, and Romania is considered a fledging democracy.
"You don't want to come all this way and take in countries that are not ready," the official said.
The ministers left it to Secretary General Javier Solana to try to build a consensus, but most officials believe a final decision will come only after Mr. Clinton and other NATO leaders weigh in, perhaps not until the eve of the summit meeting in Madrid, on July 8 and 9.
Russia, which has resisted the expansion from the beginning, has not expressed opposition to any specific number of new members, but has objected strenuously to any consideration of former Soviet republics, particularly the Baltics.
The divisions emerged as the alliance pledged today to strengthen relationships with countries that will not be invited to join, including the former Soviet satellites of the Baltics and Ukraine.
Two days after President Clinton and other NATO leaders joined President Boris N. Yeltsin in Paris to sign a "Founding Act" outlining mutual cooperation and security, NATO reached agreement today on a similar, though less sweeping, pact with Ukraine.
"The charter gives expression to the special importance we attach to Ukraine as a democratic, independent state at the heart of Europe," Mr. Solana said as he initialed the agreement with Ukraine's Foreign Minister, Gennadi I. Udovenko.
In her public remarks, Ms. Albright said any expansion needed to make the alliance "stronger and more cohesive" but also make it clear to those passed over now that future expansion remains a goal, as a way to encourage the nations of Central and Eastern Europe to continue to take steps toward democracy and market economies.
"We must have high standards for new members today," she said, "but we must also have a process that encourages other nations to meet those standards tomorrow."
Ms. Albright and others left open the possibility that the United States could support more than three members. "There has been no final decision either by the United States or the NATO alliance," she said in a news conference this evening.
The inclusion of Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic has never really been in doubt; since the end of the cold war, each has nurtured a thriving democracy.
Romania and Slovenia have been considered long shots for membership, but they have made significant strides toward democratic changes in recent years and have strong advocates making their case at NATO -- with France leading the way for Romania and Italy for Slovenia.
Each has mounted an intense lobbying campaign, winning support not only in Europe but also in Washington. This month, Senator Joseph Biden of Delaware, the ranking Democrat on the Senate's Committee on Foreign Relations, declared that the United States should support four new members, including Slovenia.
NATO members from southern Europe have said they believe including Romania and Slovenia would strengthen the alliance along the Mediterranean.
