HEADLINE: Wealth Whets Chinese Taste for Freedom

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER 

DATELINE: BEIJING, May 29

 BODY:
Just around the corner from where Democracy Wall was torn down more than a decade ago, a 40-year-old clothing merchant named Zhang perched himself on the stoop of his storefront and surveyed the explosion of commerce that has changed the face of China.
Twenty years ago, when Mao had the whole country roiling with politics, there was nothing here in the Xidan district but a pitiful stretch of markets; now the street is a canyon of department stores overrun with strolling couples as colorful as the previous generation that shopped here was drab. 
 "We don't talk about politics anymore," Mr. Zhang said. "Why should we? Who is our leader now? Money is our leader."
Asked directly about politics, the voluble Mr. Zhang turned cautious, refusing even to reveal his full name to a foreign reporter.
"You see those two policemen over there?" he asked, as he pointed to two patrolmen. "They could throw my behind in jail or kill me," he said, echoing the widely held fear in China of arbitrary police power.
"We don't have any freedom. We can earn money, but -- are you kidding, what freedom?"
The state of freedom in China today has become an important question in American politics, as President Clinton and Congress clash over whether to extend China's "most favored nation" trading privileges. By "engaging" China, Mr. Clinton believes he will encourage a long-term trend toward democracy and the rule of law, but his critics assert that a course of greater pressure, even confrontation, is necessary until China ends repression and undertakes genuine political reform.
Never in their history have the Chinese been as free to flourish, to earn, to invest and to move about their country building networks of commerce and opening markets at home and abroad.
Chinese officials, eager to end the long political isolation that followed the Tiananmen massacre of 1989, argue that the Chinese are running their own affairs as never before; peasants are taking part in village elections and China's rubber-stamp Parliament is beginning to assert itself.
Yet part of the great complexity of China today is that even as the Chinese are more and more unleashed, they are far from free and far from satisfied about the state of their liberties.
Across the country, many Chinese say in interviews that above all things they treasure stability so they can prosper, but they also seethe over the corruption and inequities that flourish today. They yearn for a system in which their voices could be heard, their grievances addressed, but often they do not know what such a system of government would be called, and many dare not say.
The death of Deng Xiaoping, China's paramount leader, in February has done nothing to clarify the prospect for political reform. Thousands of political offenders still languish in prisons and labor camps for challenging the corrupt or arbitrary acts of countless local Communist Party chieftains. But some experts believe that strong social pressures for change are building.
To fend off these pressures, Communist Party leaders are seeking to discredit democracy by equating it with chaos. But even as they do, the return of Hong Kong to Chinese sovereignty on July 1 will bring into sharp focus the different states of liberty in China.
Many mainland Chinese are already asking, if freedom is good enough for Hong Kong, then why not for us?
 
Liberty Divided
Freedom to Prosper But Not to Publish
For most Chinese, the state of their personal freedom must be measured against the dark years of the 1950's and 1960's, when hundreds of millions lived on the edge of starvation. Those were years of tempestuous political campaigns, the names of which howl through history with the voices of lives destroyed.
The "masses" were smothered by intrusions: Housing was assigned and essential coupons for food and clothing shackled every Chinese to state dormitories, communes or cramped factory quarters. Party committees controlled who could marry, who could bear a child or get an apartment.
Now millions of Chinese are on the move, more and more free to live and work near opportunity. The tyranny of the coupon system and neighborhood committees has collapsed or been replaced by free-wheeling private markets.
The work unit, the system that assigned every Chinese to a factory or government organization where his life was closely supervised and his file maintained, still dominates state industries and enforces state policies for hundreds of millions.
But in many families, one member stays anchored to a state job to retain cheap housing and other benefits, while others dive into the sea of private enterprise, driving cabs, running curbside eateries, beauty parlors and clothing stores in hopes of getting rich. And many are.
"It is true that many Chinese feel more free than ever before," said Wang Ruoshui, a former vice chief editor of The People's Daily and a leading advocate for democracy in the 1980's. "Today, if you want, you can dress like a pirate on the street and you can say anything you like; you can attack Jiang Zemin or Li Peng or the Communist Party as long as you don't publish your views," he added, referring to the President and Prime Minister.
"People have the freedom to earn money, and this is very important," Mr. Wang said, "but this is very complicated. The people have no opportunity to get news or to choose their leaders."
Sun Changjiang, a political reformer under the late Communist Party chief Hu Yaobang, offered a bleak estimate of Chinese aspirations for democracy. "The common people don't know what democracy is because their education level is so low," he said. "Even college students don't care about democracy now."
Yet many Chinese, especially party members and Government officials, have been indoctrinated to distrust democracy. Over lunch recently, a senior Government official, fresh from a mid-career course at the Central Party School, said what many top officials think: "Democracy is a luxury that China cannot afford."
"If we let the people loose, or start a general debate on what form of government we should have," he said, "it would be chaos, just like the Cultural Revolution."
About 30 miles south of Beijing, Huo Baoxian is the local Communist Party chief in Heixifa village, where local elections have been held three times in the last decade.
Heixifa displays the economic achievements of the Chinese peasantry. Along the dusty lanes, many new brick homes stand where mud-walled huts used to be and the bounty of the fall harvest bulges in each household storeroom. The peasants enjoy better nutrition and health care, and jobs have opened in township enterprises or on fish farms.
There is no democracy here, though there are democratic instincts even as the Communist Party's dominance in local affairs grows.
"In the countryside today, the party secretary is in charge of everything," said Mr. Huo, a bespectacled man in his early 40's who is a rather popular figure in the village of 260 households. "All the political moves are decided by the party before they can be implemented."
In his village, he added, the village committee members and the village chief are elected, but they must be nominated by the party.
 
Liberty Disguised
The Power to Vote But Not to Decide
In a country where all policy is made at the top by the Politburo and the Communist Party Central Committee, the significance of village elections at the bottom of the system is not that they are liberating the peasantry, but that they provide for improved local leadership and a measure of participation.
Village elections in China have done little to relieve the average peasant's burdens or eliminate arbitrary government intrusions. County governments levy taxes and fees and county family planning officers relentlessly enforce the one-child policy, while township and county police officials crack down on religious practice and unsanctioned political activity.
Yet in Heixifa, even the party secretary, Mr. Huo, displays a democratic instinct at times. "In my village," he said, "all major decisions have to get the approval from the people in the village. For example, when it comes to buying farm equipment, the villagers have to vote on it."
And when the village receives an order from above, the villagers do not always obey. "Sometimes, when we don't like a decision," Mr. Huo said, "we procrastinate on purpose and won't execute it. For example, the county pushed hard to build a fertilizer factory and asked our village to raise 100,000 yuan [$12,000]. We didn't like it, and finally our procrastination trashed the deal."
But these rights are not enshrined in any law. The farmers are more concerned about their land, their grain production and their taxes and fees. Ignorance is the greatest limitation on political development; even Mr. Huo recognized that and has started reading groups to raise the literacy level.
"I don't think the majority of voters realize that they are engaging in democratic elections," he said. "But despite the fact that the village election is only a form or procedure, we take it seriously."
So do many of China's intellectuals. But for now, there is no groundswell to take more than the incremental steps China is taking in the countryside, and in legislation that is creating the foundation for a rule of law that does not yet exist.
Many Chinese believe that it would take a reversal of China's economic fortunes, a sudden downturn in growth or a return of high inflation, to rekindle Chinese interest in democracy and political reform.
"An entrepreneur said to me once, 'As long as I am making money, what do I care about who is the prime minister?' " said Mr. Wang. "I am not optimistic about the future. Perhaps they will have more freedom, but not more democracy."
 
Liberty Discredited
Democracy Called A Road to Chaos
To Westerners, China from a distance often seems a bleak landscape of repression and human rights abuse, but at closer range, the vastness of opportunity incites an overwhelmingly different impression. Confronted with forests of building cranes and spanking new factories many visitors say the China they encounter does not resemble the image of China shaped in the West.
But political repression is always less visible than commerce.
The economic freedoms so visible today in major cities and along China's bustling coastline mean little to hundreds of millions of other Chinese, living in inland provinces where hunger and scarcity reign, or in old industrial cities where millions of unemployed workers and pensioners are shackled by subsistence.
Despite reforms in criminal procedures this year, there are few if any basic protections against arbitrary arrest and imprisonment. There is no freedom of the press nor any prospect for freedom of expression in the arts, film or news media.
The Ministry of Justice admits to holding more than 2,000 "counter-revolutionary" political prisoners, a number that has declined in recent years. But countless thousands of other political and religious prisoners of conscience are in labor camps and mental institutions.
In a heavily policed society, little has changed since 1979, when young intellectuals like Wei Jingsheng and Xu Wenli pasted up on Democracy Wall their calls for reform. That stretch of masonry was dismantled in 1980, and in 1982 big character posters were outlawed. Mr. Wei went to prison, where he remains today, and Mr. Xu is a political hermit.
In 1986 and again in 1989, pro-democracy movements erupted on the streets of Beijing, and each time the Government cracked down, most brutally on June 4, 1989.
Mr. Wang and other intellectuals who participated in the robust democracy debates of the 1980's have watched since 1989, as the Communist Party has sought to tar the concept of democracy as a viable system for China.
"The propaganda organs show fighting in the Taiwan parliament or they show poor Russian farmers and they tell the people: this is democracy," he said.
The people, he added, "have been misled to believe that democracy equals chaos in the streets so they don't think of June 4th as an example of students making a demand for democracy. They think of it as an expression of democracy" -- more chaos and riot.
In the early 1980's, when Mr. Wang served on a high-level party commission with Li Ruihuan, now a member of the ruling Politburo, Mr. Li interrupted a discussion on the merits of democracy by asking, "Do we still want more democracy? Didn't we get enough of that in the Cultural Revolution?"
These deeply ingrained attitudes, Mr. Wang says, prove that many Chinese have taken a negative lesson about democracy. "And they think it is useless to oppose the Government because you cannot change it."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Mutual Fund Inflows Rise; Dow Average Loses 27.05
With investors bullish, cash flows to mutual funds have gained strength in the last two months -- with inflows to stock funds reported at $15.7 billion in April and estimated at $19 billion in May. But fund managers have been cautious, leaving two-thirds of April's new stock money in cash. 
 Stocks fell yesterday, weakened by slack demand for technology issues and a disappointing earnings forecast by Nike. The Dow industrials lost 27.05 points, closing at 7,330.18. [Page D4.]
 
Nike shares fell 13 percent after the company said that its quarterly earnings would be below expectations because of disappointing sales and the shutdown of a factory in Canada that makes skates. [D3.]
 
Prices of Treasury securities rose, helped by foreign purchases and reinforced by a better-than-expected auction of five-year notes. The yield on the 30-year bond fell to 6.97 percent, from 7.02 percent on Wednesday. [D14.]
 
Former Nomura President Arrested Nomura Securities' former president, Hideo Sakamaki, has been arrested in connection with a widening scandal involving suspected illegal payoffs to a racketeer. [D14.]

Scholastic Cutbacks Bolster Shares
Scholastic announced cost cuts that include the elimination of 400 jobs, intended to save at least $25 million annually. The company's battered stock rose by 11.4 percent. [D2.]
 
2 Suitors Pursue Bozell
A sale of Bozell, Jacobs, Kenyon & Eckhardt, the world's 14th-largest advertising agency company, may be near as two bigger competitors, Omnicom and True North Communications, pursue acquisition bids. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D2.]

Another Shift in Marvel Fight
Toy Biz, a company at the heart of the battle for control of Marvel Entertainment, has changed sides again, backing away from an agreement with Marvel bondholders. [D4.]

20 State Securities Regulators Act
Securities regulators from 20 states, led by Attorney General Dennis C. Vacco of New York, filed lawsuits and took other actions against stockbrokers pushing penny stocks by phone. They acted just as Congress, responding to securities industry pressure, is attacking state enforcement efforts. [D2.]
 
Trouble at Boston Chicken Unit
Boston Chicken said that it was unhappy with results in its restaurant division and that the president of the unit, Laurence M. Zwain, had resigned. The announcement sent the company's shares down sharply. [D4.]
 
American Airlines Seeks Shuttle
American Airlines moved to buy the U S Airways Shuttle, but its bid is expected to encounter heavy resistance. [D2.]

Sybase Executive Leaves
The turnaround of Sybase, a maker of data base software that has been struggling to regain its former luster, ran into another hurdle when a key executive left. [D14.]

Kravis Fund Invests in Hospitals
A venture capital fund established last year by the financier Henry R. Kravis to encourage economic development in New York City is investing $2 million with two troubled hospitals to help them expand a company that will go head-to-head with giant health maintenance organizations. [B5.]

Suit Over Secondhand Smoke
A lawsuit brought by an American Airlines flight attendant and nonsmoker who has lung cancer could present another front -- the health consequences of secondhand smoke -- in the broadening legal assault on cigarette companies. [A14.]

HEADLINE: Excerpts From Closing Arguments in the Oklahoma City Bombing Case

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: DENVER, May 29

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the closing arguments today in the trial of Timothy J. McVeigh on charges from the bombing of the Federal Building in Oklahoma City, as prepared by the official court reporter and distributed by Pubnetics Inc. of Denver:
 
THE PROSECUTION
Larry Mackey 
 The events that were set in motion two years ago are drawing to a close. On April 19, 1995, a crime of ghastly proportions was committed. On that day a truck packed with explosives parked in downtown Oklahoma City filled with explosives. Only a wall of windows separated the unsuspecting children and women and men inside that building from the truck and the explosives that sat outside. The truck bomb exploded, the building gave way, and suddenly many lives were ended, and many, many more were changed forever.
America stood in shock. Who could do such a thing? Who could do such a thing? It's a question that began to ripple across this country coast to coast. And finally it's come to rest right here in this courtroom.
It's fallen to you as members of this jury to answer that question. Based on the evidence, based on what you've heard, the answer is clear. Tim McVeigh did it. Tim McVeigh and Terry Nichols in concert with each other planned and executed the violent attack on the Murrah Building and are responsible for the murders of those persons who died. . . .
We promised and we've proven, in more ways than one, a number of important factual propositions. No. 1, Timothy McVeigh, motivated by hatred of the Government, in a rage over the events at Waco, deliberately and with premeditation planned the bombing of the Murrah Building; No. 2, that he educated himself on how to build bombs; No. 3, that he enlisted at least one co-conspirator and attempted to recruit yet another, Michael Fortier, to help him in that criminal act and that he and Terry Nichols acquired and attempted to acquire all of the necessary components to build a massive bomb; that Timothy McVeigh carefully, very deliberately selected his target, the Murrah Building, he surveyed it, and that in April of 1995, he rented the truck, built the bomb and detonated it against the Murrah Building.
That's the Government's case. Promises made, promises kept. . . .
From all of that evidence, what is clear is that Easter Sunday, Tim McVeigh had staged his getaway, the Mercury, in downtown Oklahoma City; and most likely, most likely, given all the other evidence, in the direction of the Y.M.C.A. building.
So by Easter Sunday, the getaway car was in place. All the ingredients had been purchased. And the next day on Monday, the Ryder truck was picked up. And the next day on Tuesday, Tim McVeigh left the Dreamland.
In that time, between the time he leave the Dreamland on Tuesday, the 18th, and the time the bomb went off on April 19 in downtown Oklahoma City, he had time, plenty of time, to get those ingredients and to build the bomb with the help of Terry Nichols and to drive to Oklahoma City. He also had time to wash up and change his clothes, if he was so inclined.
The next day was April 19. It was the anniversary of Waco. It was Liberty Day, as Tim McVeigh would describe it, a day that Tim McVeigh had long been waiting for. It takes about five hours to drive from Junction City and that area down to downtown Oklahoma City, maybe longer if you're transporting something as sensitive as was in the back of this particular Ryder.
Minutes before 9:00 -- and you saw in this case the ghostly image of a Ryder truck appears just outside the Regency Towers captured by the video camera. It pauses by the curbside. We may never know why it pulled over. We may never know that. But we know that it vanished and left the scene.
McVeigh was driving that truck, just like Michael Fortier told you was his intent. You can't see it from the video, but you can see it from all of the other evidence in this case.
McVeigh lit the fuse and left the truck, left the truck separating only by a wall of glass that truck and the people inside.
At that point in time, nothing is going to stop this bomb. You cannot turn the hands of time back; and in fact, the hands of time fell to rest that morning at 9:02 A.M.
Over the four short years between that event and prior to that, Tim McVeigh had been at the helm of two weapons of mass destruction. In the Gulf War, he was a good soldier. He was a soldier who manned a Bradley tank, a vehicle -- a vehicle of mass destruction that's built for that reason. It's built to kill other people.
And on the side of that tank are the words, "U.S. Army" and an American flag. Those are words and symbols meant to declare its purpose, its killing power. That was one weapon of mass destruction that Tim McVeigh manned.
The second one was a Ryder truck, a Ryder truck that he delivered to the door of the Murrah Building on April 19, 1995. On the side of that truck was the name "Ryder," were the words "18,000 pounds" for its capacity and a 1-800 number. These are words meant to disguise the purpose that was packed in the back of that truck by Tim McVeigh.
That truck crawled to a stop in front of the Murrah Building on that morning, right in front of children and women and men; and then Tim McVeigh ran away.
The law enforcement officers that died that morning were not "treasonous" officials, as Tim McVeigh had declared. . . .
The credit union employees that disappeared that morning were not tyrants whose blood had to be spilled in order to preserve liberty. And certainly the 19 children that died that morning were not storm troopers that Tim McVeigh had said must die, innocent storm troopers who must die because of their association with an evil empire. . . .

THE DEFENSE
Stephen Jones
Now, the Government's position is that much of what Michael and Lori Fortier says is corroborated. Well, I suppose in a kind of a backhanded sense, that's true. But let's examine carefully here the story that they first told and then why they changed and then the story that they later told. . . .
In their community and with the beliefs that they had, Tim McVeigh was being persecuted by the F.B.I. just like they were being persecuted. His life was being disrupted and so was Terry's and so was theirs. And so they went out and did what they had done before: They defended themselves, and that's what they were doing. . . .
And then something happened. Now, what happened was Terry Nichols was arrested on May the 10th. But in the meantime, something else had happened. Mrs. Fortier, despite the fact they're writing press releases and rewriting them and holding press conference, she just wasn't interested in what appeared in the press, just totally oblivious to it except in one or two instances.
The reason that Mrs. Fortier had to say, No, I didn't really read them that much, and finally Mr. Fortier came around and said, particularly when it's on the tapes, that he knows about Time magazine, knows about The Arizona Republic, he knows about CNN and he's got his friends, Lonnie and Glenn and his brother John calling him and telling him that they've seen him. And you can hear the excitement in their voice. They know a celebrity.
But the reason they didn't want to admit reading it is because then, if I said, Well, did you read the story about how the F.B.I. thought it was carried in a Ryder truck; and then, Well, did you read the story the next day about they thought this Ryder truck was rented in Michigan; and then, Did you read the story that there was a false driver's license that might have been used; and then, Did you read the story there was ammonium nitrate, they could say, no, I didn't read it. Of course they read it.
And when it dawned on them like the great French play, "No Exit," that there was no exit for them, they had to come up with a plan. . . .
Everything about Terry Nichols applies at least to Michael if not Lori. Both of them knew Tim McVeigh, both of them served in the military with him at the same time, both of them shared the same political beliefs, both of them had similar type devices and stuff in their house, both of them had the same kind of books, and they were all linked together with Tim McVeigh. So first Terry Nichols is held as a material witness, and then on May the 10th, he's arrested and charged as an aider and abettor in the case.
Now, they knew by then, because they told you, they had seen the Attorney General and the President of the United States, and they knew that a death penalty was going to be sought in this case. So here they are, broke, unemployed, no money in the bank, no prospects, not even the money to hire a lawyer; and they know that the vise is closing in on them. . . .
So he leaves a message with the F.B.I. one early morning that he's interested in talking to them about a proffer. Now, he hasn't said anything that implicates himself or Tim McVeigh, he's just kind of out scouting as to what he can do. This is a man that still thinks he can have it his way. So they get subpoenaed to come to Oklahoma City, and they meet in a motel and they call some F.B.I. agents. . . .
And Mr. Fortier excuses himself from Mrs. Fortier. Now, that's kind of an interesting development, isn't it, because remember, I trust Lori, I tell Lori everything. Tim McVeigh wrote me, according Mr. Fortier, and said, Don't tell your wife; but I tell my wife everything. But, boy, the door closes behind him on this one. And Lori stays in the hotel room while Michael goes out, and what does he say? He doesn't say, We can give you Tim McVeigh; he says, I can give you Tim McVeigh. But I want immunity from prosecution. Now, even if he intended it to apply to both of them, what he wanted was I'll give you Tim McVeigh, but I don't want to be prosecuted. For nothing. Period. Nothing.
Well, the agents told him, Well, we don't have any authority to make that kind of promise or commitment. Would you like to correct your statements? I mean after all, that's why you called us out here, Michael. Would you like to correct your statements?
And then -- now they're back inside -- Mr. Fortier turns to Mrs. Fortier and says: You correct yours first. Greater love hath no man than he lay down his wife for his life. That is precisely what he was willing to do. But Lori -- remember, these are not stupid people -- says, no, you correct yours first.
And so he begins, quote, to correct his. But according to him, he is still lying. So he feels his way as he is engaged in this maneuver, and his wife has the good sense to keep her mouth shut. Chivalry died that afternoon in the Fortier home. That's for sure. Their lawyer, or at least her lawyer, shows up and tells her not to talk anymore, which is certainly appropriate advice in the circumstances; but she already knew that. And then supposedly because he has this great feeling for the people of Oklahoma, he starts cooperating.
Well, if you take all of these things that he said, 99 percent of them were already known to the F.B.I.; or if they weren't known to the FBI, they were certainly known to the press, because they were in the public domain. But nevertheless, he did serve a function of tying them up. . . . But those things that he says that aren't in the public press, such as, Well, now, you know Tim McVeigh wrote me a letter, said he wanted to take direct action. Well, do you have the letter? Well, no, I don't have the letter. And I wrote him back. Well, did you make a copy of that? And I agree it's not likely somebody would make a copy of a letter, but some people do. No, I don't have that. . . .
But my point is simply this: If you say that George Washington sat in that chair and there's the chair, it doesn't prove that George Washington sat in it at all. For any of this to have any believability, you have to first believe Michael and Lori Fortier.
Now, according to them, their parents believed them, their friends believed them; didn't have any trouble lying to them. Or were they telling the truth now [sic] and lying now? . . .
Now, when you add up all of Michael Fortier and Lori Fortier -- not small number, but principally Michael's criminal offenses, to plead guilty to no more than 23 years was Christmas in August. But he's not even facing the 23 years, because under the terms of his agreement, it can be reduced, or at least the Government can move for a reduction. . . .
Well, Mr. Fortier understands that he could be sentenced to a total of 23 years in prison. Well, if I were facing all the offenses that he was facing, I would think that was a pretty good deal.
The parties agree to seek a continuance of Mr. Fortier's sentencing until he has completed his cooperation and rendered his testimony. What that simply means is we got him. He has to deliver for us before we deliver for him.
Wouldn't it have been better if he had already been sentenced? Then you would have a position to know that he had no motive to lie; he had already been sentenced, so what he said wouldn't affect his sentence. But it's just the opposite.
The Government in this remarkable candor clearly says, We're not going to deliver till you deliver. So why are they still holding the Sword of Damocles over his head? Maybe because they don't trust him; and maybe you shouldn't trust him, either. Because they want to be sure he delivers.

HEADLINE: Africa Isn't Free to Develop Thriving Economy

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Contrary to "Tiger Tales: Why Africa Can Thrive Like Asia" (Week in Review, May 25), the Asian "tigers" (Hong Kong, Singapore, Taiwan and South Korea) did not develop by following free-market prescriptions, as you imply. They were able to take the fiscal steps you correctly praise -- control inflation, undervalue currencies, enforce savings and invest in education and infrastructure -- because their governments carefully regulated the economy, subsidizing fledgling local industries, controlling wages and movement from farms to factories, and stimulating agricultural and industrial exports.
In contrast, since the mid-1980's, most African countries have been subjected to development strategies imposed by the World Bank and International Monetary Fund that dictate that these countries lower trade and tariff barriers, cut government spending, remove subsidies and concentrate on stimulating agricultural, rather than industrial, exports. 
 As a result, local industries, unable to compete with foreign goods or investments, have closed; spending on education and health care has plummeted, and commodity prices have fallen even as exports have increased because many countries now export the same handful of crops.
Africans will not "thrive" -- and may not survive -- until the international financial institutions agree to substantial debt relief and stop imposing their structural adjustment programs.
 
MARTHA HONEY
Silver Spring, Md., May 26, 1997
 
The writer teaches a graduate course in African trade and investment at George Mason University.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Rift in Germany Helps Dollar Close Week Higher vs. Mark

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The dollar closed a volatile week higher against the German mark yesterday, bolstered by concern about plans for a single European currency and comments from a Federal Reserve official.
The dollar has gained this week on a dispute between the German Government and the central bank, the Bundesbank, over a Government plan to revalue the Bundesbank's gold reserves. The move would allow the Government to book a quick profit and thus sharply cut a budget deficit, putting the country closer to qualifying for the single European currency, the euro. 
 Yesterday, a Bundesbank council member, Reimut Jochimsen, said the Government's plan marked a "dramatic conflict" with the central bank.
"The market recognizes that the Bundesbank isn't going to take this gold revaluation plan lightly," said Brian McCarthy, a market analyst at Ruesch International. "So, whenever the value of the euro comes under doubt, of course, they sell marks for dollars and sterling."
In trading in New York late yesterday, the dollar settled at 1.7063 marks, up from 1.6966 on Thursday. The dollar slipped to 116.22 yen, from 116.28 on Thursday. The pound rose to $1.6410 from $1.6404.
Further support for the dollar emerged in comments by William McDonough, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, who said a strong dollar was in the interest of the United States.
The dollar also continued to gain over political uncertainties in France. The Socialist Party and its leftist allies go into a runoff this weekend after having scored an impressive showing in the first round of elections May 25. The Socialists favor a less stringent interpretation of the standards that must be met in order to qualify for monetary union.
"If the Socialists do get in, that could certainly have implications for French monetary policy," said Frank Pusateri, vice president of corporate foreign exchange at the Mellon Bank. "It could throw the whole European thing off track, presumably."
The dollar was also helped by a rebound in the United States stock market. Stocks tumbled on a plunge in Intel and other technology issues, but recovered to end little changed.

HEADLINE: Some Artistic Neighbors Try to Lower Their Fence

BYLINE: By MEL GUSSOW

DATELINE: HARRIMAN, N.Y., May 29

 BODY:
The future relationship of the arts and the world of commerce in the United States is under intense scrutiny this weekend by leaders of foundations and corporations, academics and others gathered here at a conference called "The Arts and the Public Purpose."
Among the diverse participants in the conference, sponsored by the American Assembly, are an expert in intellectual property law, an executive with the Nissan Motor Corporation, a California muralist, the poet laureate Robert Pinsky, James Earl Jones and Jane Alexander, chairman of the National Endowment for the Arts. The core of the meeting, which began on Thursday, is the still-tenuous alliance between for-profit and nonprofit organizations. 
 Alberta Arthurs, the co-chairman of the conference and the director of the program on culture and development at the Council on Foreign Relations, said that panelists were searching for "the appropriate agenda for the arts in the American experience in the first 20 or 25 years of the next century." Because of changes in the arts and in their financing, including cuts in support from the Federal arts endowment, she asked if it was necessary "to find new partnerships, new allies, new ways of working," and if so, where.
In recent years, the arts have proliferated throughout the United States on an amateur as well as a professional level. The conference's discussions are broad, addressing the needs of innovative artists who have come under attack from members of Congress and special interest groups. Addressing possible ways to improve conditions for arts that are now threatened, Ms. Arthurs said: "There's an enormous amount of things going on at the local level: new ideas about taxation and levies, about mobilizing people to work together within communities. I think there's a much more popular agenda for the arts than we're aware of."
At times discussions were heated, and there was also a fair amount of woolgathering as panelists dutifully tried to define "the arts" and "the public purpose." But generally there was a feeling that something had to be accomplished. At the end of the conference on Sunday, the chairmen will summarize the various points of view. Then books will be published and perhaps other meetings will be held.
The goal of the conference is to wrestle with problems that affect people on both sides of the fence, or rather, to remove the fence. "This Gaza Strip thinking is not in accord with reality," one participant commented. The reality, many suggested, is that the lines have blurred. It was suggested that audiences did not know or care if a play or opera was financed by nonprofit or commercial sources, although that distinction remains important to artists.
In the arts, "the nonprofit sector 'supports' the profit sector," in the view of Steven D. Lavine, president of the California Institute of the Arts in Valencia, Calif. His position seems to contradict the commonly held belief that nonprofit institutions and artists are supported by corporations and foundations. That financial support exists, but Mr. Lavine views it as a two-way street, with the artists supplying the creativity. His institute is partly financed by the Walt Disney Company, and he says "there is a tremendous hunger in technologically driven industries" to hire his graduates. Many of them go to work for Disney. Having said that, Mr. Lavine put a question on the conference table: how to insure the health of "that part of the arts that is socially and intellectually disruptive."
Ms. Alexander emphasized the role of artists as the research and development arm of popular culture. Frank Hodsoll, a former head of the arts endowment and co-chairman of the conference, was one of several people who expressed a belief in the "farm team" principle, suggesting that smaller institutions can feed the larger organizations, like the Metropolitan Opera.
The playwright David Henry Hwang spoke about the number of plays that began Off Broadway and in regional theater before moving to Broadway.
He predicted that there might eventually be a nonprofit touring circuit paralleling the one that existed for so many years in the commercial theater, and envisioned the rise of a nonprofit impresario who would book such tours.
Some of the panelists insisted that art itself is the end goal. David Mendoza, executive director of the National Campaign for Freedom of Expression in Seattle, said he was concerned about the demonization of the artist.
He defended Andres Serrano and Karen Finley, suggesting that those who have criticized their work have in many cases not seen it, and that in effect experimental artists are being censored by sound bite. He and others indicated that the public needed to be educated to increase its receptivity to challenging forms.
Although both conservatives and liberals attended, the arguments were less political than cultural and economic in the opening sessions. As Daniel F. Ritter, the director of the conference, said, the question was "not what this country can do for the arts but what the arts can do for this country."
State Senator Roy M. Goodman of New York said he was extremely concerned about "the appropriations of the N.E.A. being gutted" and urged that "know-nothing voices needed to be suppressed." Though there were those who argued that the endowment budget should be increased, the consensus seemed to be that it is time to look for new financing methods.
The American Assembly, founded in 1950 by Dwight D. Eisenhower at Columbia University, holds conferences on many subjects, often on foreign policy and domestic political and economic issues. Occasionally the group has focused on the arts. In 1990, the issue was biculturalism. This year, it was felt that changes in the support of the arts and the approaching millennium made the arts a timely area of study. The conference is supported by private organizations, including AT&T, the Ford Foundation and the New York Times Foundation.
Opinions diverged widely, but there seemed to be agreement that that more collaboration is essential. Don Frantz, an executive with Walt Disney Theatrical Productions, spoke of "coming out of the Magic Castle and up the hilltop," the hilltop being Arden House, where this conference and many others have been held. In suggesting that people think more about the individual artist, he noted that Julie Taymor, an experimental theater director, was staging the live version of the Disney animated musical "The Lion King" on Broadway.
Repeatedly cases were mentioned in which fringe artists broke through to a larger audience: all they needed was the exposure, preferably in prime time. At the end of one panel discussion, Ms. Alexander wondered aloud where "The Ed Sullivan Show" was "now that we need it." Her fellow panelists chorused that the current equivalent was Oprah Winfrey's program. An appearance on "Oprah," they said, could transform the life of a singer as well as an author.
Taking a longer view, Ms. Arthurs said that what was needed was "a map or a strategy," perhaps a new role in the arts for the business community or some way to combine public and private financing to meet the needs of the arts in the 21st century.

GRAPHIC: Photo: A conference in Harriman, N.Y., is addressing the ties between the arts and commerce. (Librado Romero/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: First-Quarter Growth Rate Rose to 5.8%

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 30

 BODY:
The economy's growth was stronger than initially estimated during the first three months of the year, the Commerce Department reported today. It recalculated the first-quarter output to an annual pace of 5.8 percent, the highest in nearly a decade, from 5.6 percent.
But more current indicators, including a slump of housing sales last month, seemed to confirm that the economy was slowing markedly from its late 1996 and early 1997 burst and could be settling back toward a sustainable pace before reviving inflation. 
 Sales of new homes, the department reported, dropped 7.7 percent in April, the steepest decline in six months and far more than could be attributed to a slim rise in mortgage rates. This followed Tuesday's report that sales of existing homes fell 2.4 percent last month.
Analysts saw the fresh signs of deceleration as supporting the Federal Reserve's decision last week to forgo raising rates despite a very tight labor market in which the unemployment rate has fallen to 4.9 percent.
"That is shaping up to look like a very good decision," said David F. Seiders, chief economist for the National Association of Home Builders. "Things are falling into place in terms of convincing evidence for a slowdown."
Mr. Seiders lowered his forecast for gross domestic product -- the output of goods and services within American borders -- to a 1.7 percent annual rate for the current quarter, less than a third of the rate in the January-March period.
Leif H. Olsen, a consultant in New Canaan, Conn., largely concurred. "We're already seeing a sharp slowdown in demand" this spring, he said, referring to soft automotive and other retail sales.
Yet some analysts contend that the slowdown will not be enough to keep the Federal Reserve from raising rates at its meeting in early July.
"We've got an economy that is really pretty hot," said Stephen S. Roach, economist at Morgan Stanley. "Any slowdown is going to be short-lived," he added, declaring that the Federal Reserve's "bet" in May that the economy was returning to a sustainable growth pace of 2 to 2 1/2 percent "is just dead wrong."
The Commerce Department's modest upward revision of first-quarter G.D.P, to a 5.8 percent pace from the 5.6 percent estimated at the end of April, was largely in line with expectations, though some analysts had guessed the figure would be upward of 6 percent.
Larger inventories and exports each contributed $5.5 billion more to output than first estimated, more than offsetting a $4.9 billion downward adjustment to personal spending for nondurables.
Inflation, as gauged by the price index for gross domestic purchases, remained at 2.2 percent, the same rate reported a month ago.
Corporate profits for the winter quarter, computed by the department for the first time, soared as expected though some analysts expressed mild disappointment.
"In relation to G.D.P., they're rather subdued since they're highly leveraged to growth," commented Maureen F. Allyn, chief economist at Scudder, Stevens & Clark.
Profits from current production surged 7 percent, or $46.7 billion, after declining 1.1 percent, or $7.2 billion, in the final 1996 quarter, the department found.
Cash flow from current production -- the internal funds available for investment -- advanced 4.5 percent after edging two-tenths of 1 percent lower in the fourth quarter.
"All the available evidence suggests that our economy is on a sustainable trajectory for continued job creation and sustained growth in output," Janet L. Yellen, chairwoman of the President's Council of Economic Advisers, said in a written statement. "The main symptom of economic overheating would be rising inflation. And we simply have not seen that."
Although the drop in new homes was the steepest in six months, analysts said this was a volatile set of statistics, one that may have also been artifically swollen lately by new methodology.
And the 7.7 percent skid, to an annual pace of 772,000, would have been only two-thirds as large had the department not improved the March performance with a sizable revision that transformed a 2.5 percent decline to a 2 percent advance.
"We're not really alarmed," said Mr. Seiders, who cited the decline as consistent with the slowing economy, with sales slipping below the 800,000 pace for the first time this year. Inventories of new homes remain lean, he added, with less than five months' supply at the latest selling rate.
Activity in the Northeast was unchanged in April while sales fell 16.5 percent in the West, 6 percent in the South and 2.9 percent in the Midwest.

(Sources: Commerce Department)(pg. 35)

HEADLINE: Treasury Prices Move Higher, Aided by Foreign Purchases

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Treasury prices ended higher yesterday, bolstered by economic reports that showed no unwelcome surprises for the bond market and also aided by overseas buying and comments by a Federal Reserve official.
The price of the 30-year bond ended the day up 26/32, to 96 15/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 6.91 percent, from 6.97 percent on Thursday, reaching the lowest level in two weeks. 
 "This is one of the quietest rallies you can imagine, with central bank buying from overseas really driving the market up," said Robert Pugliese, senior vice president and director of fixed-income investing at J. W. Charles Securities Inc. in Boca Raton, Fla.
"From a technical standpoint," he said, "we saw a key support area tested and hold, then reverse and go up through minor resistance levels; technicians love that. Going forward, expect increased volatility, and I definitely expect a Fed tightening in July."
Kim Rupert, an economist with MMS International in San Francsico, said the whole tone of the market was different from earlier in the week. "Hedge fund selling has dried up and it seems to be back on the buy side," she said near midday yesterday. "There is foreign investor buying, with flows out of Europe to the safe haven of the U.S. securities market, and because of the decline in stocks, some flight to quality to the short end of the Treasury market."
Economic data released yesterday helped confirm a view that the economy is slowing. The Purchasing Management Association of Chicago said that its manufacturing index fell to 56.8, from 57.2 in April. While its prices component was up, the employment component was down.
The Government said the economy grew at a 5.8 percent rate in the first quarter, up from an original estimate of 5.6 percent. It also reported that sales of new homes fell 7.7 percent last month, exceeding analysts' expectations of a 1.7 percent drop.
Comments by the president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, William McDonough, bolstered the market's optimism over the data.
Speaking to reporters after a speech to the Financial Markets Association in Toronto, Mr. McDonough said that "the data for the second quarter thus far indicate that the economy clearly is starting to slow down."
Prices and, for that matter, inflation have remained subdued, he said, in part because the gain in the value of the United States dollar against foreign currencies last year helped to hold down the price of imported goods. Were it not for the dollar's strength, he said, the rate of inflation would have been half a point higher in the first quarter of this year.
Next week, the market will shift its focus to a survey from the National Association of Purchasing Management on Monday and the May employment report on Friday.
"This week economic data were not too significant, with market participants more focused on next week's numbers," Ms. Rupert of MMS International said.
In other trading, "the municipal bond market has been so quiet you can hear two butterflies kiss," Mr. Pugliese of J. W. Charles Securities said, "probably because the yield spread to Treasuries has widened."
"However, there are two huge refunding months in June and July and we are seeing demand," he said. "But again the investment public is mesmerized by the stock market right now and that is affecting the fixed-income markets."

LENGTH: 784 words

HEADLINE: Slovenia Discards the Yoke That Was Yugoslavia

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES 

DATELINE: CRNGROB, Slovenia, May 30

 BODY:
A group of soldiers seated under a clump of trees, with drooping ferns tucked into the bands of their helmets, called out coordinates to companions manning four old Soviet-style mortars.
The rustle of the leaves overhead, the gentle chatter of songbirds and the tranquillity of an Alpine slope on a spring afternoon all seemed to mock the art of war. 
 And nothing in this small band of conscripts, or the old tube-shaped mortars in the valley below, evoked the savage blood quest that has seized so many young men a few hundred miles away in Bosnia.
"Most of us just want to do our seven months in the army and go home," said Matej Ahacic, 21.
But this tiny nation, with a professional army smaller than an American division, is pressing to add its name to the list of countries whose applications for membership are being considered by NATO. At the meeting of NATO foreign ministers in Sintra, Portugal, on Thursday, 9 of 16 NATO countries said that despite United States calls to limit new members to Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic, they would also back the entry of Slovenia and Romania.
Officials here readily acknowledge that their military is years away from reaching NATO's standards, and it is not clear what Slovenia would do if the alliance granted its wish immediately.
"We hope to have the whole Slovenian Army, including the reservists, integrated with NATO equipment by 2010," Gen. Janez Kavar, a member of the general staff, said in a recent interview. "If we do not make it into NATO in the first round, our energy and vision will remain the same. Membership in NATO, for us, means achieving real national security."
Only Slovenia has lifted itself out of the morass that grips the countries of the former Yugoslavia. It has emerged as one of the crucial demarcation points between the new Western-oriented countries of post-cold-war Europe and those that seem destined to wallow for decades in political and economic disaary.
The other nations that once made up Yugoslavia -- Serbia, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Macedonia, Montenegro and Croatia -- appear increasingly likely to remain outside most Western military and economic organizations.
"Membership in NATO and the European Union is as much about the identity Slovenes hope to forge as a European state as it is about anything else," a Western diplomat said. "And if they fail to enter these two organizations, it will be a real blow to their perception of who they are and where they are headed."
The alliance will meet to admit new members next month. It last admitted a new member -- Spain -- 15 years ago.
Slovenia has little to offer the alliance militarily. This country of two million has only 4,100 career combat troops and 56,000 reservists, most of whom still use old Soviet-style weapons left behind by the retreating Yugoslav Army at the time of independence six years ago. Few officers have much formal military training, and it will be years, especially with an annual military budget of $310 million, before it can fully modernize its arsenal.
But Slovenia has geopolitical value. The country, which flanks Austria and Italy, provides a land link to Hungary. Its inclusion in the alliance would also send a message to other nations in transition from Communism, like Croatia, that have refused to liberalize their economies, establish full democratic rule or abide by the Bosnian peace accords: there are tangible benefits to cooperating with the Western alliance. Given the possibility of renewed unrest in the former Yugoslavia, Slovenia offers both stability and a bridgehead in the region, Western diplomats say.
The Slovenes often seem to be retreating as fast as they can from the embrace of the former Yugoslavia. Road and rail links into Austria and Hungary are being expanded while the asphalt strip to Zagreb, Croatia's capital, is so narrow and decrepit that cars and trucks crawl over the border. There is no military cooperation agreement with Croatia, although agreements have been signed with Italy, Austria and Hungary.
And Slovene officials, who led a 10-day war of independence from Yugoslavia in 1991, never tire of emphasizing their ties with the Hapsburg empire, which ruled Slovenia from the 16th century until the end of World War I in 1918.
Memberships in NATO and the European Union seem to most Slovenes the best tickets out of a region with a history rife with war and bloodshed.
"Take a look at what is happening in the former Yugoslavia," said a senior Foreign Ministry official. "The conflict is not over, the economies are a disaster and there is no real democratic rule. Can you blame us for wanting to cut our ties as fast as we can?"

HEADLINE: Peru's Congress Is Assailed Over Its Removal of Judges

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES, May 30

 BODY:
Peru's Congress came under sharp criticism today from Western diplomats, human rights groups and business and political leaders for its decision on Thursday to oust three members of a constitutional court who ruled that President Alberto K. Fujimori could not seek a third consecutive term.
The United States Ambassador in Lima, Dennis C. Jett, said today that the dismissal of the judges was "a step backward in the consolidation of democracy in Peru," a poor Andean country emerging from years of guerrilla warfare and military dictatorship. 
 The Peruvian Congress voted 52 to 33 to dismiss the judges, Manuel Aguirre, Delia Revoredo and Guillermo Rey, who ruled in December that Mr. Fujimori could not run for President again because he will have already served the constitutional limit of two terms.
"I definitely see this as a step backward because when one key democratic institution oversteps its bounds and attacks another, rather than respect a decision, it only serves to weaken the whole democratic system, which is based on institutions and not on individuals," Mr. Jett said, referring to the control Mr. Fujimori exercises over the Congress through his political party, Change 90.
Human rights groups said the removal of the judges was the worst setback for democracy in Peru since Mr. Fujimori dismissed Congress and the courts and seized near-dictatorial powers in 1992 -- a move the President said was necessary to fight terrorism and rebuild the shattered economy.
"There are no longer any checks on Fujimori's power," Anne Manuel, deputy director for Human Rights Watch/Americas, said in a telephone interview from Washington. "He so thoroughly controls the Congress that he can get them to do whatever he wants, and now dismissing these judges clearly demonstrates that his Government won't tolerate an independent judiciary."
Mirko Laurer, a prominent political analyst, said in his column in the newspaper La Republica that the three judges had been removed in the same spirit that Mr. Fujimori had eliminated the Tupac Amaru rebels in the military operation that freed hostages at the Japanese Ambassador's residence in Lima last month.
The ouster of the three judges paves the way for Mr. Fujimori to run for a third term in 2000. When he was first elected President, in 1990, the Constitution prohibited re-election. After declaring martial law in 1992, he persuaded a new Congress, dominated by his party, to change the Constitution to allow two consecutive five-year terms. He was re-elected in 1995 by an overwhelming margin.
Last year, Mr. Fujimori persuaded Congress to approve a legal interpretation that considers his current term his first under the new Constitution, making him eligible to run for a third term.
But when the congressional interpretation went before the constitutional court at the request of a Peruvian lawyers' group, the three judges ruled that allowing Mr. Fujimori to run again would violate the Constitution.
Three other justices of the court abstained, and the head constitutional justice upheld the decision of his three colleagues who voted against Mr. Fujimori's right to run again.
Congress dismissed the three judges on ground that they violated the Constitution because they exceeded their authority by signing a document on behalf of the full court. The head justice's case is still pending before Congress.
Although he has not formally announced this intention to seek a third term, Mr. Fujimori has said in recent interviews that he would not rule out that possibility.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Wagenia Journal;
By Hook or by Crook, a Fishing Village Gets By

BYLINE: By DONALD G. McNEIL Jr. 

DATELINE: WAGENIA, Congo

 BODY:
This fishing village is authentic; it has been here so long that the Wagenias boast that they were the first Africans to know how to swim. But because its nets extend into the famous Stanley Falls and because it is right next to Congo's third-largest city, Kisangani, it is also something of a national monument.
There's a decaying arch at the village entrance commemorating visits by Mobutu Sese Seko, the former President, and the elaborate nets that the villagers hang in the falls were once featured on the national currency. 
 These days, though, any visitor here, watching a six-inch fish struggling to wriggle out of a 10-foot trap while a Wagenia villager reaches a long arm in after it, knows exactly how the fish feels.
Congo has just fought a civil war, and tourists are few and far between. And it has been a long time since fishing was enough to pay the village's bills by itself.
So when a couple of out-of-towners appeared in the village one recent day, interrupting a late-afternoon soccer game, 20 young men broke off from the crowd to walk with them. The day's catch, it was apparent, had arrived.
Stanley Falls -- better known here as the Chutes des Wagenia -- have none of the drama of Niagara Falls in New York or Victoria Falls in Zimbabwe. They are really a series of rapids with drops of eight feet or so. Their historical importance is that they made the River Congo impassable, so the town of Stanleyville had to be built as a terminus for a short railroad that carries passengers and freight around the cataracts to the upstream boats and barges. In recent weeks, it has carried Hutu refugees out of the forest.
Under their legendary chief, Lowao, the Wagenia built villages on both sides of the falls and the small islands in between. They are considered a separate tribe, and they say their name either means "nomads" or derives from the local word for speaking and means "people who talk too much."
They fish by building log scaffoldings over the falls and suspending rows of cone-shaped nets from them. Everything, including the ropes, is made of jungle vines and trees. But like the impossibly ordered cuteness of some restored New England whaling town, the painful authenticity here seems a bit fishy, since African villagers are usually remarkable at recycling 20th-century castoffs -- old cans into toys, old tires into sandals. Nylon rope would last longer. And, in fact, young boys who are not putting on a show for the tourists weave their hand nets out of nylon thread and weight them with bits of lead that they strip off underwater electric cables.
But the authentic nets produce income from more than one source.
"The men want to know -- would you like to see how they fish?" asked Jeff Ngazi N'sila Lissa, a villager who says he divides his time between tending his family's net and studying sociology at the University of Kisangani. "They will show you, if you will give them something."
Even though fetching scenes were unfolding free at the water's edge -- a man climbing out of his net with his catch stuck deep in his mouth, a woman washing her clothes, children dangerously deep in the rapids with hand nets -- the men seemed quite insistent.
In the prewar days, when the local government was organized enough to staff the booth near the arch, money would be collected there and used, at least in part, for the general good of the village. But now that booth is a tiny store, its shelves stocked with detergent and matches. So the villagers have to fend for themselves.
Mr. Ngazi said the official entrance price used to be $20 for a small camera and $30 for a big camera. Although that seemed high for a country where the average income is $150 a year, it was agreed to, and a dozen men eased themselves out over the torrent on the wet logs to demonstrate net-hoisting while Mr. Ngazi answered questions.
A few villagers slip each year and are dashed to death on the rocks, he said. Only old men fish at night because the river is stalked by phantoms who have the heads of men and the bodies of crocodiles and eat young people.
There are many kinds of fish, but the two prize catches are "hydroxon Goliathe" and "captain."
"For a large captain, you can get even $200 in the market and buy clothes and food and pay school fees for the children," Mr. Ngazi said.
Beside him on the riverbank, a boy built a tiny fire on a rock, wrapped a few wet fingerlings in a leaf, squeezed on peppers and lime, and plunged the leaf purse into the flames to cook.
Despite the river's bounty, times got steadily worse in Wagenia in the 32 years of Mr. Mobutu's rule as inflation rose and tourism disappeared. The whole village is ecstatic that the rebels led by Laurent Kabila ousted Mr. Mobutu, and hopes that good times are just around the river bend.
"Now we shall eat bread, like Americans," Mr. Ngazi said. "We shall have one goat for one person. Even one cow for one person, like America! We shall be very full -- like Mike Tyson!"
But as the demonstration ended in a pink sunset over the falls, it became clear that uncertain times mean other problems for a small village. With the new national Government still getting organized, there is no legal chief -- the kind of authority figure who could take $30 and distribute it evenly. So a shouting match developed on the riverbank over whose hand would accept the money.
In the end, Mr. Ngazi did, and quickly pocketed $20 of it, escalating shouting into shoving. The walk back was a long gauntlet of outstretched palms and whispered demands, and the car departed in a light shower of local blessings. If the catch had been better, they would have been fish guts.

GRAPHIC: Photos: At Stanley Falls in Congo, the visitors are few and far between these days, and those who venture there are prey to the local fishermen. Two young girls used a veil as a net while fishing in the falls. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)
 
Map showing the location of Wagenia, Congo: Fishing these days falls short of paying the bills in Wagenia.

HEADLINE: Liberties;
Live From New York

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD 

 BODY:
"Hey, hey, hey, hey, wake up, sleepyheads! It's Morning in America! Saturday Morning in America!"
Susan Molinari's round little face is all smiley and scrunched up. The perky pixie looks into the camera for her first live news show, sitting in her spanking new red, white and blue set in the studio at West 57th. 
 "First of all, I want to thank the people of Staten Island and Brooklyn for the faith they put in me during my four terms in Congress. And I want to thank Andrew Heyward, the president of CBS, for the faith he put in me, transitioning me from politics to media even though some newsies with no sense of fun complained that I do not have the experience to be a news anchor person.
"To those of you out there who are worried that I won't have enough objectivity and detachment to be a serious journalist, I would simply say -- Lighten up! How objective does a girl have to be to follow in Diane Sawyer's high heels? Besides, D'Amato and Pataki think that CBS on Saturday morning is the perfect positioning for me to run for the New York Senate seat against George Stephanopoulos, since he's running from ABC on Sunday morning.
"Oh, my goodness, media critics have tried to turn this into some sort of watershed in the corruption of TV news. But, I mean, what did Rosie O'Donnell know about talk shows before she got one?
"The only person at CBS who was really upset -- she came at me one day in the cafeteria with an icepick -- was Laura Ingraham, who thought that if Andy wanted a G.O.P. blonde with no experience to star in this show, it should be her.
"I'm going to work hard to be balanced, and I've assembled a crack team to help me out. Over here on my left is my band, Trent Lott and the 'Singing Senators.' And on my right is my sidekick, Guy Molinari. I call him 'Daddy.' And in his lap is my baby, America's baby, Susan Ruby. This morning, when I got Susan Ruby up, her diaper was off, so then I had to bathe her before I fed her and that annoyed her, so she pooped on the comforter, and we haven't really recouped from that."
Susan takes a drag on a cigarette, and giggles. "I really should quit because of Susan Ruby. But tobacco has done so much for the gross national product.
"We have a very exciting show today. We'll hear from our answer to Martha Stewart, Kay Bailey Hutchison, who will show us some low-cost, nutritious menus for those moms we finally kicked off welfare. Christie Todd Whitman will take us abroad for a visit with the Beautiful Men of Sweden. We'll have our own free-for-all discussion, in which all points of view will be heard -- someone from the right will argue with someone from the far right. We'll have a review of new hip-hop releases by our rap critic, Pat Boone. We'll have an interview with Tiger Woods by our special correspondent to the U.S. Open, Dan Quayle. Dick Armey will do 'Book Notes.' And Peggy Noonan gives the first of her eloquent weekly commentaries on American values, 'Oh, It Was Ever Thus.'
"But first we're going to hear from our weatherman, Bill Paxon."
"You know, Susie, you don't need a weatherman to know which way the wind blows. It'll be mild and seasonable in the Republican Sun Belt but there will be showers on the liberal Northeast. Global warming continues to cool, the ozone hole is doing fine and the air quality is, as always, perfect."
"Thanks, honey, and can you change the baby's diaper before you go? Now, for my very first guest, I am so thrilled and proud to have someone I really look up to. The Speaker of the House is here to talk about his new book, 'Lessons of Leadership,' which will help him pay off that stupid cost assessment for that appearance of conflict of interest. They said I couldn't bring myself to ask you the tough questions, Newt. Let's prove them wrong."
"O.K., Susie, fire away."
"O.K., Newt, what's it like to be a great man?"
"Funny you should ask. That's just what my new book is about. Lesson No. 1: Surround yourself with lieutenants who are more hideous than you are, so that people will be afraid to get rid of you. Lesson No. 2: Think galactically."
"You're a genius, Newt. Well, this show flew by faster than a Bill Clinton promise. Join me next week, when my dad will talk about his proposal to crush the skulls of doctors who perform partial-birth abortions. And that's, like, the way it is."

HEADLINE: Beliefs

BYLINE: By Peter Steinfels

 BODY:
"He came preaching and teaching, and he left preaching and teaching," said the Rev. Jesse Jackson on Thursday when they buried the Rev. Samuel DeWitt Proctor from Abyssinian Baptist Church in Harlem. A week earlier Dr. Proctor had suffered a fatal heart attack while lecturing to college students in Iowa.
At 75, he had remained one of the most sought-after speakers in the nation. It was not surprising that among the saddened congregants, friends and family members who filled the church, where Dr. Proctor was pastor from 1972 to 1989, were an astonishing number of preachers. 
 Many began gathering early that morning in the study of Dr. Proctor's successor, the Rev. Calvin O. Butts 3d. Some were elder statesmen of the African-American clergy, like Dr. Proctor himself. Many others were Dr. Proctor's students. The words on everyone's lips were "masterful preacher" and "mentor."
"He never wasted a sentence," said the Rev. Lawrence Carter, Dean of the Chapel at Morehouse College in Atlanta.
"He opened up doors," said the Rev. William S. Epps, who was Dr. Proctor's associate pastor at Abyssinian from 1972 to 1975 and now leads the Second Baptist Church in Los Angeles.
"He didn't hesitate to give us every opportunity to grow and do new things," said the Rev. Marvin A. McMickle, who had been on the staff at Abyssinian and is now pastor of Antioch Baptist Church in Cleveland. "By the thousands, there are people who feel he made the decisive impact in their lives."
New Yorkers knew Dr. Proctor as the man who succeeded the Rev. Adam Clayton Powell Jr. as pastor of Abyssinian. The church, founded when Jefferson was President, had been led since 1938 by Mr. Powell, the legendary Harlem churchman and Congressman, and it had felt the ups and downs -- since the late 1960's, mostly downs -- of Mr. Powell's public career. It was Dr. Proctor who had steadied the vessel and restored the spiritual ballast.
But the scores of young clergymen from all corners of the United States were testimony to Dr. Proctor's status as a national figure. So was the presence of Sargent Shriver, President John F. Kennedy's aide and brother-in-law, who had persuaded Dr. Proctor to assume important posts in the Peace Corps and later in the Office of Economic Opportunity.
As much an educator as a pastor, Dr. Proctor was president of two historically black universities, Virginia Union and North Carolina A&T State. He was the initiator of major programs for advancing African-American scholars and, for 15 years, the Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial Professor at the Rutgers University School of Education.
Befriend, instruct, aid, advise -- that was the lifelong pattern. In 1950, on a return trip to Crozer Theological Seminary in Chester, Pa., where Dr. Proctor had once been the only African-American student, Dr. Proctor met Martin Luther King Jr. He quickly engaged the bright young man in conversation, maintained contact over the next years, offered him speaking opportunities and, as the bus boycott got under way in Montgomery, Ala., spoke in turn at Dr. King's church in Montgomery.
He counseled Medgar Evers in Mississippi and taught the future Gov. L. Douglas Wilder of Virginia in an ethics course.
"He gave me a chance," Mr. Jackson said on Thursday, telling the mourners how, after leaving the University of Illinois and his football scholarship in a despondent mood, he had showed up at North Carolina A&T without money or academic credentials.
Mr. Jackson said his generation was full of "Proctorians," who sometimes compete to see who can be the "most Proctor."
"I have spent all of my adult life trying to make Dr. Proctor proud of me," he said.
One mourner, sitting largely unrecognized among many of the "Proctorians" present, was Lorraine Smoller, Dr. Proctor's secretary at Rutgers for 21 years. She described his constant battles to catch planes and keep track of his speeches.
In 1979, when he was hospitalized after a heart attack, she said, "he had me come to the hospital to take dictation." And before he left for Iowa this month, she recalled, "he was kidding around -- 'Don't they know I'm an old man?' "
In May 1989, the year Dr. Proctor retired as pastor, Bill Moyers, who had worked with him in the Peace Corps and the War on Poverty, noted this tirelessness:
"Sometimes I think Sam Proctor must have lived his entire life on a public platform, speaking," Mr. Moyers wrote in a foreword to some of Dr. Proctor's autobiographical reflections, "My Moral Odyssey" (Judson Press, 1989). "The first time I saw him, he was speaking," Mr. Moyers wrote. "The last time I saw him, he was speaking. Even as I write, it is commencement season in the U.S., and reliable sources tell me that Sam Proctor will deliver more graduation speeches than any other person in the country."
"Actually, Sam does not just speak," Mr. Moyers continued. "He preaches. Oh, my, how he preaches. He is a born preacher. And I am certain that when the Pearly Gates swing open and Sam strides through to claim his richly deserved crown, the first question he will put to St. Peter will be, 'Where's the pulpit?' "
At Thursday's service, the Rev. Gardner C. Taylor, Pastor Emeritus of Concord Baptist Church of Christ in Brooklyn, was the final speaker. Dr. Taylor hammered out the ways in which Dr. Proctor remained faithful to his calling until the repeated "faithful" was picked up by the congregation and reverberated around the sanctuary.
Only Dr. Taylor, in many people's estimation the greatest pulpit orator in the United States today, could declaim that Dr. Proctor died "with his sword unsheathed and his armor still in place" and that "he went directly to see the King with the stain of battle still on his garments."
Jesse Jackson put it less dramatically: 'He came preaching and teaching, and he left preaching and teaching."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Don't Punish Nigerians

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Nadine Gordimer (Op-Ed, May 25) accurately describes the facts regarding the lack of freedom of the press in Nigeria.
But when she asks for international sanctions that will "put a stop to all abuses of flesh, mind and spirit in Nigeria" she is asking us to punish the millions who live under the corrupt regime of Gen. Sani Abacha and who will starve in greater numbers if her idea is implemented. 
 We need a foreign policy that will weaken corruption, injustice and human rights abuses by the rulers of countries, but one that will not punish helpless citizens.
 
CARL R. HUTTER
Wilmington, Del., May 27, 1997

HEADLINE: Ruling Party In Indonesia Tightens Grip

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: JAKARTA, Indonesia, May 30

 BODY:
As votes were counted today from the remote mountains and distant islands of this far-flung nation, the Government party consolidated what appeared to be a record margin of victory in Thursday's election.
But although the tightly choreographed election produced the result the Government intended, Indonesia was left with more questions than answers to the issues raised in the often violent campaign: 
 *Will the riots that unsettled the nation before the election subside, or will the discontent they demonstrated continue to build?
*Did the overwhelming victory for the party of President Suharto enhance his credibility after 31 years in office, or will it only increase the frustrations of those who demand a more open political system?
*And will the leadership now take more effective steps to address the causes of discontent, including corruption, social and economic disparities and overbearing and unresponsive Government institutions?
With 85 percent of the vote counted, the Government party, Golkar, had won 74 percent of the vote, exceeding its 68 percent in the last election, five years ago, and its previous record of 73.2 percent, in 1968.
The total today for the United Development Party was 23 percent, up from 17 percent in 1992. The fragmented and squabbling Indonesian Democratic Party won just 3 percent, down from 15 percent.
The data reported today did not include figures for the turnout, and there was no indication of the effectiveness of an opposition campaign to boycott the election.
There were indications today that the opposition might protest the vote. "There were many frauds that we consider as very, very serious," said Tosarai Wijaya, campaign manager of the United Development Party.
And there was at least one outbreak of violence today, in Sampang, 400 miles east of here, where the police said 3,000 opponents of Golkar had rampaged through the streets, throwing stones at Government buildings, burning houses and damaging cars in a pattern that became familiar during the campaign.
Even if the opposition protests the vote, that would not prevent the new Parliament from convening as planned. It will include the 425 members elected on Thursday, plus 75 members from the military. Next March, this group will be joined by 500 delegates chosen by the Government to form an assembly to select the President. Mr. Suharto, 75, is generally believed to plan to remain in office for a seventh term.
In every past assembly since 1971, Mr. Suharto has been named President without opposition.

WASHINGTON, May 30 (Reuters) -- The United States called today for changes in Indonesia's political system, citing reports of multiple voting irregularities.
John Dinger, a State Department spokesman. said: "The electoral system severely limits political competition. Indonesian citizens do not have the ability to change their government through democratic means."
