HEADLINE: Lots of Dollars Chasing a $70 Million State Lottery Dream

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN 

 BODY:
Worried that television ads cluttered with yachts and limos might be sending the wrong message, Gov. George E. Pataki asked state lottery officials last year to tone down their enticing "Hey, you never know" pitch. The ads should avoid raising unrealistic expectations, he said, and should stress that the lottery's primary purpose is to raise money for education.
But an informal survey of people lined up dozens deep at pharmacies, newsstands, candy shops and bodegas yesterday suggested that the old message had stuck and the new one had failed to make much of an impression. Few of those waiting to plunk down money, at 12,913,583-to-one odds, for a chance at today's $70 million jackpot mentioned education financing. 
 They wagered at the rate of $1.6 million an hour, and for the old reasons, often explaining themselves in the language of the defunct commercials. "A dollar and a dream," said Derrick Duhon as he bought a handful of tickets at a delicatessen in downtown Brooklyn, bringing his total to $90. "A dollar and a dream," he repeated.
The man in line behind him, who declined to give his name, repeated another slogan: "You have to be in it to win it."
This is the third biggest jackpot in New York lottery history, after January 1991, when nine winners shared $90 million, and October 1994, when four winners shared $72.5 million. But in keeping with their sober new tone, lottery officials were not exactly touting the chance of big winnings.
Ric Grenell, a New York State Lottery spokesman, advised New Yorkers to buy just one ticket.
"Be careful," Mr. Grenell said. "You're probably not going to win."
This is not news to most. Charles Hearne, 46, who bought tickets from five places in the Bronx's Parkchester section and was not finished, said that in his lifetime he has spent $10,000 on lottery tickets and made $1,000. "It's not a wise investment," he said.
But it appears to be an irresistible one. "It's like a shiny lure in the water," said Jeffrey Papale, 44, an unemployed painter from Whitestone, Queens. "It's a stupendous amount of money."
People dreamed about what they might do with the jackpot. Christy Smith, who bought a ticket at an East Village pharmacy since she needed some cigarettes anyway, would buy a houseboat in Amsterdam. Raymond McCoy, a farmer in Riverhead, N.Y., would hire a chauffeur.
Gil Miranda, a customer at the pharmacy, had a larger vision. "I'm going to clean up the environment, help fight cancer and buy a condo in Miami Beach," he said.
The enthusiasm for the lottery is suggested by a record $4 billion spent on tickets last year, a pace that has accelerated since. "What other gambling can you do where you spend $1 and it can change your life forever?" said Barry Miller, managing editor of LottoWorld, a Naples, Fla., magazine. "It's a fantasy."
Yesterday, in another indication of the public fascination with lotteries, Mr. Miller began writing a column in The Daily News under the title, "Ask Mr. Lotto." In his first column, Mr. Miller explained how the key numbers in the actress Melanie Griffith's life -- like 14, the age she began living with Don Johnson -- might provide the key to a winning card.
Drawings are held each Wednesday and Saturday. Today's drawing at 11:21 P.M. follows five in which nobody won. The cycle began with a $4 million prize. Since there was no winner, it was automatically increased to $10 million the next drawing, then $15 million, $25 million and finally $40 million this Wednesday.
Mr. Grenell said amounts above $40 million are determined on the basis of a combined analysis of the finances of all the state's lottery games. So he could not predict what the prize would be next Wednesday were no winner drawn tonight.
There were still at least a few people who were not remotely interested. "My old legs couldn't stand it," said Eleanor Haas, a retired nurse, who gazed at the long line buying tickets at the Port Authority Bus Terminal as she sipped a cup of coffee.
Nicole Cole, an architecture student at Cooper Union, said she was too busy for lotteries. A bus ticket salesman who declined to give his name said he was a pessimist. Anna Shteynshleyger, waiting for a bus, said, "I wasn't even aware it was going on."
Others refused to play because they consider the lottery pernicious. Jim Rodriguez, 54, a retired security guard from the South Bronx, said he was not contributing to what he considers legalized theft from working people. He said: "It's all rigged. It seems like a lot of money goes into it and I don't see a lot of improvements. There's not much being done to improve the schools for the kids."
Mr. Grenell said the lottery contributed $1.4 billion to education last year. Of each $1 spent on tickets, he said, 50 cents goes to winners, 39 cents to education, 6 cents to the seller of the ticket and the rest for advertising and administration.
As for the assertion that the lottery takes advantage of the disadvantaged, Mr. Miller said that studies done by LottoWorld found that the average lottery player is 30 to 50 years of age, makes $30,000 to $50,000 a year and has completed high school and one year of college.
But as thousands line up clutching hot pink cards and cool green cash, some people see it as a waste.
'People will spend their whole life savings and never win," said Kamiyla Van Cleave, 39, who was selling incense and scrolls in the Bronx. "They are dreaming, and what are dreams but illusions?"

HEADLINE: Price Decrease For Used Autos Reverses Trend

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, May 30

 BODY:
After rising briskly for four years, prices for used cars have begun to fall, and the effects are rippling through the auto industry.
The used-car market has always been a complicated bellwether, both of industry trends and of the overall American economy. Weaker prices now may reflect a fading in the popularity of used cars, after several years in which they had increasingly been seen as alternatives to new cars. 
 Used-car prices climbed 4 to 9 percent a year from 1992 through 1995. Auto industry officials attributed the rise to the greater durability of cars, the wider availability of used-car warranties and a growing public acceptance of low-mileage, late-model used cars as an alternative to new cars.
But retail prices for used cars, as measured by part of the Consumer Price Index, have fallen 2 percent the last 12 months, even as new-car prices have inched up by nine-tenths of 1 percent.
These are several effects that are clear from the current drop in used-car prices:
*Better deals are becoming available for consumers shopping for used cars.
*New-car buyers are beginning to get less money for their trade-ins.
*People who lease cars later this year may have to pay a few more dollars a month than they would today, because lease payments are calculated to cover the drop in a car's value over the term of the lease.
*Profits for auto makers and dealers alike will be hurt, as it becomes harder for auto makers to sell profitably cars returned at the end of leases and as dealers' used-car inventories erode in value.
*Overall inflation may lessen in the United States, because lower prices for used cars tend to slow the rise in new-car prices, and new and used cars together account for 6.3 percent of the Consumer Price Index.
The combination of falling used-car prices -- and thus lower trade-in values -- and slightly higher prices for new cars makes new cars a little less affordable, said Pete D. Cansler, a director at the Chrome Data Corporation in Portland, Ore., which tracks car prices.
"The cost of the new keeps rising, while the price of the used is falling, and you're paying the difference," he said.
That means less attractive deals for millions of Americans trying to sell or trade in their old cars and light trucks. Kelley Blue Book, an industry standard, shows a typical retail price for a two-year-old 1995 Ford Crown Victoria is $16,900, down from $17,050 a year ago for a similarly equipped 1994 model. Yet the initial price for a new Crown Victoria rose $1,970, to $21,315, from 1994 to 1995.
Used subcompacts, sports cars and luxury cars have seen particular weakness in price, said Patricia R. Erney, managing editor of the National Automobile Dealers Association's used-car handbook. Prices for used pickup trucks, midsize cars and sport utility vehicles have held up well, while prices for used mini-vans have been mixed, she said.
Retail prices might edge down a little more because wholesale prices have been falling slightly faster. According to the dealers' association, the average price of used cars and light trucks sold at wholesale auctions for dealers has dropped 3 percent the last 12 months to $9,938 from $10,242.
Industry experts attribute the erosion in used-car prices to many factors, including the steadily rising number of cars coming off lease each year and the growing availability of used sport utility vehicles, mini-vans and other vehicles previously in short supply. But the biggest single reason for the price drop seems to be a tapering off of the early 1990's great boom in used cars.
The growing reliability and durability of two- and three-year-old cars persuaded millions of car buyers over the last several years to consider used cars instead of new ones. But there are many signs now that prices have risen enough to reflect the fact that cars last longer these days than a decade ago.
There are even a few indications that the trend may be reversing itself.. Surveys by the Dohring Company, a market research group in Glendale, Calif., found this spring that only 50 percent of people who had just bought a new car would consider the purchase of a used car next time, down from 57 percent a year ago.
Most auto analysts and economists say that the popularity of used cars has simply stopped rising, so prices have also stopped rising. From now on, they predict, used-car prices will tend to bob up as the economy strengthens and dip as the economy weakens while remaining at a generally higher level than a decade ago.
"Now you're getting back to the normal trajectory" of used-car prices, said Mustafa Mohatarem, chief economist at the General Motors Corporation.
The fall in used-car prices is one of many factors contributing to a slackening in new-car sales this spring. Auto makers are scheduled to report their May sales beginning on Monday, and industry analysts expect them to report an overall drop of 5 or 6 percent from a year ago. Sales were also lower in April but very strong in the first three months of the year, when consumer demand was high for many industries.
Even if falling used-car prices do not hurt new-car sales, they could hurt auto makers and dealers in other ways. If cars returned at the end of leases cannot be sold for as much as the auto makers anticipated, the manufacturers can also face losses. And auto dealerships, including the used-car superstores opening in many cities, like Auto Nation and Car Max, expect to make money on cars that sit on their lots, not lose it.
"It's like wine sitting in the cellar -- you'd rather see it appreciate than depreciate," said Scott Merlis, an independent auto analyst in Westport, Conn.
Lessors, notably the financial arms of the auto makers but also many banks, have the bigger exposure. The average car coming off lease is now worth about 1 percent less than the lessors had calculated in advance, said Thomas C. Webb, chief economist at the National Automobile Dealers Association in Washington.
Samuel H. Insolera, the owner of Pacific Rim Leasing and Financial Inc. in West Paterson, N.J., which specializes in financing used cars, said that the market was weakest for two-year-old cars, which are commonly coming off lease. "Cars from the 1995 and 1996 model years are the problem children for everyone," he said.
Auto makers have been cagey about their financial reserves and other preparations for potential lease losses. Ford Motor, the most aggressive user of leases and often the most optimistic in calculating how much cars will be worth at the end of leases, is under particular scrutiny by Wall Street analysts.
Ford officials told analysts at a meeting last week that the company could be exposed to losses if used-car prices continued to fall, according to two analysts who attended the gathering. But Della DiPietro, a Ford spokeswoman, said this week that the company was not alarmed.
"It's something we're watching, but we feel we're on track," she said.

HEADLINE: Some Provisions Softened In Family Welfare Accord

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, May 30

 BODY:
Two years after Connecticut imposed the nation's shortest time limit on family welfare benefits, lawmakers have agreed to soften a provision that could have ended assistance to up to a tenth of the state's families on welfare.
Under the agreement, welfare recipients who have been sanctioned twice for failing to look for work will no longer be subject to immediate termination when the 21-month time limit expires. Instead, the state will provide them with counseling and support after their second sanction and allow them to get a six-month extension if they are judged to have made a legitimate effort to comply with the program. 
 The change is part of a package of measures to revamp the state's welfare system that tightens restrictions for able-bodied recipients who can work, while extending a safety net for those who are considered most vulnerable.
The agreement, which was reached late Thursday night, includes a number of provisions.
*It extends Medicaid benefits to teen-age children of the working poor.
*It ends welfare for single adults who are judged to be employable.
*It provides no transitional cash benefits to family welfare recipients who are cut off after 21 months because they have found work that pays more than their monthly grant -- even if that work pays less than the Federal poverty level.
*It offers Medicaid and family welfare benefits to legal immigrants who arrived here before the Federal welfare law was passed last August, despite Federal rules allowing states to deny them benefits.
The deal, which has yet to be acted on by the Legislature, could still fall apart over whether to provide Medicaid and family welfare benefits to legal immigrants who arrived in this country after the Federal welfare law was passed. Democrats want Connecticut to be one of the few states to offer them benefits, while Republicans say that such a measure would attract immigrants from New York, where such benefits are unlikely to be offered.
"That could be a deal breaker," said State Representative Robert Farr, a Republican of West Hartford, who has been part of the group negotiating the new welfare laws. "Otherwise, the agreement is pretty much in place."
In some respects, the proposal represents a major shift in the state's approach to welfare, as it acknowledges what many experts have said for years: that some recipients will need help to make the transition to employment from welfare.
Until now, the Republican Governor, John G. Rowland, has taken the position that welfare recipients should be largely responsible for finding work on their own.
On the one hand, the state imposed the time limit and allowed the Social Services Department to sanction recipients who did not look for work by reducing their benefits 20 percent for three months on the first offense, and 35 percent for six months on the second.
On the other hand, the state encouraged recipients to work by allowing them to keep all earnings in addition to their welfare checks until their income passed the poverty level. The state did not offer extensive job training services or other types of transitional assistance beyond running a job placement program.
The time limit does not apply to roughly one-third of the welfare recipients, who have been deemed unemployable. The new agreement recognizes that some recipients will need additional help.
Claudette Beaulieu, a Social Services Department spokeswoman, said the new program could be described in part as hand-holding. It would involve intensive case management with "one-on-one review with a very low ratio of clients per case manager," she said.
While Ms. Beaulieu said that only 170 families of the 43,000 on family welfare now had two sanctions, Michael W. Koslowski, Mr. Rowland's budget chief, wrote in a letter in February that roughly 10 percent of the recipients would have a problem with sanctions.
Mr. Farr said the intensive assistance was a step back from the Republican philosophy that people should "help themselves." It represented a bow to the Democratic view that there are some welfare recipients "with special needs who deserve special assistance," he said.
But Betty Gallo, a lobbyist for nonprofit antipoverty groups, said it was a meager concession in a package with significant punitive measures. "We never thought that right before the 1998 election, the Governor would throw children off of welfare."

HEADLINE: Political Notes;
Giuliani Enlists 'Latino' Backer Who Doesn't Quite Fit the Bill

BYLINE: By DAVID FIRESTONE 

 BODY:
Ever since Fernando Ferrer dropped out of the mayor's race, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has been aggressively courting Hispanic voters with a series of news conferences on the steps of City Hall featuring endorsements from prominent Hispanic New Yorkers, preferably Democrats.
Yesterday, the campaign held yet another event that aides said would highlight an endorsement from a "Latino Democrat," but in this case, the guest of honor was not quite as Hispanic as was his billing. 
 Assemblyman Vito J. Lopez presides over a sprawling social-services and political empire in Bushwick, Brooklyn, one of the most Hispanic neighborhoods in the city, but his own heritage is principally Italian. His surname derives from a paternal grandfather born outside Barcelona, and as he has acknowledged, he speaks limited Spanish.
That did not stop Mr. Lopez, a Democrat, from crossing party lines for the first time and praising Mr. Giuliani's efforts on behalf of Hispanic residents and immigrants. Surrounded by cheering neighborhood residents, many of whom were chanting in Spanish, he said the Mayor was the only candidate in the race who does not need a road map to Hispanic neighborhoods.
Pressed by several Spanish-speaking reporters as to his ethnic qualifications, he said he considered himself Hispanic.
"I'm proud that my grandfather was born in Spain, that he went to Italy and married my grandma," Mr. Lopez said. "I'm proud of my name, Vito Lopez, and maybe I'm not pure this or pure that, but I'm proud of who I am."
 
A Remark Stirs Criticism
 Mr. Giuliani has not been nearly as active in seeking endorsements from black officials, and his testy relationship with black New Yorkers became an issue this week after an offhand remark he made to reporters from The Washington Post in an article about New York City.
Asked by The Post to respond to critics who say he is not doing enough for minorities, the Mayor said: "They are alive. How about we start with that. You can't help people more directly than to save lives."
He was apparently referring to the decline in the murder rate, which disproportionately affects minority neighborhoods.
But the infelicitous phrasing of the remark produced an instant reaction from his political opponents and other critics, who monitor his every utterance. Former Mayor David N. Dinkins said the remark demonstrated Mr. Giuliani's insensitivity to minority concerns, and others agreed.
"I thought it demonstrated his lack of sensitivity to the problems that he's inflicted on the minority community, like overcrowded schools and high unemployment," said Sal F. Albanese, a Brooklyn Councilman who is running for the Democratic mayoral nomination.
The Mayor said that observation represented a "warped philosophy." The crime drop in minority neighborhoods has not only improved residents' lives, he said, but it has also improved their economies.
"That's what I was talking about, and that's what I delivered," Mr. Giuliani said. "And I think the old-fashioned incendiary politicians of this city will try to turn that around. It ain't going to work."
 
Waging a Green War
 Mark J. Green, the Public Advocate, had been hoping for a smooth ride to re-election -- the better to position himself to run for the United States Senate next year. But Mr. Green, who learned two weeks ago that he would face a Republican opponent, has a new problem: a primary opponent with the same last name.
Assemblyman Roger L. Green of Brooklyn says he will almost certainly circulate petitions to run against Mark Green in the Democratic primary, posing the possibility that Democrats will have a choice of Green versus Green.
Roger Green's bid is arguably quixotic: he is entering the race too late to qualify for public campaign financing, and Mark Green has sewed up almost all the organized Democratic support, which counts in a primary. But Roger Green still has the potential to cause Mark Green more than a few problems.
The Assemblyman said he is entering the race because all three Democrats running for citywide office this year are white: Mark Green, City Comptroller Alan G. Hevesi and Borough President Ruth W. Messinger of Manhattan, who is considered the candidate most likely to win the Democratic mayoral nomination. "The slate is not inclusive, and doesn't reflect the tradition of coalition politics in the Democratic Party," Roger Green said.
He already has pledges of support from other prominent black Democrats who share the same concern. Assemblyman Gregory M. Meeks of Queens, head of the Council of Elected Black Democratic Officials, said he will certainly support Mr. Green -- Roger Green -- if he runs. "We need a party of inclusion, and the party right now is not including African-Americans," Mr. Meeks said.
Mark Green's advisers said they welcomed Roger Green's entry into the race -- and professed not to be concerned about any confusion voters might display at a choice of Green versus Green. "It's not too complicated at all," said Joe DePlasco, who is Mark Green's spokesman. "One is spelled G-R-E-E-N. And the other is spelled G-R-E-E-N."

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL 3-6
 
Far Right May Decide Winner of French Vote
As France prepares to vote on Sunday, swing voters who support the far-right National Front are emerging as a force that could determine who governs France for the next five years. There are signs that the party's supporters, in their distaste for the establishment, may hand victory to the opposition Socialists. The party, which won 14.9 percent in the first round of voting, is in a unique position to influence the outcome, and its leader has expressed no enthusiasm for the governing center-right coalition, led by President Chirac. 1
 
Peru Congress Chastised
Peru's Congress came under attack from Western diplomats, human rights groups and business and political leaders for its decision to oust three members of a constitutional court who ruled that President Fujimori could not seek a third consecutive term. The United States called the dismissals "a step backwards in the consolidation of democracy in Peru," which is now emerging from years of guerrilla warfare and military dictatorship. 4
 
Pope Goes Home to Challenges
Pope John Paul arrives in Poland on Saturday for an 11-day trip that may be his valedictory pilgrimage to his homeland. The 77-year-old Pope will find a church where a conservative faction appears to have the upper hand, and a political scene excited by parliamentary elections in September between the former Communists and a regrouped Solidarity, which is now in opposition. 5

Beheading Alarms Japan
Japan is riveted by the beheading of an 11-year-old boy. The case has caused alarm and soul-searching in a nation that, while generally safe, has witnessed some spectacular killings in the last few years. The latest is perhaps the grisliest of all. 3
 
Uneasy Indonesian Victory
With late vote counting, the Indonesian Government party consolidated what appeared to be a record margin of victory in elections. Still, important questions were raised, about the course of recent riots, the credibility of President Suharto, and whether the leadership will take steps to address sources of discontent, including corruption, economic disparities and overbearing Government institutions. 3
 
Evacuation in Sierra Leone
United States marines landed in the capital of Sierra Leone and evacuated hundreds of foreigners fleeing from looting and violence that followed an armed coup. The marines began the operation before dawn, and over the day removed some 900 people, including 330 Americans. 4
 
New Envoy to Russia
President Clinton nominated James Collins, a senior State Department adviser, as ambassador to Russia. Mr. Collins is special adviser to Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright. (Reuters)
 
NATIONAL 7-9
 
Pentagon Is Deploying New Type of Nuclear Bomb
The United States is deploying a new type of nuclear bomb, one that burrows to destroy underground factories or laboratories while causing relatively little surface damage. It is the first time since the end of the cold war that the Pentagon has put a nuclear weapon with new capabilities into the field. Military officials said the weapon, called the B-61, was less dangerous because its effects were more concentrated. Critics say it expands the nation's threat against rogue states suspected of building nuclear and chemical weapons. 1
 
Bombing Jury Deliberates
The Federal jury in the Oklahoma City bombing trial completed its first day of deliberations in the murder and conspiracy case of Timothy J. McVeigh. The judge in the case warned jurors, who are being sequestered, not to be swayed by sympathy for the victims or their families. 7

Texas Tax Revision Loses
The Texas Legislature has defeated Gov. George W. Bush's proposal to revamp the state's tax code. The plan was intended to provide more equitable financing of schools by shifting the burden from property taxes to sales and other taxes. The plan would have cut taxes over all, but opponents focused on its 0.5 percent increase in the sales tax. The defeat was the first legislative setback for the popular Governor. 7

Tobacco Talks Get Tougher
With attorneys general in several states resisting tobacco company demands for limited immunity from lawsuits, the cigarette makers are asking the opposing negotiators to set a final position in settlement talks over tobacco litigation. 8

Death Penalty Not Sought
Prosecutors have decided not to seek the death penalty for the teen-ager charged with murdering the son of Bill Cosby. No reason was given for the decision, but Mr. Cosby, in a statement, did not indicate that he had any objections to it. 8

NEW YORK/REGION 21-25
 
Inspiration of Megan's Law Convicted of Girl's Murder
A New Jersey jury convicted Jesse K. Timmendequas of raping and murdering 7-year-old Megan Kanka. Her death almost three years ago led the state and others to pass laws requiring the registration of convicted sexual predators, like Mr. Timmendequas. The jury will hear arguments beginning on June 9 on whether Mr. Timmendequas should be executed. 1

Order on Special Education
Federal officials ordered the city to reduce the number of minority students in special-education programs. The Government said there was a disproportionate number of black and Hispanic students in such classes. The city schools could face the loss of tens of millions of dollars of Federal aid. 1

Legal Battle Over Hostos
An appellate court judge overturned a ruling by a state judge in the Bronx that would have allowed 125 students at Hostos Community College to graduate on Sunday even though they had not passed the City University writing examination. 23

Students Indicted in Rape
Four high school seniors were indicted on charges of raping a classmate in a Queens school last month. District Attorney Richard A. Brown attacked school officials for their one-month delay in reporting the accusations and he joined Mayor Giuliani in calling for the Police Department to take over the training and supervision of security guards in the city's schools. 24

Welfare Rules Moderated
Connecticut legislators agreed to relax a series of work requirements for welfare recipients, to extend Medicaid benefits to teen-age children of the working poor and to offer Medicaid and family welfare benefits to legal immigrants who arrived here before the Federal welfare law was passed last August. But the lawmakers also voted to end welfare for single adults who are judged to be employable and to provide no transitional cash benefits to family welfare recipients who are cut off after 21 months because they have found work that pays more than their monthly grant. 22

BUSINESS DAY 32-45
 
Used Car Prices Are Falling
Used car prices fell 2 percent over the last 12 months, after rising for four years. The biggest reason may be that the growing popularity of used cars, due to greater confidence in them, has simply cooled off. But the rising number of cars coming off lease and the growing availability of used sport utility vehicles, mini-vans and other vehicles previously in short supply may have contributed. 1

Intel's Share Price Plunges
The value of Intel stock dropped 7.5 percent after the company announced that second-quarter profits would be lower than expected, largely because of weak demand for its Pentium processor chips. 33

HEADLINE: Peru's Cut-Rate Fighter Jets Were Too Good to Be True

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: LIMA, Peru

 BODY:
Call it a case of bargain-hunter's remorse.
Last year, Peru's generals scoured the discount market and found the perfect deal to turn the neighbors green with envy: not one, not two, but a dozen top-rated jet fighters for just $350 million. 
 Now they've begun taking delivery, but there's a problem. The planes may never be ready for combat, because it turns out that they come without a warranty or service contract.
The planes are Soviet-era MIG-29's, by far the most modern in Latin America, where the United States has long tried to forestall a high-tech arms race. They were bought not from Russia, where they were built, but from Belarus, which these days has to rely on the Russians to service its own air force. And the Russians, who would have liked to make the sale themselves, are simply refusing to service merchandise bought from a competitor.
The Peruvian generals have been deeply embarrassed by the comic-opera aspect of the affair, but in some ways it isn't funny at all.
Peru is a desperately underfinanced country where poverty is widespread, and the blunder highlights how its powerful military can override pressing domestic needs, even as it risks setting off an arms race that could heighten tensions and strain the budgets of Peru's equally impoverished neighbors.
For just those reasons, the United States has long refused to sell high-tech warplanes in Latin America -- although it is now reconsidering that stand, given the entry of former Soviet arms dealers into the competition.
So it was a surprise in Latin America when diplomats and military sources revealed how Peru, having engaged Belarus as an arms supplier, came to find that Russia, its big competitor next door, was doing its utmost to sour the deal.
Last week, Russia confirmed that it would not cooperate with servicing the aircraft, saying that was up to Belarus.
But military and diplomatic sources say that Belarus is in no position to provide service, and without technical support, upgrades and a reliable supply of spare parts from the manufacturer, the new squadron is unlikely to ever perform the complex combat missions for which it was designed.
"Buying those MIG-29's from Belarus was like buying a refrigerator at a rummage sale and then realizing after you get it home that not only is the warranty nontransferable but you can't even get spare parts," said a local military expert, who has close dealings with Peru's Air Force.
Peruvian government and military officials declined to comment on the MIG-29's, but they are said to be furious with Belarus for not securing a service commitment from the Russians before selling the aircraft.
Military analysts said that Belarus did not approach the Russians about maintaining the MIG-29's until after Peru had already received four of the combat planes. Belarus has reportedly halted shipments of the MIG-29's to Peru, which is seeking to cancel the deal unless Belarus persuades the Russians to provide technical support.
Embarrassment is not confined to Peru's generals. Critics of President Alberto K. Fujimori, who bought the planes as a reward to the military for successes in its war against terrorism, have accused him of squandering limited resources that could have been used to battle the extreme poverty that gave rise to the terrorist insurgency in the first place.
Mr. Fujimori's detractors point out that even at bargain-basement prices, the fighter planes cost Peru hundreds of millions of dollars when more than half of its 24 million people live below the poverty line, and 70 percent are unemployed or do not earn enough to buy basic necessities.
"There is no justification for this type of uncontrolled military spending," said a high-ranking Western diplomat based here. "Can you imagine how many schools, hospitals, roads and lunches $350 million would have paid for?"
Peru's turn to the former Soviet lands for high-tech weaponry has already become an arguing point for American business advocates who want the United States to end its ban on such sales in Latin America; they say that foreign competitors have been moving in on a potentially lucrative market. The Clinton Administration is considering lifting the ban as the region moves from military dictatorship to democracy, and it recently allowed a United States contractor to submit technical bids to supply Chile with F-16 aircraft.
American contractors argue that allowing them to sell advanced weapons to Latin America would give the United States leverage over what the arms are used for, since its suppliers would control maintenance, the industry officials said.
"The Peruvians have bought Soviet equipment in the past, so you'd think they would have learned their lesson that the Russians have a dismal track record when it comes to support," said Joel Johnson, vice president for the Aerospace Industries Association.
"If Peru only plans to use these airplanes for parades on national day, then they don't have a problem," Mr. Johnson added, "but if they plan to take them into battle, they will have to get some long-term maintenance."
But Sarah Walking, an analyst for the Arms Control Association, said that Peru's troubled MIG purchase shows that the United States has not been replaced as the preferred supplier of advanced weapons in Latin America, and that therefore the arms embargo should remain. Peru's difficulties "will make these countries think twice before rushing off to buy these weapons from suppliers who cannot guarantee their merchandise," Ms. Walking said.
Although Peru's MIG-29's appear to be grounded for the moment, United States analysts who assess the balance of power in the region said other Latin militaries are expected to seek comparable fighter planes to match Peru's presumed advantage. Both Chile, which has sparred with Peru in the past, and Brazil have expressed interest in buying United States fighter planes like the F-16, which is made by the Lockheed Martin Corporation.
When Peru announced the purchase of the MIG-29's six months ago, its neighbor, Ecuador, was outraged because Peru bought the combat planes just as the two countries began talks to resolve a border dispute that led to a brief war in the Amazon jungle two years ago. The Russian MIG-29 fighter performs air interception and air-to-ground missions that are not very useful in the kind of jungle wars that Peru has fought, military analysts say.

HEADLINE: Russia Aide Cuts Ties to Harvard Center That Ousted Advisers

BYLINE: By ALESSANDRA STANLEY 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, May 30

 BODY:
The architect of Russia's economic reform program, Deputy Prime Minister Anatoly B. Chubais, said today that he would stop working with the Harvard institute that had lent him its brightest economists to assist Russia's rocky transition to a market system.
The decision followed the dismissal of two consultants from the prestigious Harvard Institute for International Development. American officials accused the two men of misusing their positions for personal gain. Mr. Chubais had worked with the two men for years. 
 While Mr. Chubais was breaking ties with Harvard, he spoke highly of the two consultants. One of his allies said Russia intended to hire at least one of the consultants as an adviser, regardless of the accusations.
The two consultants were accused this month by the United States Agency for International Development, which financed the Harvard consulting programs, and Harvard then dismissed them.
But Mr. Chubais, who described the two advisers' work as "fantastically important," seemed to suggest that they -- and he -- were victims of a wider conspiracy.
"The investigation which was made upon the Harvard researchers had political roots," he said after a news conference today. "I guess those roots were produced by those in the United States who do not support the idea of Russia's radical reform. This is why I decided it is not convenient for me to do business with that kind of institute."
It was not immediately clear why Mr. Chubais referred to American opponents of economic reform, when most of the criticism directed at him and his policies is centered in Russia.
Mr. Chubais was probably choosing a popular scapegoat, Western interference, to protect the consultants and himself from further scrutiny at home from his Russian critics, of which he has many.
The office of the Agency for International Development in Washington confirmed today that Mr. Chubais worked for six weeks as a consultant for one of the A.I.D.-financed programs while he was out of government before the 1996 presidential elections. The agency was unable to provide details about Mr. Chubais's services or earnings.
There is nothing illegal or inappropriate in his earning money as a private consultant to a United States Government agency, but Mr. Chubais may find the relationship difficult to explain in Moscow's highly charged political atmosphere.
The Russian press has already raised questions about Mr. Chubais's sources of income when he was out of office, making charges of impropriety which he has rigorously denied. Questions have also been raised about the accounting practises of The Russian Privatization Center, which provided Mr. Chubais with expertise and several key advisers.
Mr. Chubais's warm words of support for the ousted American consultants and his sharp criticism of A.I.D. for what he described as the agency's unfair haste in accusing them of impropriety were clearly aimed at a domestic audience.
But his harsh words were a severe blow to Harvard, which had hoped to replace the consultants and continue its programs. It was also a slap at the United States Government, which has spent $43 million in funding for Harvard institute programs over the last several years.
Mr. Chubais's assessment was sharply contradicted in Washington. "As I see it, this is not related to Russian politics or American politics," said Richard Morningstar, a senior State Department official who is the coordinator for American assistance to the newly independent states. "This only involves the actions of two individuals."
The two individuals, Jonathan Hays, the program director of the Harvard institute in Moscow, and Harvard economics professor Andrei Shleifer, the project director, had been deeply involved in advising the Russian Government on privatization, securities markets and tax policy for several years.
Alerted to allegations of financial misconduct by the Moscow office of A.I.D., the agency's inspector general opened an investigation, and in preliminary findings, concluded that both men had violated conflict-of-interest rules. Mr. Hays was found to have invested in Russian government bonds. Professor Shleifer's wife, Nancy Zimmerman, who owned a private investment concern, received investment advice and support from her husband's office.
Both men denied any impropriety, and issued a statement saying the agency was "wrong in its conclusions, wrong in its assumptions, inaccurate in its facts and foolish in its rhetoric." Neither man could be reached today for comment.
A spokesman for one of Mr. Chubais's closest allies, Dmitry Vasilyev, chairman of the Federal Securities Commission, said the commission intended to hire Mr. Hays, as a consultant to the commission, which is the Russian stock market regulatory organization.

CORRECTION-DATE: June 2, 1997, Monday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Saturday about two consultants to the Russian Government from the Harvard Institute for International Development, who were dismissed by Harvard, misstated the surname of one of the consultants. He is Jonathan Hay, not Hays.
